Obligations of Ghusl: Mouth and Nose – Hanafi Position
Hanafi proof for fard of mouth and nose in ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on madmadah and istinshaq in ghusl, explain the rational proof for their obligation, and contrast it with the Shafi'i view.

Core Idea
The mouth and nose are borderline organs – ghusl requires complete purification, so they must be washed.

This passage addresses a key dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the obligations of ghusl. The Hanafi school argues that rinsing the mouth and nose are fard, not merely sunnah, based on the intensive command for ghusl and the dual nature of these parts. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly performing ghusl according to the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Determine what the Hanafi school rules for madmadah and istinshaq in ghusl, and how the Shafi'i school differs.
The issue is whether these acts are fard or sunnah.
2
Examine the Hanafi proof
Analyze the three categories of body parts (purely internal, purely external, mixed) and the intensive form of the command for ghusl.
The mixed nature of mouth and nose together with the intensive command (al-ghusl) requires washing them.
3
Understand the Shafi'i position
Recognize that Shafi'i analogizes ghusl to wudu, where these acts are sunnah, and lacks a specific text making them fard.
This is an example of qiyas.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
See how the Hanafi school invalidates the analogy by pointing out the difference in command intensity (mubalaghah vs. plain command).
The intensive form in ghusl implies complete purification, which wudu does not.
5
Note the practical consequence
Understand the thamarah al-khilaf: missing these acts in ghusl invalidates it for Hanafis but not for Shafi'is.
This directly affects the validity of ghusl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
باب الغسل
وفرضُ الغُسل : المَضْمَضَةُ والاستنشاق
(وفرضُ الغُسل :
المَضْمَضَةُ والاسْتِنْشَاق)، وهما سنتان" عند الشَّافعي . ولنا : " أنَّ الفم داخل من وجه خارج من وجه حساً عند انطباق الفم
وانفتاحه
[۳]
[١] قوله : وهما سنتان ؛ أشار بهذا إلى دفعِ ما يَرِدُ من أنه لو قال المصنف : وفرض الغسل غسل جميع البدن لكان أخصر بأن فرضيّة المضمضة والاستنشاق لما كان مختلفاً فيهما احتاج إلى إفرادهما بالذكر، وإلى أنَّ المراد بالفرض في المتن ليس ما هو القطع ، ويكفر جاحده، فإنّ فرضية المضمضة والاستنشاق مما وقع فيه اختلاف المجتهدين، بل المراد ما يعم منه ويشمل الفرض العملي.
[٢] قوله : ولنا ؛ استدلال على افتراضهما بالقرآن بحيث يتضح به الفرق بين الوضوء والغسل، ويبطل قياس الشافعي (١) الله الغُسل على الوضوء، وتوضيحه : : أن
أعضاء البدن على ثلاثة أقسام :
١. داخل من كل وجه.
٢. وخارج من كل وجه.
. وداخل من وجه خارج من وجه.
(۱)
أما القسم الأوّل فلا يفترضُ غسله لا في الوضوء ولا في الغُسل.
والقسم الثاني : يفترض في الغُسل غسلُ كله، وفي الوضوء غسل ثلاثة منه
ومسح الرابع. وأما الثالث فلكونه ذا الشبهين، افترض غسلهما في الغُسل دون الوضوء، ولم يعكس الأمر ؛ لأنّ الوارد في باب الغُسل صيغة المبالغة، فيفيد أنَّ المفروض في الغُسلِ التطهير الكامل، وهو بأن يغسل ما هو داخل من وجه، خارج من وجه أيضاً، ولا كذلك في الوضوء، فإنّ الوارد فيه مجرَّدُ الأمر بالغسل.
[٣] قوله : عند انطباق الفم وانفتاحه ؛ يعني إذا فتح الفم يحس كون داخله : وهو محلّ المضمضة خارجاً ، وإذا انطبق يحكم حسّاً بكونه داخلاً.
(1) ينظر: «مغني المحتاج» (۱ : ۷۳)، وغيره.
Chapter of Ghusl. And the fard of ghusl: rinsing the mouth and rinsing the nose. (And the fard of ghusl: rinsing the mouth and rinsing the nose), and they are sunnah according to al-Shafi'i. And our proof: "The mouth is inside in one respect and outside in one respect, sensorially, when the mouth is closed and opened." [3] [1] His statement: "And they are sunnah" — he indicated by this to refute what may be objected, that if the author had said: "The fard of ghusl is washing the entire body" it would be more concise, but since the obligation of rinsing the mouth and nose is disputed, he needed to mention them separately, and to indicate that the meaning of 'fard' in the text is not that which is definitive and whose denier becomes a disbeliever, for the obligation of rinsing the mouth and nose is among matters where mujtahids differed, rather the meaning is that which is general and includes practical obligation. [2] His statement: "And our proof" — a proof for their obligation by the Quran, by which the difference between wudu and ghusl becomes clear, and which invalidates al-Shafi'i's analogy of ghusl to wudu. Its clarification: The body parts are of three types: 1. Inside in every respect. 2. Outside in every respect. 3. Inside in one respect and outside in another. As for the first type, washing it is not obligatory in either wudu or ghusl. The second type: in ghusl, washing all of it is obligatory, and in wudu, washing three of them and wiping the fourth. As for the third type, because it has two aspects, washing them is obligatory in ghusl but not in wudu, and the opposite is not done, because what has come in the chapter of ghusl is an intensive form, indicating that what is obligatory in ghusl is complete purification, which is to wash what is inside in one respect and outside in another as well, and such is not the case in wudu, for what has come in it is merely the command to wash. [3] His statement: "when the mouth is closed and opened" — meaning, when one opens the mouth, he sensorially perceives its inside, which is the place of rinsing, as outside, and when it is closed, he sensorially judges it to be inside.
Source pages p. 341

Commentary
This passage introduces the chapter on ghusl and establishes that rinsing the mouth (madmadah) and rinsing the nose (istinshaq) are obligatory (fard) in ghusl, contrary to al-Shafi'i who considers them sunnah. The author explains why the author of the matn mentioned these two specifically: because their obligation is disputed, and because 'fard' here means a practical obligation, not the absolute fard whose denier becomes a disbeliever. The Hanafi proof is a rational argument: the mouth and nose are simultaneously inside and outside. The body parts are three categories: purely internal (no washing required), purely external (must be washed), and mixed. The mouth and nose belong to the mixed category. In ghusl, because the command uses an intensive form (al-ghusl, the washing), it implies complete purification, so these mixed parts must be washed. In wudu, the command is plain (amsahu bi ru'usikum), so only the external parts are washed. This disproves Shafi'i's analogy of ghusl to wudu. The text also notes that when the mouth is open, the inside feels like outside, and when closed, it feels inside, indicating it is a borderline area.

Key Terms
المضمضة (al-madmadah): Rinsing the mouth with water
الاستنشاق (al-istinshaq): Inhaling water into the nose and then expelling it
فرض (fard): In this context, it means a practical obligation that is subject to ijtihad, not the definitive fard that necessitates disbelief upon denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the status of rinsing the mouth (madmadah) in ghusl?
A) It is sunnah
B) It is fard
C) It is makruh
D) It is mustahabb

Key Takeaway
In ghusl, rinsing the mouth and nose are fard according to the Hanafi school due to the intensive command and the dual nature of these parts.

Obstructions in Ghusl: The Obligation to Deliver Water to Every Part
Commentary on washing entire body with examples of obstructions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which obstructions invalidate ghusl and which are excused, applying the Hanafi principle that water must reach every part of the external body.

Core Idea
Dough under the nail invalidates ghusl; dirt and oil are excused — the key is whether water can penetrate or removal causes hardship.

Ghusl requires water to reach every part of the external body. But what happens if something blocks the water? This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains that not all obstructions are equal: dry dough must be removed, but dirt, mud, and oil are excused under certain conditions. Understanding this distinction is key to performing a valid ghusl.

Guided Path
1
Identify the obligation
Recognize that washing sā'ir al-badan (the entire external body) is the rukn of ghusl. The commentator clarifies that 'sā'ir al-badan' means all external parts, excluding internal cavities.
The hadith 'under every hair is janabah' establishes the requirement.
2
Categorize obstructions
Separate obstructions into three groups: (1) removable and water-blocking (dry dough, dye that forms a layer, henna that waterproofs) → must be removed; (2) penetrable (dirt, mud) → excused; (3) hardship-causing (oil) → excused due to haraj.
The commentator contrasts dough (external object) with dirt (body-generated) to illustrate the principle.
3
Apply the hadith
The hadith 'Whoever leaves a spot of a hair...' is the basis. It implies water must reach every part; if an obstruction prevents that, ghusl is invalid unless an exception applies.
The hadith is ahad and zanni, but accepted as proof for obligation.
4
Note the Hanafi concession for oil
Even if water does not reach the skin due to oil, ghusl is valid because requiring removal would cause hardship (haraj). This is an istihsan exception.
Other schools may require removal; this is a point of ikhtilaf.
5
Summarize the ruling
The core ruling: ghusl is valid only if water reaches every external part. Easily removable obstructions must be removed; those difficult to remove or naturally penetrable are excused.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغسل سائر البدن) : أي جميع ظاهر البدن ، حتى لو بقي العجينُ في الظُّفْرِ فاغتسل لا يجزئ " ، وفي الدَّرن (۳) يجزئ ؛ إذ هو متولد من هنالك ، وكذا الطين ؛ لأن الماء ينفذ فيه [١] قوله : في ابتلاع ؛ فإنّ الصائم لا يفسد صومه بابتلاع الريق : أي إدخاله في حلقه من فمه، وهذا آية كونه داخلاً، فإنّه لو كان خارجاً يفسد صومه ؛ لأنَّ دخول شي من خارج إلى داخل مفسد له، وإذا دخل شيء من خارج إلى فمه ولم يصل إلى حلقه لا يفسد صومه أيضاً، وهذا آية كونه خارجاً ، فإنّه لو كان داخلاً لفسد صومه ؛ لوجود الدخول من الخارج إلى الداخل. [٢] قوله : وكذلك الأنف ؛ فإنّه داخل حسّاً حيث لا يرى ظاهراً، وخارج حساً من حيث أنه يرى بعد الإمعان وداخل حكماً فإنه لو دخل مخاط الصائم من الأنف إلى الحلق لا يفسد صومه، وخارج حكماً باعتبار أنه لو دخل شيء من خارج في الأنف لم يفسد صومه. [٣]قوله : لا يجزئ ؛ معروف من الإجزاء بمعنى الكفاية، يجئ لازماً ومتعدياً، أو من الجزاء بالفتح، والمعنى واحد، وفي بعض النسخ: لا يجزئ به، فهو مجهول من الإجزاء المتعدي. والأصل في هذا الباب حديث : من ترك موضع شعرة من جسده في جنابة لم يغسلها فعل به كذا وكذا من النار)) ، أخرجه أبو داود وابن ماجة والدارمي وابن جرير وغيرهم، وفي رواية الترمذي: «تحت كل شعرة جنابة، فاغسلوا الشعر وأنقــوا البشرة). فعُلِمَ من هذا أنه يفترض إيصال الماء إلى كلِّ جزء من الأعضاء في الغُسل، فإذا بقى العجين اليابس في الظفر ولم يصل الماءَ تحته لم يصح غسله، وإن بقي الطين فيه لا بأس به ؛ لأنَّ الماء ينفذ فيه فلا ضرورة إلى إزالته ولو بقى الوسخ يكفي ؛ لأنه متولد من ذلك الموضع بخلاف العجين، فإنّه عارض يمكن إزالته. وكذا الصبغ والحِنَّاء ، فالحاصل أنَّ المعتبر في هذا الحر هو وصول الماء. [ ] قوله : الصبغ ؛ بفتح الصاد المهملة وكسرها ، والحناء بكسر الحاء المهملة وتشديد النون، وبالمد ورق معروف يصبغ به الأيدي ولونه أحمر. [٢] قوله : وإذا ادهن ؛ بتشديد الدال المهملة من الادّهان : أي استعمل الدهن في شعر الرأس أو اللحية فأمر – بتشديد الراء المهملة من الإمرار – أي أسال إليه الماء، فلم يصل ؛ أي الماء إلى العضو وفي بعض النسخ : فلم يقبل - أي العضو – يجزئ ؛ أي يكفي من دون ضرورة إزالة الدهنِ وغسل الرأس بالخطمي وغيره لما فيه من الحرج
(And washing the rest of the body) meaning all of the external body, such that if dough remains under the nail and he performs ghusl, it is not sufficient. But if dirt remains, it is sufficient because it is generated from there, and similarly mud because water penetrates it. [1] His saying: concerning swallowing; the fasting person's fast is not invalidated by swallowing saliva, i.e., bringing it from his mouth into his throat. This indicates it is internal; if it were external it would break his fast because entry of something from outside to inside invalidates it. And if something from outside enters his mouth and does not reach his throat, it does not break his fast either. This indicates it is external; if it were internal his fast would break due to entry from outside to inside. [2] His saying: and similarly the nose; it is internally in terms of sense where it is not visible externally, and externally in terms of sense in that it is visible after scrutiny, and internally in terms of ruling in that if mucus of a fasting person goes from the nose to the throat it does not break his fast, and externally in terms of ruling in that if something enters the nose from outside it does not break his fast. [3] His saying: it is not sufficient; known from 'ijza' meaning sufficiency, used intransitively and transitively, or from 'jaza' with fatha, meaning the same. In some copies: 'la yujza' bihi' making it passive from transitive 'ijza'. The basis in this chapter is the hadith: 'Whoever leaves a spot of a hair on his body in janabah, not washing it, such and such will be done to him from the Fire.' Reported by Abu Dawood, Ibn Majah, al-Darimi, Ibn Jarir and others. In Tirmidhi's narration: 'Under every hair is janabah, so wash the hair and clean the skin.' So it is known from this that it is obligatory to deliver water to every part of the limbs in ghusl. So if dry dough remains under the nail and water does not reach beneath it, his ghusl is not valid. If mud remains on it, there is no harm because water penetrates it, so there is no need to remove it. If dirt remains, it suffices because it is generated from that place, unlike dough which is an accidental thing that can be removed. Similarly, dye and henna. So the conclusion is that what matters in this is the reaching of water. [ ] His saying: al-sabgh (dye) with fatih or kasr of the sad; al-hinna' with kasr of the ha' and tashdid of the nun, with alif mamdudah, is a known leaf used to dye hands, its color red. [2] His saying: 'wa idha iddahana' with tashdid of the dal from al-iddihan: i.e., he applied oil to the hair of the head or beard, then passed (with tashdid of the ra from al-imrar) i.e., flowed water to it, but it did not reach; i.e., water to the limb; in some copies: 'falam yaqbal' (it did not accept) - i.e., the limb – it suffices; i.e., sufficient without needing to remove the oil and wash the head with khittmi or other, due to difficulty.
Source pages p. 342

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling of washing the entire body (sā'ir al-badan) in ghusl. The commentator first clarifies that 'sā'ir al-badan' means all the external parts of the body. He then addresses the issue of obstructions: if dry dough remains under a fingernail and prevents water from reaching the skin, the ghusl is not valid. However, if dirt (wasakh) remains, it is excused because it originates from the body itself and water can penetrate it. Similarly, mud is excused because water can pass through it. The commentator then digresses to discuss the mouth and nose in the context of fasting (sawm), explaining that the mouth and nose are considered 'internal' for some rulings (like swallowing saliva not breaking the fast) but 'external' for others (like something entering from outside not nullifying the fast). This discussion is part of the broader topic of what constitutes the body's exterior in ghusl. The commentator then cites a hadith that emphasizes washing every hair and skin in janabah, establishing the obligation to ensure water reaches every part. Based on this, if dye (sabgh) or henna (hinna') forms a layer preventing water from reaching the skin, it must be removed, but if the water can penetrate through it, it is acceptable. Regarding oil (duhn), if a person applies oil to head or beard and then passes water over it, but the water does not reach the skin due to the oil, the ghusl is still valid without needing to remove the oil first, due to hardship (haraj). Thus, the core legal principle is that in ghusl, the obligation is to ensure water reaches every part of the external body, but obstacles that are difficult to remove (like oil) are excused, while easily removable obstacles (like dry dough) must be removed.

Key Terms
سائر البدن (sā'ir al-badan): All of the external body, including skin and hair, but not internal cavities.
الدَّرَن (al-daran): Dirt or grime that originates from the body itself.
العجين (al-'ajīn): Dough that is dry and stuck under the nail; an external obstacle that must be removed.
الصبغ (al-sabgh): Dye that colors the skin and forms a barrier if it prevents water penetration.
الحناء (al-hinna'): Henna; leaves used to dye hands, similar ruling to dye.
الادهان (al-iddihān): Applying oil to hair or beard; if water does not reach skin due to oil, ghusl still valid due to hardship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If dry dough remains under a person's fingernail during ghusl for janabah, and water does not reach the skin under the dough, what is the ruling?
A) The ghusl is valid because dough is not impure.
B) The ghusl is invalid because water must reach every part of the exterior body.
C) The ghusl is valid because fingernails are not part of the body.
D) The ghusl is invalid only if the dough was present before intention.

Key Takeaway
The key to valid ghusl is ensuring water reaches every external part; removable obstructions must be removed, but those that are penetrable or cause hardship are excused.

Obstructions to Water Reaching the Skin During Ghusl
Explains rulings on substances blocking water during Ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rulings on obstructions during Ghusl and distinguish between invalidating blockages and excused residues.

Core Idea
Water must reach every part; obstructions are excused only if water can penetrate or removal causes hardship.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah examines various substances and conditions that may prevent water from reaching the skin during Ghusl. The foundational proof is a hadith commanding washing the hair and cleansing the skin, establishing the obligation to ensure water reaches everywhere. The Hanafi school then applies this principle to specific cases, distinguishing between removable obstructions (like dough under the nail) and natural residues (like dirt), and making exceptions for hardship (like oil). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for determining the validity of Ghusl.

Guided Path
1
State the general obligation
From the hadith, identify that water must reach every hair and skin area. Determine the ruling level (fard) and the evidence (the imperative in the hadith).
The hadith is a single transmission (zanni thubut) but its meaning is clear (qati dalala) for obligation.
2
Examine removable obstructions
Look at the example of dry dough under the nail. Understand why it invalidates Ghusl – because it is a foreign substance that can be removed.
The ruling applies to any removable yet water-resistant substance.
3
Examine natural residues
Compare mud and dirt. Despite remaining under the nail, they do not invalidate Ghusl because water penetrates them (the substance is not a barrier).
The basis is whether the substance allows water to pass through to the skin.
4
Apply the hardship principle
Analyze the ruling on oil: even if water does not reach the skin under the oil, Ghusl is valid due to hardship (haraj) of complete removal.
This is an exception based on istihsan, prioritizing ease.
5
Identify specific body modifications
Review the rulings for earring holes, tight rings, and the prepuce. Determine what actions are required to ensure water reaches these areas.
Moving the ring/earring and retracting the prepuce are necessary if water cannot reach otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الطهارة/ الغسل
وكذا الصبغ والحِنَّاء ، فالحاصل أنَّ المعتبر في هذا الحَرَ
وإذا ادهن فأمر الماء عليه فلم يصل يجزئ. يغسلها فعل به كذا وكذا من النار)) ، أخرجه أبو داود وابن ماجة والدارمي وابن جرير وغيرهم، وفي رواية الترمذي: «تحت كل شعرة جنابة، فاغسلوا الشعر وأنقــوا البشرة).
فعُلِمَ من هذا أنه يفترض إيصال الماء إلى كلِّ جزء من الأعضاء في الغُسل، فإذا بقى العجين اليابس في الظفر ولم يصل الماءَ تحته لم يصح غسله، وإن بقي الطين فيه لا بأس به ؛ لأنَّ الماء ينفذ فيه فلا ضرورة إلى إزالته ولو بقى الوسخ يكفي ؛ لأنه متولد من ذلك الموضع بخلاف العجين، فإنّه عارض يمكن إزالته.
[1] قوله : الصبغ ؛ بفتح الصاد المهملة وكسرها ، والحناء بكسر الحاء المهملة وتشديد النون، وبالمد ورق معروف يصبغ به الأيدي ولونه أحمر.
[2] قوله : وإذا ادهن ؛ بتشديد الدال المهملة من الادّهان : أي استعمل الدهن في شعر الرأس أو اللحية فأمر – بتشديد الراء المهملة من الإمرار – أي أسال إليه الماء، فلم يصل ؛ أي الماء إلى العضو وفي بعض النسخ : فلم يقبل - أي العضو – يجزئ ؛ أي يكفي من دون ضرورة إزالة الدهنِ وغسل الرأس بالخطمي وغيره لما فيه من الحرج.
وأمَّا ثُقْبُ القُرْط : فإن كان القُرْطُ فيها ، فإن غلب على ظنه أنَّ الماء لا يصل من غير تحريك، فلا بد منه. فإن لم يكن القُرْطُ فيها ، فإن غلب على ظنهِ أَنَّ الماء يصل من غير تكلف لا يتكلف ، وإن غلب على ظنّه أَنَّهُ لا يصلُ إلا بتكلف يتكلف. وإن انضمَّ الثَّقْبُ بعد نزعِه ، وصار بحال إن أمر الماء عليها يدخلها، وإن غفل لا يدخلها أمر الماء، ولا يتكلّفُ في إدخال شيء سوى الماء من خشب أو نحوه.
وإن كان في أُصْبَعِهِ خاتم ضيق يجب تحريكه ؛ ليصل الماء تحته.
ويجب على الأَقْلَف إدخالُ الماءِ داخلَ القُلْفَة، وإن نزل البولُ إليها، ولم يخرج عنها نقض الوضوء، هذا عند بعض المشايخ، فلها حكم الظاهرِ من كل وجه .
[1] قوله : وأما ثقب ، بالضم والضمتين جمع ثقبة بالضم ، هي التي يدخل فيها القُرط وغيره ، ويقال له بالفارسية : سوراح، والقرط بالضم: حلية تعلّق في الأذن أو في شحمته للتزيين ، وهو جائز للنّساء، كما قال في «جامع أحكام الصغار»: «لا بأس بثقب أذن الطفل من البنات ؛ لأنهم كانوا يفعلون ذلك في زمان النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم من غير إنكار». انتهى. ومكروه للرجال كما صرح به في «الحاوي القدسي»، وكذا يجوز ثقب الأنف للبنات ؛ لتعليق الخزام، صرح به الطحطاوي في «حواشي الدر المختار».
[2] قوله : وإن كان في أصبعه ؛ بكسر الهمزة ، وسكون الصاد : إصبعه؛ أي إصبع المغتسل ، خاتم بكسر التاء وفتحها بالفارسية : انگشتري ضيق : صفة للخاتم ؛ أي يكون بحيث لو لم يحرك لم يصل الماء تحته.
[3] قوله : هذا عند بعض المشايخ ؛ الحاصل أنا عند بعض لها حكم ظاهر من كل وجه.
And similarly, dye and henna. So the conclusion is that what matters in this difficulty... And if he applied oil and then passed water over it but it did not reach, it is sufficient. [Hadith] 'Under every hair is janabah, so wash the hair and cleanse the skin.' So it is understood from this that it is obligatory to make water reach every part of the limbs in Ghusl. If dry dough remains under the nail and water does not reach under it, his Ghusl is not valid. If mud remains under it, there is no harm because water penetrates it, so there is no need to remove it. If dirt remains, it is sufficient because it is generated from that place, unlike dough which is an incidental thing that can be removed. [Commentary] Regarding the earlobe hole: if the earring is in it, and he predominantly thinks that water will not reach without moving, then it is necessary. If the earring is not in it, and he predominantly thinks water will reach without extra effort, he does not make extra effort. If he predominantly thinks it will not reach except with extra effort, he makes extra effort. If the hole closes after removal and becomes such that if he pours water on it, it enters, but if he neglects it does not, then he pours water and does not need to insert anything besides water such as wood or the like. If there is a tight ring on his finger, he must move it so that water reaches under it. It is obligatory for the uncircumcised man to make water enter under the prepuce. If urine enters it and does not come out, it breaks wudu; this is according to some mashayikh, so it has the ruling of the apparent part in every respect.
Source pages p. 343

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses rulings related to obstructions that may prevent water from reaching the skin during Ghusl. The central principle, established by the hadith 'Under every hair is janabah, so wash the hair and cleanse the skin,' is that it is obligatory (fard) to ensure water reaches every part of the body, including the hair and skin. The text then provides specific examples. First, regarding external substances: dry dough under the nail invalidates Ghusl because it can be removed, but mud or natural dirt does not because water penetrates it. Oil applied to hair or beard: if water passes over it and reaches the skin, it suffices even if the oil remains, due to hardship (haraj). Second, regarding the earlobe hole: if the earring is in place, the water must be made to reach underneath by moving it if necessary. If the earring is removed and the hole remains open, water will enter without extra effort; if the hole closes partially, pouring water suffices without inserting objects. Third, a tight ring must be moved so water can reach under it. Fourth, the uncircumcised man must ensure water reaches under the prepuce (qulfah). If urine enters the prepuce and does not exit, it breaks wudu according to some later scholars, treating it as an external part. These rulings demonstrate the Hanafi school's nuanced approach: the default is obligation to ensure water reaches, but exceptions are made for substances that allow water penetration or where removal would cause hardship.

Key Terms
قُلْفَة (qulfah): The foreskin covering the glans of the penis, which must be retracted or water must reach under it during Ghusl for the uncircumcised man.
أَقْلَف (aqlaf): A man who has not been circumcised.
قُرْط (qurt): An earring worn by women in the earlobe for adornment.
ثَقْب (thaqb): The hole pierced in the earlobe for inserting an earring.
خاتم ضيق (khatim dayyiq): A tight ring that prevents water from reaching the skin underneath if not moved.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person has a tight ring on his finger during Ghusl, what must he do?
A) He can leave it as long as water passes over it.
B) He must remove the ring entirely.
C) He must move the ring so that water reaches under it.
D) He does not need to do anything special for the ring.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school requires water to reach every part of the body in Ghusl, but allows exceptions for natural residues and hardships; specific obstructions like rings, earrings, and the prepuce require active movement or retraction to ensure water penetration.

Washing Under Earrings and Tight Rings in Ghusl
Explains when to move earrings and rings for water to reach during ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi criterion of ghalabat al-zann to determine whether moving earrings or rings is required during ghusl.

Core Idea
The obligation to move jewelry in ghusl is determined by one's predominant certainty about water reaching the skin.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya elaborates on the details of washing areas covered by earrings and tight rings. The Hanafi school bases the ruling on the principle that water must reach every part of the skin during obligatory ghusl. However, when jewelry prevents direct access, the requirement to move it depends on whether one is predominantly certain that water reaches without moving. Understanding this nuance helps avoid overburden or neglect in purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
The text covers three cases: earring in place, earring removed (hole open), and hole closed. For each, apply ghalabat al-zann to determine necessity.
Also includes tight ring as a separate case.
2
Apply ghalabat al-zann for earring in place
If the earring is in and you predominantly think water won't reach without moving, moving is wajib. If you think it will reach, moving is not required.
Ghalabat al-zann is the standard, not certainty.
3
Assess when earring is removed
If the earring is out, judge based on ghalabat al-zann. If water likely reaches without effort, no effort; if only with effort, then effort is required.
Effort means moving or adjusting to allow water in.
4
Handle a closed hole
If the hole has closed but water can still enter if poured, pour water normally. Do not insert any object like wood to force water in.
Only water should be used; no foreign objects.
5
Rule for a tight ring
If a ring is tight on the finger, moving it is wajib so that water reaches underneath, regardless of ghalabat al-zann? The text says 'must move' implying it is obligatory.
This is a clear ruling: moving is wajib.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأمَّا تُقْبُ القُرْط : فإن كان القُرْطُ فيها ، فإن غلب على ظنه أنَّ الماء لا يصل من غير تحريك، فلا بد منه. فإن لم يكن القُرْطُ فيها ، فإن غلب على ظنهِ أَنَّ الماء يصل من غير تكلف لا يتكلف ، وإن غلب على ظنّه أَنَّهُ لا يصلُ إلا بتكلف يتكلف. وإن انضمَّ الثَّقْبُ بعد نزعِه ، وصار بحال إن أمر الماء عليها يدخلها، وإن غفل لا يدخلها أمر الماء، ولا يتكلّفُ في إدخال شيء سوى الماء من خشب أو نحوه. وإن كان في أُصْبَعِهِ خاتم ضيق يجب تحريكه ؛ ليصل الماء تحته.
As for the earring hole: if the earring is in it, then if he predominantly thinks that water will not reach without moving it, then moving is necessary. If the earring is not in it, then if he predominantly thinks that water reaches without effort, he does not make effort; if he predominantly thinks that it does not reach except with effort, he makes effort. And if the hole closes after removing it, and it becomes such that if he pours water on it, it enters, but if he neglects, water does not enter, then he pours water and does not make effort to insert anything other than water, like wood or the like. And if on his finger there is a tight ring, he must move it so that water reaches underneath it.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the ruling on washing areas covered by earrings and tight rings during ghusl. The Hanafi school bases the obligation on ghalabat al-zann (predominant certainty) regarding water reaching the skin. For an earring hole, if the earring is in place and water likely won't reach without moving it, moving the earring is wajib. If the earring is out, one assesses. If the hole has closed, one pours water but does not insert objects. For a tight ring, moving it is wajib to allow water underneath. This follows the Hanafi principle that in ghusl, water must reach all skin, and the measure is one's predominant certainty.

Key Terms
ثقب (thaqb): The hole in the earlobe for an earring.
قرط (qurt): Earring, a piece of jewelry worn in the ear.
غلبة الظن (ghalabat al-zann): Predominant certainty, the criterion for obligation in washing hard-to-reach areas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must always be moved to ensure water reaches.
B) It depends on the person's predominant certainty about water reaching.
C) It is only moved if the earring is made of gold.
D) Moving is never required.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, moving earrings and tight rings during ghusl is wajib only if one is predominantly certain that water cannot reach the skin otherwise.

Hanafi Dispute on Washing Under the Prepuce
Presents three intra-Hanafi views on the legal status of the prepuce.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on washing under the prepuce (qulfah) and explain Al-Shurunbulali's conditional ruling.

Core Idea
Whether the prepuce is treated as outer or inner skin determines the obligation to wash inside it in ghusl and the effect of urine on wudhu.

This lesson addresses an important intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal status of the prepuce (qulfah) of an uncircumcised man for taharah. The core question is whether the prepuce is considered outer skin (zahir) or inner skin (batin), affecting both the obligation to wash inside it during ghusl and whether urine reaching it invalidates wudhu. We will examine three views presented by earlier mashayikh, the tarjih by later authorities, and the practical resolution offered by Al-Shurunbulali.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Hanafi views
The passage lists three distinct positions held by earlier Hanafi scholars regarding the prepuce's legal status. Read each view carefully and note the ruling for ghusl and wudhu in each.
The first view treats qulfah as outer skin in all respects; the second treats it as inner for ghusl, outer for wudhu; the third reverses that.
2
Classify each view by its treatment of qulfah
For each view, determine whether the prepuce is classified as 'had al-zahir' (outer skin) or 'had al-batin' (inner skin) for ghusl and for invalidation of wudhu. Write them down clearly.
This classification directly drives the practical obligations.
3
Trace the tarjih (preference) in the text
The author of the text cites preferences from later Hanafi authorities: Al-Bada'i corrected the first view; Al-Hidayah chose it; Al-Bahr preferred dropping the requirement due to hardship. Finally, Al-Shurunbulali's view is mentioned as 'perhaps the truth'.
Note that tarjih is not unanimous; later scholars differed.
4
Understand Shurunbulali's conditional rule
Al-Shurunbulali's ruling in Nur al-Idah sets a condition: if the prepuce can be retracted to expose the glans, washing inside is obligatory; otherwise not. This balances the principle of removing impurity with avoiding hardship.
This is the mufta bihi position in the later school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويجب على الأَقْلَف إدخالُ الماءِ داخلَ القُلْفَة، وإن نزل البولُ إليها، ولم يخرج عنها نقض الوضوء، هذا عند بعض المشايخ ، فلها حكم الظاهرِ من كل وجه . وعند البعض : لا يجب إيصال الماء إليها في الغُسْلِ ، مع أَنَّهُ ينقض الوضوء إذا نزل البول إليها ، فلها حكم الباطن في الغُسل ، وحكم الظاهر في انتقاض الوضوء. وعند البعض لها حكم الظاهر في حق انتقاض الوضوء، وحكم الباطن في حق وجوب الغسل. وصحح في «البدائع» القول الأول واختاره صاحب «الهداية» في «مختارات النوازل». وصحح في «البحر»: سقوط غسل ما تحتها للحرج. ولعل الحق ما اختاره الشَّرنبلالي في «نور الإيضاح» : «إنّه إن أمكن فسخ القلفة وقلبها وظهورُ الحشفة يجب حينئذ غسلُ ما تحتها، وإلا فلا.
It is obligatory upon the uncircumcised to enter water inside the prepuce, and if urine descends to it and does not exit from it, it invalidates wudhu. This is according to some mashayikh, so it has the ruling of outer skin in all respects. According to some others: it is not obligatory to make water reach it in ghusl, but it invalidates wudhu if urine reaches it, so it has the ruling of inner skin in ghusl and ruling of outer skin in invalidation of wudhu. According to yet others: it has the ruling of outer skin regarding invalidation of wudhu, and ruling of inner skin regarding obligation of ghusl. And Al-Badai' corrected the first opinion, and the author of Al-Hidayah chose it in Mukhtarat al-Nawazil. And in Al-Bahr, they corrected: dropping the obligation of washing under it due to hardship. Perhaps the truth is what Al-Shurunbulali chose in Nur al-Idah: that if it is possible to retract the prepuce and fold it back so that the glans appears, then washing under it is obligatory; otherwise no.
Source pages p. 344

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya details a significant intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the uncircumcised male (al-aqlaf). The prepuce (qulfah) raises the question: is it treated as outer skin (requiring water in ghusl and invalidating wudhu if urine touches it) or inner skin (not requiring water in ghusl but not invalidating wudhu)? Three positions are presented: (1) It is outer skin in all respects – water must reach inside for ghusl, and urine inside invalidates wudhu. (2) It is inner skin for ghusl (no need to wash inside) but outer for wudhu (urine invalidates). (3) The reverse – outer for wudhu, inner for ghusl. The author of Al-Badai' and Al-Hidayah preferred the first view. Al-Bahr preferred not requiring washing under it due to hardship. Finally, Al-Shurunbulali's compromise is mentioned: if the prepuce can be retracted easily, washing is obligatory; otherwise not. This reflects the Hanafi methodology of balancing textual principles with ease.

Key Terms
قُلْفَة (qulfah): The prepuce, the skin covering the glans of the penis.
أَقْلَف (aqlaf): An uncircumcised man.
حكم الظاهر (hukm al-zahir): Ruling of outer skin: water must reach it and urine invalidates wudhu.
حكم الباطن (hukm al-batin): Ruling of inner skin: water not required in ghusl and urine does not invalidate wudhu.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is always obligatory.
B) It is never obligatory.
C) It is obligatory only if the prepuce can be retracted to expose the glans.
D) It is obligatory only if urine has touched the prepuce.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school contains a nuanced, multi-view dispute on the prepuce, resolved by Al-Shurunbulali's conditional ruling that balances obligation with ease.

Ruling on Rubbing (Dalk) in Ghusl
Clarifies that rubbing is not fard in ghusl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on dalk in ghusl and explain the dalil from the hadith of Abu Dharr.

Core Idea
Dalk is sunnah, not fard—simply touching water to the skin suffices.

In ghusl, is it obligatory to rub the body? The Hanafi school says no, contrary to the Maliki view. This lesson unpacks the evidence from the hadith of Abu Dharr, which shows that merely letting water touch the skin is enough. We will also examine a report from Abu Yusuf that suggests otherwise, and see why the dominant Hanafi position holds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Act and Ruling
Determine exactly which act is being ruled upon—dalk (rubbing) during ghusl—and what hukm level is assigned in the Hanafi school.
The matn says 'لا دلكه' meaning no obligation.
2
Locate the Dalil
Find the proof cited: the hadith of Abu Dharr from the Sunan authors. Note its chain (ahad) and text.
Hadith: 'When he finds water, let him touch it to his skin.'
3
Trace the Wajh al-Dalalah
Analyze how the hadith proves the ruling. The phrase 'فليمسه بشرته' implies mere contact, not rubbing.
The command to touch (massa) does not entail rubbing (dalk).
4
Note the Ikhtilaf
Identify the opposing views: Maliki school (fard) and Abu Yusuf (required). Understand their reasoning—thorough purification requires dalk.
Abu Yusuf's view is a weaker riwayah.
5
Evaluate the Tarjih
Understand why the Hanafi position (sunnah) is preferred: the hadith is clear, and dalk is not explicitly commanded.
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that dalk is sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا دلكه قوله : لا دلكه : أي لا يفترضُ ذلك البدن كما قاله مالك ، وروي عن أبي يوسف له معللاً بأنّ المأمور به في الغُسلِ المبالغة في التطهير، وذا يكون بالدلك ؛ أي مرس البدن باليد ومسحها عليه، وعلل أصحابنا الله بقوله لأبي ذره : «إنَّ الصعيد الطيب وضوء المسلم، وإن لم يجد الماء عشر سنين، فإذا وجد الماء فليمسه بشرته» ، أخرجه أصحاب السنن.
No rubbing: Meaning it is not obligatory upon the body, as Malik said, and it is narrated from Abu Yusuf that he reasoned because what is commanded in ghusl is thorough purification, and that is achieved by rubbing – i.e., passing the hand over the body and wiping it. And our scholars (Allah have mercy on them) reasoned with His saying to Abu Dharr: 'Pure earth is the wudu of a Muslim, even if he does not find water for ten years, and when he finds water, let him touch it to his skin.' This was reported by the authors of the Sunan.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage addresses the ruling of dalk (rubbing) during ghusl. The matn states 'لا دلكه' meaning no rubbing. The commentary explains that dalk is not fard (obligatory) on the body, contrary to the view of Imam Malik and a report from Imam Abu Yusuf. Abu Yusuf's reasoning was that ghusl requires thorough purification, which is achieved by rubbing. However, the dominant Hanafi position, supported by the hadith narrated to Abu Dharr, is that simply letting water touch the skin suffices. The hadith indicates that when a person finds water after tayammum, he merely touches it to his skin; no rubbing is required. Thus, dalk is sunnah but not fard in ghusl.

Key Terms
دلك (dalk): Rubbing or massaging the body with hands during ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It is fard (obligatory).
B) It is sunnah (recommended) but not fard.
C) It is prohibited.
D) It is only required for men.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, rubbing (dalk) during ghusl is sunnah, not fard; the hadith of Abu Dharr shows that merely touching water to the skin fulfills the obligation.

Ruling on Washing Under Prepuce and Dalk in Ghusl
Intra-Hanafi dispute on prepuce ruling and dalk obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on washing under the prepuce in ghusl and evaluate the dispute on whether rubbing (dalk) is obligatory.

Core Idea
Washing under prepuce is wajib only if retraction is possible; rubbing is sunnah, not fard.

This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute on two subtle points of ghusl: whether water must reach under the prepuce, and whether rubbing the body is required. The majority Hanafi view, reconciled by later scholars, balances textual evidence with avoiding hardship. Understanding this ruling clarifies the boundaries of obligation in purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disputed issues
The passage addresses: (1) obligation to wash under the prepuce, and (2) obligation to rub the body during ghusl. Note the difference between them.
2
Trace the Hanafi position on prepuce
Some Hanafis say the prepuce has dual nature: interior ruling for ghusl, exterior for wudu. Others reverse it. Al-Shurnuballali's reconciliation: if retraction is possible, washing underneath is wajib; otherwise not.
The mufta bihi position follows al-Shurnuballali.
3
Analyze the dalil for dalk
The Hanafi school uses the hadith of Abu Dharr: 'When he finds water, let him touch his skin with it' to argue that mere contact suffices, not rubbing. Malik and a narration from Abu Yusuf consider rubbing obligatory based on 'exaggeration in purification.'
The Hanafi response limits the command to contact, not rubbing.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical consequence: if prepuce cannot be retracted, ghusl is valid without washing underneath; ghusl is valid without rubbing.
This answers many real-world scenarios (e.g., medical conditions).
5
Confirm the mufta bihi
Al-Shurnuballali's position is relied upon: washing under prepuce is wajib only when possible; rubbing is not wajib.
This is the operative fatwa.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعند البعض : لا يجب إيصال الماء إليها في الغُسْلِ ، مع أَنَّهُ ينقض الوضوء إذا نزل البول إليها ، فلها حكم الباطن في الغُسل ، وحكم الظاهر في انتقاض الوضوء. لا دلكه الأعضاء فيفرض غسلُ ما تحتها، وينتقض الوضوء بوصول البول إليها، وإن لم يخرج من رأس الإحليل. وعند البعض لها حكم الظاهر في حق انتقاض الوضوء، وحكم الباطن في حق وجوب الغسل. وصحح في «البدائع القول الأول واختاره صاحب «الهداية» في «مختارات النوازل» وصحح في «البحر»: سقوط غسل ما تحتها للحرج. ولعل الحق ما اختاره الشَّرنبلالي في نور الإيضاح» : «إنّه إن أمكن فسخ القلفة وقلبها وظهورُ الحشفة يجب حينئذ غسلُ ما تحتها، وإلا فلا. قوله : لا دلكه : أي لا يفترضُ ذلك البدن كما قاله مالك ، وروي عن أبي يوسف له معللاً بأنّ المأمور به في الغُسلِ المبالغة في التطهير، وذا يكون بالدلك ؛ أي مرس البدن باليد ومسحها عليه، وعلل أصحابنا بقوله لأبي ذره : «إنَّ الصعيد الطيب وضوء المسلم، وإن لم يجد الماء عشر سنين، فإذا وجد الماء فليمسه بشرته» ، أخرجه أصحاب السنن.
And according to some: it is not obligatory to make water reach it (the prepuce) in ghusl, even though wudu is nullified when urine reaches it. So it has the ruling of the interior for ghusl, and the ruling of the exterior for nullification of wudu. No rubbing (dalk) of the limbs? So it is obligatory to wash what is under it, and wudu is nullified by urine reaching it, even if it does not exit from the tip of the urethra. And according to some, it has the ruling of the exterior with respect to nullification of wudu, and the ruling of the interior with respect to the obligation of ghusl. And in al-Bada'i' the first opinion was corrected, and the author of al-Hidayah chose it in Mukhtarat al-Nawazil. And in al-Bahr it was corrected: the washing of what is under it is dropped due to difficulty. Perhaps the truth is what al-Shurnuballali chose in Nur al-Idah: 'If it is possible to fold back the prepuce and turn it and the glans appears, then washing what is under it is obligatory; otherwise, no.' His saying: 'No rubbing' means that it is not obligatory on the body as Malik said, and it was narrated from Abu Yusuf that he (Abu Hanifa) reasoned that what is commanded in ghusl is exaggeration in purification, and that is achieved by rubbing, i.e., rubbing the body with the hand and wiping it. And our companions (Hanafis) reasoned with His saying to Abu Dharr: 'Pure earth is the ablution of a Muslim, even if he does not find water for ten years; when he finds water, let him touch his skin with it.' The authors of the Sunan narrated it.
Source pages p. 345

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding two issues: (1) whether water must reach under the prepuce (قلفة) in ghusl, and (2) whether rubbing (دلك) the body is obligatory in ghusl. On the first issue, some Hanafi scholars hold that the prepuce has a dual nature: internally it is considered part of the body for ghusl (so water must reach under it), but externally it is like the surface for wudu (so urine reaching it nullifies wudu even if it does not exit the urethra). Others reverse this, saying it is external for wudu and internal for ghusl. The author of al-Bada'i' and al-Hidayah prefer the first view, while al-Bahr considers washing under the prepuce dropped due to hardship. Al-Shurnuballali reconciles: if the prepuce can be retracted to reveal the glans, washing underneath is obligatory; otherwise not. On the second issue, some like Malik and a narration from Abu Yusuf consider rubbing obligatory, but the Hanafi school (اصحابنا) argues that ghusl is valid without rubbing, citing the hadith where the Prophet told Abu Dharr that dry ablution suffices, and when water is found, one only needs to touch water to the skin, not rub.

Key Terms
قلفة (qulfah): The foreskin covering the glans (حشفة).
دلك (dalk): Rubbing the body with the hand during ghusl to ensure water reaches all parts.
إحليل (ihlil): The urethra; the outlet of urine.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, when is washing under the prepuce obligatory in ghusl?
A) Always, regardless of ability to retract.
B) Only if the prepuce can be retracted to reveal the glans.
C) Only if urine reaches the prepuce.
D) It is never obligatory.

Key Takeaway
In ghusl, washing under the prepuce is wajib only when retraction is possible; rubbing the body is sunnah, not fard.

The Sunnah Acts of Ghusl: Washing Hands, Private Parts, and Removing Filth
Statement of recommended acts in ghusl with supporting hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three sunnah acts of ghusl according to the Hanafi school and explain their evidence from the hadith.

Core Idea
Washing hands, private parts, and removing filth are sunnah muakkadah, proven by the Prophet’s practice.

The passage details three recommended acts (sunan) in ghusl based on authentic hadith. Understanding these helps distinguish what is obligatory from what is meritorious. The commentary also clarifies that washing private parts applies equally to men and women, and that removing filth is a separate sunnah, not merely a consequence of impurity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three recommended acts
From the opening sentence, locate the three acts described as sunnah: washing hands to wrists, washing the private parts, and removing any filth. Note that they are listed separately.
These are distinct from the fard acts of ghusl.
2
Analyze the hadith evidence
Examine the hadith from Aisha and Maymunah to see how the Prophet performed these acts. Notice that washing hands first is emphasized, and that the Prophet’s practice confirms the sunnah status.
The hadith are authentic and used as primary dalil.
3
Clarify the ruling on washing private parts for women
The commentator explains that the ruling applies to both genders equally. Women are not required to wash the interior of their private parts, only the exterior.
Jalbī’s comment resolves potential exclusivity misunderstanding.
4
Distinguish removal of filth from washing private parts
The author refutes al-Zayla'i’s opinion that washing the private part is only for filth. It is a separate sunnah, recommended even if no filth is present.
This shows the precision of Hanafi fiqh in classifying acts.
5
Summarize the legal classification
Classify these acts as sunnah muakkadah (highly recommended), not wajib. Their omission does not affect the validity of ghusl, but one loses the reward of following the Prophet’s example.
Refer to the hanafi_position_box for the exact hukm.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسنته : أن يغسل يديه إلى رسغيه ، وفرجه، ويزيل نجساً إن كان. قوله : أن يغسل يديه ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنه يسن الابتداء بغسلهما؛ لأنهما آله التطهير، فينبغي أن يطهرا أولاً، وهو غير الغسل الذي في بدء الوضوء، به شهدت أخبار الغسل النبوي المروية في كتب الصحاح، كما بسطنا في «السعاية». منها: عن عائشة رضي الله عنها : كان النبي إذا اغتسل من الجنابة بدأ فغسل يديه ، ثم يتوضأ كما يتوضأ للصلاة، ثم يدخل أصابعه في الماء، فيخلل بها أصول شعره، ثم يصب على رأسه ثلاث غرف بيده ، ثم يفيض الماء على جلده كله. وعن ميمونة رضي الله عنها قالت: صببت للنبي صلى الله عليه وسلم غسلا فأفرغ بيمينه على يساره فغسلهما، ثم غسل فرجه، ثم قال بيده الأرض فمسحها بالتراب، ثم غسلها ثم تمضمض واستنشق ثم غسل وجهه وأفاض على رأسه، ثم تنحى فغسل قدميه ، ثم أتي بمنديل فلم ينفض بها. قوله : وفرجه؛ قال أخي جلبي: «غسلُ الفرج غير مختص بالرجل؛ لأنّ غسلها كغسله غاية الفرق أنّ لها فرجين، ولا يجب عليها تطهير الباطن». انتهى. وأنت تعلم أن ضمير فرجه راجع إلى المغتسل، والفرج يعم فرج الرجل والمرأة، فأين الاختصاص. قوله : ويزيل نجساً؛ هذه سنة على حدة، غير غسل الفرج، فإنّه غير مختص بوجودِ النجاسة عليه، كما صححه الطرابلسي في «البرهان شرح مواهب الرحمن»، وصاحب «البحر»، وغيرهما، وما توهم الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز»: إنّ غسل الفرج إنّما هو للنجاسة باطل.
And its sunnah: that he washes his hands up to the wrists, and his private parts, and removes any filth if present. His saying: 'that he washes his hands' indicates that it is sunnah to start with washing them because they are the instruments of purification, so they should be purified first. This is different from the washing at the beginning of wudu. This is attested by the narrations of the Prophet's ghusl in the authentic books, as we elaborated in al-Si'ayah. Among them: from Aisha (رضي الله عنها): 'The Prophet, when he performed ghusl from janabah, started by washing his hands, then performed wudu as for prayer, then inserted his fingers in water and combed the roots of his hair, then poured three handfuls of water on his head, then poured water over his entire body.' And from Maymunah (رضي الله عنها): 'I poured water for the Prophet for ghusl. He poured with his right hand over his left and washed them, then washed his private parts, then struck his hand on the ground and wiped it with dust, then washed it, then rinsed his mouth and nose, then washed his face and poured water over his head, then moved aside and washed his feet, then a towel was brought but he did not wipe with it.' His saying: 'and his private parts' – my brother Jalbī said: 'Washing the private part is not specific to men, because her washing is like his washing, the only difference being that she has two private parts, and she is not required to purify the interior.' End. And you know that the pronoun 'his' in 'his private parts' refers to the person performing ghusl, and 'private part' includes the private part of both man and woman – so where is the exclusivity? His saying: 'and removes filth' – this is a separate sunnah, distinct from washing the private part, because it is not specific to the presence of filth on it, as corrected by al-Tarabulsi in al-Burhan Sharh Mawahib al-Rahman, and the author of al-Bahr and others. And what al-Zayla'i imagined in Sharh al-Kanz, that washing the private part is only for filth, is false.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage states the sunnan (recommended acts) of ghusl and clarifies their details. The three sunnan are: (1) washing the hands up to the wrists at the beginning, (2) washing the private parts, and (3) removing any filth from the body if present. The commentary explains that washing the hands first is an independent sunnah because hands are the instruments of purification; this is different from the hand-washing at the start of wudu. The hadith of Aisha and Maymunah provide evidence. For washing the private parts, the commentator notes that it applies to both men and women equally; women are not required to wash inside their private parts. The removal of filth is a separate sunnah, not merely part of washing the private part, because it is recommended even if no filth is present, contrary to what al-Zayla'i mistakenly thought.

Key Terms
رسغ (rusgh): The wrist joint; the end of the forearm.
فرج (farj): The private parts (genitals) of a man or woman.
نجاسة (najasah): Filth; any impure substance on the body.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Which of the following is NOT among the sunnan of ghusl mentioned in this passage?
A) Washing hands up to wrists.
B) Washing private parts.
C) Removing filth from the body.
D) Rubbing the entire body.

Key Takeaway
The sunnah acts of ghusl are washing hands to wrists, washing private parts, and removing filth; they are recommended, not obligatory, and proven by the Prophet's practice.

Sunnah Acts of Ghusl: Washing Hands, Private Parts, and Removing Impurity
Explaining the sunnah acts of ghusl and their proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three sunnah acts at the beginning of ghusl and explain their evidence and purpose.

Core Idea
Washing hands, private parts, and removing impurity are sunnah acts that perfect ghusl but are not obligatory.

The matn of al-Wiqaya lists three sunnah acts that precede the full body wash in ghusl. These are derived from the Prophet's practice and are followed to achieve the complete sunnah manner. Understanding these acts helps distinguish the recommended from the obligatory in ritual purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three sunnah acts
From the text, extract: washing hands to wrists, washing private parts, and removing impurity from the body.
These are listed in the first sentence.
2
Understand the rationale for each
Hands are the instruments of purification, so they should be purified first. Private parts are washed because they are the source of impurity. Removing any impurity is a separate sunnah, not contingent on contamination of the private part.
The commentator elaborates on each.
3
Distinguish from fard and from wudu
These sunnah acts are not fard; omitting them does not invalidate ghusl. Also, the hand washing is different from the initial hand washing in wudu.
The text explicitly says 'ghayr al-ghusl alladhi fi bad’ al-wudu'.
4
Note the evidence
The evidence is from hadith in Sahih al-Bukhari describing the Prophet's ghusl. The commentator references these without full quotation.
The dalil is ahad, thus establishing sunnah, not wujub.

وسنته : أن يغسل يديه إلى رسغيه ، وفرجه، ويزيل نجساً إن كان
[1] قوله : أن يغسل يديه ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنه يسن الابتداء بغسلهما ؛ لأنهما آله التطهير، فينبغي أن يطهرا أولاً ، وهو غير الغسل الذي في بدء الوضوء، به شهدت أخبار الغُسلِ النبوي المروية في كتب الصحاح، كما بسطنا في «السعاية».

على بدنه، ثم يتوضأ إلا رجليه
[2] أي إن كانت النجاسة ( على بدنه ، ثم يتوضأ " إلا رجليه)، استثناء متصل ،
أي يغسل أعضاء الوضوء إلا رجليه.
[1] قوله : أي إن كانت النجس ؛ فائدة التفسير الأول الإشارة إلى أن ضمير كان راجع إلى النجس لا إلى المغتسل : كالضمائر السابقة، وفائدة التفسير الثاني الإشارة إلى أن النجس في المتن بفتح الجيم.
[2] قوله : على بدنه ؛ أشار به إلى أنّ المسنون هاهنا هو تقديمُ إزالة النجس من بدنه، وأما إزالته من ثوبه فأمر آخر، وإلى أنّ «النَّجس» أعم من أن يكون على فرجه أو عضو آخر.
[3] قوله : يتوضأ ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنّه يمسحُ الرأس في هذا الوضوء، وهو الصحيحُ كما في «الخلاصة». 
[4] قوله : استثناء متصل ؛ دفع لما يتوهم أنه لا يصح الاستثناء ؛ فإن الشرط فيه أن يكون ما بعده من جنس ما قبله داخلا في حكمه لولا الاستثناء، ومن المبين أن ما بعد إلا هاهنا أعني رجليه ليس من جنس الوضوء، وحاصله أن المستثنى منه هاهنا ليس هو الوضوء، بل أعضاء الوضوء.
[0] قوله : أي يغسل ؛ تفسير لقوله : «يتوضأ» بحيث يظهر المستثنى منه ، وأورد عليه : بأن هذا التفسير ناقص ؛ لعدم ذكر مسح الرأس فيه، مع أنه ليس في هذا الوضوء أيضاً على الرأي المعتمد، كما في «الخلاصة»، و«التاتارخانية». 
والجواب عنه : بأنه اختار رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة له أنه لا يمسح ، وبأنّ في كلامه تغليباً، فمعنى «يغسل» عام شامل للمسح ، وبأن لفظ : «ويمسح» محذوف، كما ذكره الناظرون ، كله ضعيف جداً، والذي سنح لي أنه ليس تفسيراً لقوله : «يتوضأ» حتى يرد عليه ما أورد، بل هو إظهار للمستثنى منه ، وإشارة إلى أنه استثناء من المفهوم لا من المنطوق.
And its sunnah: that he washes his hands up to the wrists, and his private parts, and removes any impurity if there is. [1] His saying: 'that he washes his hands'—there is an indication in it that it is sunnah to begin by washing them because they are the instruments of purification, so they should be purified first. This is different from the washing at the beginning of wudu. The reports of the Prophet's bath in the authentic books testify to this, as we have detailed in al-Si'aya. [2] His saying: 'and his private parts'—my brother Jalbī said: 'Washing the private parts is not specific to a man because her washing is like his washing, the only difference is that she has two private parts, and she is not obligated to purify the interior.' End. And you know that the pronoun 'his private parts' refers to the one bathing, and 'private parts' includes both a man's and a woman's private parts—so where is the specification? [3] His saying: 'and removes any impurity'—this is a separate sunnah, not the same as washing the private parts, because it is not specific to the presence of impurity on it, as al-Tarabulusi verified in al-Burhan Sharh Mawahib al-Rahman, and the author of al-Bahr, and others. What al-Zayla'i imagined in Sharh al-Kanz—that washing the private parts is only for impurity—is false. 'On his body, then he performs wudu except his feet' [2] That is, if there is impurity on his body, then he performs wudu except his feet—a connected exception. That is, he washes the limbs of wudu except his feet. [1] His saying: 'that is, if there is impurity'—the benefit of the first interpretation is to indicate that the pronoun of 'there is' refers to the impurity, not to the one bathing like the previous pronouns. The benefit of the second interpretation is to indicate that 'impurity' in the text is with fathah on the jim. [2] His saying: 'on his body'—he indicated by this that what is sunnah here is to remove impurity from his body first. As for removing it from his clothes, that is another matter. And he indicated that 'impurity' includes whether it is on his private part or another limb. [3] His saying: 'he performs wudu'—there is an indication in it that he wipes his head in this wudu, and that is the correct view as in al-Khulasa. [4] His saying: 'a connected exception'—it repels the notion that the exception is invalid. The condition for a connected exception is that what follows it is of the same kind as what precedes it, entering its ruling were it not for the exception. But it is clear that what follows 'except' here, i.e., his feet, is not of the same kind as wudu. The summary is that the excepted-from here is not wudu itself, but the limbs of wudu. [0] His saying: 'that is, he washes'—an interpretation of his saying 'he performs wudu' such that the excepted-from becomes apparent. An objection was raised: this interpretation is incomplete because it does not mention wiping the head, even though in this wudu, according to the relied-upon view, there is no wiping, as in al-Khulasa and al-Tatarkhaniyya. The response to it: (1) that he chose the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa that he does not wipe, (2) that in his speech there is a generalization, so the meaning of 'he washes' includes wiping, (3) that the word 'and wipes' is omitted, as the commentators mentioned. All of this is very weak. What has occurred to me is that it is not an interpretation of his saying 'he performs wudu' such that the objection would apply. Rather, it is a manifestation of the excepted-from and an indication that the exception is from the understood meaning, not from the uttered words.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the sunnah acts of ghusl (ritual bath) according to the Hanafi school, based on the matn of al-Wiqaya. The commentator begins by listing three sunnah acts: (1) washing the hands to the wrists, (2) washing the private parts, and (3) removing any impurity present on the body. Then the text states that after these, one performs wudu except for the feet. The commentator explains each point.

First, washing the hands is sunnah because they are the tools of purification; they should be purified before washing other parts. This is different from the initial washing of hands at the start of wudu. He cites hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari as evidence (though not quoted in full here).

Second, washing the private parts is not gender-specific; the pronoun 'his' in the matn refers to the person bathing, whether male or female. The commentator quotes his brother Jalbī, clarifying that a woman's washing is similar to a man's, except she has two private parts and is not required to wash internally.

Third, removing impurity is a separate sunnah from washing the private parts. It is not conditional upon the presence of impurity on the private part, but on any part of the body. The commentator refutes al-Zayla'i's claim that washing the private parts is only for impurity.

Then the passage discusses the phrase 'then he performs wudu except his feet'. This is a connected exception (istithna muttasil), meaning the excepted-from is not the entire wudu but the limbs of wudu; the feet are excluded because they will be washed later in the ghusl (typically after moving aside). The commentator explains that wiping the head in this wudu is included according to the correct view, as stated in al-Khulasa. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa holds that there is no wiping, but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that wiping is sunnah. The commentator critiques an interpretation that 'yaghsil' (he washes) is used as a general term covering wiping, calling such attempts weak. He proposes that the phrase 'يغسل أعضاء الوضوء' (he washes the limbs of wudu) is not a direct interpretation of 'يتوضأ' but rather an exposition of the excepted-from, indicating that the exception applies to the understood limbs of wudu, not to the spoken word.

Key Terms
غسل اليدين (ghusl al-yadayn): The act of washing the hands up to the wrists as a sunnah act at the beginning of ghusl.
استثناء متصل (istithna muttasil): A grammatical exception where the excepted item is part of the same category as the excepted-from. Not directly covered in this lesson.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on washing hands to the wrists at the start of ghusl?
A) It is fard (obligatory).
B) It is sunnah (recommended).
C) It is wajib (necessary).
D) It is makruh (disliked).

Key Takeaway
Three specific sunnah acts precede the fard washing in ghusl: washing hands, private parts, and removing impurity.

The Wudu Accompanying Ghusl and the Dispute on Wiping the Head
Explaining the wudu component of ghusl and resolving the dispute on wiping the head.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on the wudu performed during ghusl and identify the relied-upon view on wiping the head.

Core Idea
Wiping the head in the wudu of ghusl is sunnah according to the relied-upon Hanafi view, despite a weak contrary narration.

After the initial sunnah acts, the matn instructs performing wudu except for the feet. A key question is whether wiping the head is included in this wudu. The commentator navigates an intra-Hanafi dispute to establish the correct view.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah of wudu in ghusl
The matn says 'thumma yatawadda’ illa rijlayhi'—it is sunnah to perform wudu as part of ghusl, but the feet are left for later.
This wudu is not fard; it is sunnah.
2
Recognize the dispute on wiping the head
Some scholars, based on a narration from Hasan from Abu Hanifa, held that wiping is not done in this wudu. The majority (al-Khulasa, al-Tatarkhaniyya) hold that wiping is included.
The commentator says 'wa huwa al-sahih' (that is the correct view).
3
Analyze the arguments
The narration from Hasan is considered weak. The commentator critiques attempts to reinterpret 'yaghsil' to include wiping. He concludes that wiping is part of the wudu.
The mufta bihi is to wipe.
4
Determine the practical ruling
When performing ghusl, one should wipe the head during the accompanying wudu, following the relied-upon view.
The exception of feet remains because they will be washed later.

وسنته : أن يغسل يديه إلى رسغيه ، وفرجه، ويزيل نجساً إن كان
[1] قوله : أن يغسل يديه ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنه يسن الابتداء بغسلهما ؛ لأنهما آله التطهير، فينبغي أن يطهرا أولاً ، وهو غير الغسل الذي في بدء الوضوء، به شهدت أخبار الغُسلِ النبوي المروية في كتب الصحاح، كما بسطنا في «السعاية».
[2] قوله : وفرجه ؛ قال أخي جلبي : «غسلُ الفرج غير مختص بالرجل ؛ لأنّ غسلها كغسله غاية الفرق أنّ لها فرجين، ولا يجب عليها تطهير الباطن». انتهى. وأنت تعلم أن ضمير فرجه راجع إلى المغتسل، والفرج يعم فرج الرجل والمرأة، فأين الاختصاص.
[3] قوله : ويزيل نجساً ؛ هذه سنة على حدة، غير غسل الفرج، فإنّه غير مختص بوجودِ النجاسة عليه، كما صححه الطرابلسي في «البرهان شرح مواهب الرحمن»، وصاحب «البحر»، وغيرهما، وما توهم الزَّيْلَعِيُّ في «شرح الكنز»: إنّ غسل الفرج إنّما هو للنجاسة باطل.
على بدنه، ثم يتوضأ إلا رجليه
[2] أي إن كانت النجاسة ( على بدنه ، ثم يتوضأ " إلا رجليه)، استثناء متصل ،
أي يغسل أعضاء الوضوء إلا رجليه.
[1] قوله : أي إن كانت النجس ؛ فائدة التفسير الأول الإشارة إلى أن ضمير كان راجع إلى النجس لا إلى المغتسل : كالضمائر السابقة، وفائدة التفسير الثاني الإشارة إلى أن النجس في المتن بفتح الجيم.
[2] قوله : على بدنه ؛ أشار به إلى أنّ المسنون هاهنا هو تقديمُ إزالة النجس من بدنه، وأما إزالته من ثوبه فأمر آخر، وإلى أنّ «النَّجس» أعم من أن يكون على فرجه أو عضو آخر.
[3] قوله : يتوضأ ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنّه يمسحُ الرأس في هذا الوضوء، وهو الصحيحُ كما في «الخلاصة». 
[4] قوله : استثناء متصل ؛ دفع لما يتوهم أنه لا يصح الاستثناء ؛ فإن الشرط فيه أن يكون ما بعده من جنس ما قبله داخلا في حكمه لولا الاستثناء، ومن المبين أن ما بعد إلا هاهنا أعني رجليه ليس من جنس الوضوء، وحاصله أن المستثنى منه هاهنا ليس هو الوضوء، بل أعضاء الوضوء.
[0] قوله : أي يغسل ؛ تفسير لقوله : «يتوضأ» بحيث يظهر المستثنى منه ، وأورد عليه : بأن هذا التفسير ناقص ؛ لعدم ذكر مسح الرأس فيه، مع أنه ليس في هذا الوضوء أيضاً على الرأي المعتمد، كما في «الخلاصة»، و«التاتارخانية». 
والجواب عنه : بأنه اختار رواية الحسن عن أبي حنيفة له أنه لا يمسح ، وبأنّ في كلامه تغليباً، فمعنى «يغسل» عام شامل للمسح ، وبأن لفظ : «ويمسح» محذوف، كما ذكره الناظرون ، كله ضعيف جداً، والذي سنح لي أنه ليس تفسيراً لقوله : «يتوضأ» حتى يرد عليه ما أورد، بل هو إظهار للمستثنى منه ، وإشارة إلى أنه استثناء من المفهوم لا من المنطوق.
And its sunnah: that he washes his hands up to the wrists, and his private parts, and removes any impurity if there is. [1] His saying: 'that he washes his hands'—there is an indication in it that it is sunnah to begin by washing them because they are the instruments of purification, so they should be purified first. This is different from the washing at the beginning of wudu. The reports of the Prophet's bath in the authentic books testify to this, as we have detailed in al-Si'aya. [2] His saying: 'and his private parts'—my brother Jalbī said: 'Washing the private parts is not specific to a man because her washing is like his washing, the only difference is that she has two private parts, and she is not obligated to purify the interior.' End. And you know that the pronoun 'his private parts' refers to the one bathing, and 'private parts' includes both a man's and a woman's private parts—so where is the specification? [3] His saying: 'and removes any impurity'—this is a separate sunnah, not the same as washing the private parts, because it is not specific to the presence of impurity on it, as al-Tarabulusi verified in al-Burhan Sharh Mawahib al-Rahman, and the author of al-Bahr, and others. What al-Zayla'i imagined in Sharh al-Kanz—that washing the private parts is only for impurity—is false. 'On his body, then he performs wudu except his feet' [2] That is, if there is impurity on his body, then he performs wudu except his feet—a connected exception. That is, he washes the limbs of wudu except his feet. [1] His saying: 'that is, if there is impurity'—the benefit of the first interpretation is to indicate that the pronoun of 'there is' refers to the impurity, not to the one bathing like the previous pronouns. The benefit of the second interpretation is to indicate that 'impurity' in the text is with fathah on the jim. [2] His saying: 'on his body'—he indicated by this that what is sunnah here is to remove impurity from his body first. As for removing it from his clothes, that is another matter. And he indicated that 'impurity' includes whether it is on his private part or another limb. [3] His saying: 'he performs wudu'—there is an indication in it that he wipes his head in this wudu, and that is the correct view as in al-Khulasa. [4] His saying: 'a connected exception'—it repels the notion that the exception is invalid. The condition for a connected exception is that what follows it is of the same kind as what precedes it, entering its ruling were it not for the exception. But it is clear that what follows 'except' here, i.e., his feet, is not of the same kind as wudu. The summary is that the excepted-from here is not wudu itself, but the limbs of wudu. [0] His saying: 'that is, he washes'—an interpretation of his saying 'he performs wudu' such that the excepted-from becomes apparent. An objection was raised: this interpretation is incomplete because it does not mention wiping the head, even though in this wudu, according to the relied-upon view, there is no wiping, as in al-Khulasa and al-Tatarkhaniyya. The response to it: (1) that he chose the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa that he does not wipe, (2) that in his speech there is a generalization, so the meaning of 'he washes' includes wiping, (3) that the word 'and wipes' is omitted, as the commentators mentioned. All of this is very weak. What has occurred to me is that it is not an interpretation of his saying 'he performs wudu' such that the objection would apply. Rather, it is a manifestation of the excepted-from and an indication that the exception is from the understood meaning, not from the uttered words.
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the sunnah acts of ghusl (ritual bath) according to the Hanafi school, based on the matn of al-Wiqaya. The commentator begins by listing three sunnah acts: (1) washing the hands to the wrists, (2) washing the private parts, and (3) removing any impurity present on the body. Then the text states that after these, one performs wudu except for the feet. The commentator explains each point.

First, washing the hands is sunnah because they are the tools of purification; they should be purified before washing other parts. This is different from the initial washing of hands at the start of wudu. He cites hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari as evidence (though not quoted in full here).

Second, washing the private parts is not gender-specific; the pronoun 'his' in the matn refers to the person bathing, whether male or female. The commentator quotes his brother Jalbī, clarifying that a woman's washing is similar to a man's, except she has two private parts and is not required to wash internally.

Third, removing impurity is a separate sunnah from washing the private parts. It is not conditional upon the presence of impurity on the private part, but on any part of the body. The commentator refutes al-Zayla'i's claim that washing the private parts is only for impurity.

Then the passage discusses the phrase 'then he performs wudu except his feet'. This is a connected exception (istithna muttasil), meaning the excepted-from is not the entire wudu but the limbs of wudu; the feet are excluded because they will be washed later in the ghusl (typically after moving aside). The commentator explains that wiping the head in this wudu is included according to the correct view, as stated in al-Khulasa. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa holds that there is no wiping, but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that wiping is sunnah. The commentator critiques an interpretation that 'yaghsil' (he washes) is used as a general term covering wiping, calling such attempts weak. He proposes that the phrase 'يغسل أعضاء الوضوء' (he washes the limbs of wudu) is not a direct interpretation of 'يتوضأ' but rather an exposition of the excepted-from, indicating that the exception applies to the understood limbs of wudu, not to the spoken word.

Key Terms
الرأي المعتمد (al-ra'y al-mu'tamad): The relied-upon opinion, which here is that wiping the head is included.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi view, what should one do regarding wiping the head in the wudu that accompanies ghusl?
A) Not wipe because the head will be washed anyway.
B) Wipe the head; it is sunnah.
C) Wipe only if impurities are present.
D) Wipe but then rewash the head.

Key Takeaway
The relied-upon Hanafi position is to wipe the head during the wudu of ghusl, despite a weak contrary narration.

The Connected Exception (Istithna Muttasil) in the Wudu of Ghusl
Explaining the grammatical and usul basis of the connected exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the exception of the feet from wudu is a valid connected exception and identify the excepted-from.

Core Idea
The exception of the feet is valid because the excepted-from is the limbs of wudu, not wudu itself.

The phrase 'except his feet' might appear problematic because feet are not of the same genus as 'wudu'. The commentator resolves this by clarifying that the excepted-from is the 'limbs of wudu', making the connected exception (istithna muttasil) valid. This subtle usul point affects the correct understanding of the ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the exception phrase
The matn says 'thumma yatawadda’ illa rijlayhi'. The exception is 'illa rijlayhi' (except his feet).
This is a connected exception (istithna muttasil) if the excepted-from is of the same genus.
2
Understand the condition for istithna muttasil
A connected exception requires that the excepted item be of the same kind as the excepted-from, so that without the exception it would be included in the ruling.
Here, if the excepted-from is 'wudu', feet are not of the same genus (wudu is an act).
3
Recognize the potential objection
An objector might say: 'Feet are not of the same genus as wudu, so the exception is invalid.'
The commentator addresses this.
4
Analyze the commentator's resolution
The commentator explains that the excepted-from is not wudu itself, but 'a'da' al-wudu' (the limbs of wudu). The feet are of the same genus as limbs, so the exception is valid.
He says: 'al-mustathna minhu laysa huwa al-wudu, bal a'da' al-wudu'.
5
Note the final interpretation
The interpretation 'yaghsil a'da' al-wudu' is not a direct interpretation of 'yatawadda’', but a manifestation of the excepted-from. The exception applies to the understood meaning (limbs), not the uttered word.
Thus, the exception is istithna muttasil and valid.

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the sunnah acts of ghusl (ritual bath) according to the Hanafi school, based on the matn of al-Wiqaya. The commentator begins by listing three sunnah acts: (1) washing the hands to the wrists, (2) washing the private parts, and (3) removing any impurity present on the body. Then the text states that after these, one performs wudu except for the feet. The commentator explains each point.

First, washing the hands is sunnah because they are the tools of purification; they should be purified before washing other parts. This is different from the initial washing of hands at the start of wudu. He cites hadith from Sahih al-Bukhari as evidence (though not quoted in full here).

Second, washing the private parts is not gender-specific; the pronoun 'his' in the matn refers to the person bathing, whether male or female. The commentator quotes his brother Jalbī, clarifying that a woman's washing is similar to a man's, except she has two private parts and is not required to wash internally.

Third, removing impurity is a separate sunnah from washing the private parts. It is not conditional upon the presence of impurity on the private part, but on any part of the body. The commentator refutes al-Zayla'i's claim that washing the private parts is only for impurity.

Then the passage discusses the phrase 'then he performs wudu except his feet'. This is a connected exception (istithna muttasil), meaning the excepted-from is not the entire wudu but the limbs of wudu; the feet are excluded because they will be washed later in the ghusl (typically after moving aside). The commentator explains that wiping the head in this wudu is included according to the correct view, as stated in al-Khulasa. However, there is an intra-Hanafi dispute: the narration of Hasan from Abu Hanifa holds that there is no wiping, but the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is that wiping is sunnah. The commentator critiques an interpretation that 'yaghsil' (he washes) is used as a general term covering wiping, calling such attempts weak. He proposes that the phrase 'يغسل أعضاء الوضوء' (he washes the limbs of wudu) is not a direct interpretation of 'يتوضأ' but rather an exposition of the excepted-from, indicating that the exception applies to the understood limbs of wudu, not to the spoken word.

Key Terms
استثناء متصل (istithna muttasil): A connected exception where the excepted item is of the same genus as the excepted-from; here, the feet (limbs) are excepted from the limbs of wudu.
المستثنى منه (al-mustathna minhu): The excepted-from, which here is the limbs of wudu, not wudu itself.

What is the excepted-from in the phrase 'except his feet' in the wudu of ghusl according to the Hanafi commentator?
A) The entire wudu.
B) The limbs of wudu.

D) The concept of purification.

Key Takeaway
The exception of the feet is valid as a connected exception because the excepted-from is the limbs of wudu, not wudu itself.
