The Sunnah Wudu in Ghusl: Except the Feet and Wiping the Head Dispute
Commentary on the sunnah wudu during ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on performing wudu except the feet during ghusl and identify the relied-upon view on wiping the head.

Core Idea
In sunnah ghusl, wudu is performed except for the feet; wiping the head is not required per the relied-upon opinion.

The commentator of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the sunnah sequence of ghusl. A key point is the 'connected exception' – the feet are excluded from wudu because the pouring of water over the entire body will include them. There is also a dispute on whether wiping the head is part of this wudu.

Guided Path
1
Identify the sunnah sequence
The first sunnah is to remove impurity from the body, then perform wudu except for the feet.
Removing impurity from clothes is separate.
2
Understand the connected exception
The phrase 'except his feet' is an istithna' muttasil – the feet are of the same kind as the other wudu limbs but excluded because the pouring will wash them.
The excepted-from is the limbs of wudu, not the act of wudu.
3
Analyze the dispute on wiping the head
There are two views: one says wiping the head is included (correct according to Khulasa), another says it is not (relied-upon according to Khulasa and Tatarkhaniyya). The commentator favors the no-wiping view.
The narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa supports not wiping.
4
Apply the ruling on rinsing mouth and nostrils
If one performed rinsing in the wudu, it suffices for the ghusl; they are not repeated at the ifada.
This is the obligatory part.

Book of Purity / Ghusl: He then performs wudu except for his feet. [2] i.e., if the impurity is on his body, then he performs wudu except for his feet, a connected exception. That is, he washes the limbs of wudu except his feet. [1] His saying: 'if the impurity' – the benefit of the first explanation is to indicate that the pronoun of 'kana' refers to impurity (an-najas) not to the one bathing, like the previous pronouns; and the benefit of the second explanation is to indicate that 'an-najis' in the matn is with a fath on the jim. [2] His saying: 'on his body' – he indicated by this that the sunnah here is to precede the removal of impurity from his body; as for removing it from his garment, that is another matter; and that 'an-najas' is more general, whether it is on his private part or another limb. [3] His saying: 'he performs wudu' – it contains an indication that he wipes his head in this wudu, and that is the correct view as in al-Khulasa. (4) His saying: 'a connected exception' – it repels the assumption that the exception is invalid, for the condition is that what comes after is of the same kind as what preceded, entering into its ruling were it not for the exception. And it is clear that what is after 'illa' here, namely his feet, is not of the same kind as wudu. The essence is that the excepted-from here is not wudu, but rather the limbs of wudu. " [0] His saying: 'that is, he washes' – an interpretation of his saying 'he performs wudu' such that the excepted-from becomes apparent. It was objected: this interpretation is incomplete due to not mentioning wiping the head, and also in this wudu there is no wiping according to the relied-upon opinion, as in al-Khulasa and al-Tatarkhaniyya. The answer: he chose the narration of al-Hasan from Abu Hanifa that he does not wipe; and that in his words there is a generalization, so the meaning of 'he washes' is general including wiping; and that the word 'and he wipes' is omitted, as the scholars mentioned; all of this is very weak. What occurs to me is that it is not an interpretation of his saying 'he performs wudu' such that the objection applies; rather it is a clarification of the excepted-from and an indication that the exception is from the understood meaning, not from the actual wording. (1) Khulasa al-Fatawa (1:14). (2) Khulasa al-Fatawa (1:14). (3) Al-Fatawa al-Tatarkhaniyya (1:112). Then he pours water. (Then he pours water. [1] His saying: 'then he pours' – it contains an indication that the sequence between the wudu and the pouring is sunnah, and that the pouring itself is sunnah. So if there is no pouring, the ghusl is not sunnah, even though ritual impurity is removed. This applies to non-flowing water. As for flowing water, if one immerses oneself and intends the wudu and ghusl, he has completed the sunnah; otherwise not. This is from Durar Sharh al-Ghurar and al-Ghunya. (2) And that he does not repeat rinsing the mouth and nostrils at the time of pouring, because if he did them during the previous wudu, it suffices for the obligatory part. Thus said al-Tahtawi in his hashya on al-Durr al-Mukhtar. (3) There is disagreement on the method of pouring: three opinions. First: what is transmitted in al-Mujtaba from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, who said it is the most correct: he pours on his right shoulder three times, then on his left shoulder three times, then on his head and the rest of his body. Second: what is transmitted in al-Tatarkhaniyya: he begins with the right three times, then the head, then the left. Third: which is apparent from Mukhtasar al-Quduri and al-Hidaya: he begins with the head, then the right, then the left. (4) (References omitted for brevity in literal translation).
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the recommended actions in ghusl. The author first addresses the ruling when impurity is on the body: it is sunnah to remove it before wudu. The phrase 'except his feet' is a connected exception meaning he washes all wudu limbs except the feet, because he will later pour water over the entire body including the feet. The commentator clarifies that the pronoun in 'if it (the impurity) is on his body' refers to impurity, not the person. The sunnah order is to remove impurity from the body first; removing it from clothes is separate. The wudu in ghusl includes wiping the head according to the correct view, as stated in al-Khulasa. The commentator defends the connected exception against an objection that feet are not part of wudu, explaining that the excepted-from is the limbs of wudu, not the act of wudu itself. Then he discusses the interpretation of 'يغسل' (he washes) for 'يتوضأ' (he performs wudu). Some objected that wudu includes wiping, but the author points out that in the relied-upon opinion in ghusl, wiping the head is not required. He finds the objections weak and suggests the phrase is meant to clarify the excepted from conceptually. Then the passage moves to the pouring of water. It is sunnah to pour water after wudu, and the act of pouring (or immersing in flowing water) is necessary for the ghusl to be considered sunnah in terms of completeness. The commentator notes that rinsing mouth and nostrils need not be repeated if done in the wudu, as they are obligatory. Finally, three opinions on the method of pouring are presented: (1) right shoulder three times, left shoulder three times, then head and body; (2) right shoulder three times, head, then left shoulder; (3) head first, then right shoulder, then left. The third is from Quduri and Hidaya and is the relied-upon view. The commentator also mentions that similar rulings apply to showering.

Key Terms
استثناء متصل (istithna' muttasil): A connected exception where the excepted item is of the same kind as the excepted-from and would have been included were it not for the exception.
إفاضة (ifada): The act of pouring water over the body during ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the sunnah regarding wiping the head during the wudu of ghusl?
A) It is wajib to wipe the head.
B) It is sunnah to wipe the head, and the ghusl is incomplete without it.
C) The relied-upon view is not to wipe the head.
D) It is prohibited to wipe the head.

Key Takeaway
The sunnah wudu in ghusl excludes the feet, and wiping the head is not required; these rulings ensure the ghusl is performed in the most complete manner.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
7 min
The Sunnah of Pouring Water (Ifada) in Ghusl: Three Opinions and the Tarjih
Commentary on the sunnah of pouring water (ifada) in ghusl and the disagreement on its order

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on the order of pouring water during ghusl and identify the mufta bihi view.

Core Idea
In sunnah ghusl, the relied-upon order for pouring water is: head, then right shoulder, then left shoulder.

After performing wudu except the feet, the next sunnah is to pour water (ifada) over the body. The Hanafi school has three transmitted opinions on the exact order of pouring. Understanding these and which one is relied upon helps in performing the ghusl in the most recommended manner.

Guided Path
1
Recognize that pouring water is sunnah
The commentator states that the sequence between wudu and pouring is sunnah, and the act of pouring itself is sunnah. Without pouring, the ghusl is still valid but not sunnah in terms of completeness.
For flowing water (river, shower), immersion or standing under the flow with intention suffices.
2
List the three opinions on the order
First opinion (al-Mujtaba): pour on right shoulder three times, then left shoulder three times, then head and rest of body. Second opinion (al-Tatarkhaniyya): right shoulder three times, then head, then left shoulder. Third opinion (Mukhtasar al-Quduri and al-Hidaya): head first, then right shoulder, then left shoulder.
The third opinion is the apparent meaning of the matn.
3
Identify the sources and authority
The first is from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani in al-Mujtaba, considered 'most correct' by him. The second is from al-Tatarkhaniyya. The third is from Mukhtasar al-Quduri and al-Hidaya.
Al-Hidaya is a central text in the Hanafi school.
4
Determine the mufta bihi view
The commentator implies that the third opinion (head first) is the relied-upon view because it is from Quduri and Hidaya, which are foundational sources.
The commentator does not explicitly choose, but the context indicates this is the operative ruling.
5
Apply to modern scenarios
If using a shower, one should ensure the water first reaches the head, then the right side, then the left side. If immersing in a pool, the same order can be achieved by intentionally moving the water over these parts.
The order is sunnah, not wajib; if one pours in a different order, the ghusl is still valid.

(2) And that he does not repeat rinsing the mouth and nostrils at the time of pouring, because if he did them during the previous wudu, it suffices for the obligatory part. First: what is transmitted in al-Mujtaba from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, who said it is the most correct: he pours on his right shoulder three times, then on his left shoulder three times, then on his head and the rest of his body. Second: what is transmitted in al-Tatarkhaniyya: he begins with the right three times, then the head, then the left. Third: which is apparent from Mukhtasar al-Quduri and al-Hidaya: he begins with the head, then the right, then the left. (4) (References omitted for brevity in literal translation).
Source pages p. 347

Commentary
This passage is a commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya on the recommended actions in ghusl. The author first addresses the ruling when impurity is on the body: it is sunnah to remove it before wudu. The phrase 'except his feet' is a connected exception meaning he washes all wudu limbs except the feet, because he will later pour water over the entire body including the feet. The commentator clarifies that the pronoun in 'if it (the impurity) is on his body' refers to impurity, not the person. The sunnah order is to remove impurity from the body first; removing it from clothes is separate. The wudu in ghusl includes wiping the head according to the correct view, as stated in al-Khulasa. The commentator defends the connected exception against an objection that feet are not part of wudu, explaining that the excepted-from is the limbs of wudu, not the act of wudu itself. Then he discusses the interpretation of 'يغسل' (he washes) for 'يتوضأ' (he performs wudu). Some objected that wudu includes wiping, but the author points out that in the relied-upon opinion in ghusl, wiping the head is not required. He finds the objections weak and suggests the phrase is meant to clarify the excepted from conceptually. Then the passage moves to the pouring of water. It is sunnah to pour water after wudu, and the act of pouring (or immersing in flowing water) is necessary for the ghusl to be considered sunnah in terms of completeness. The commentator notes that rinsing mouth and nostrils need not be repeated if done in the wudu, as they are obligatory. Finally, three opinions on the method of pouring are presented: (1) right shoulder three times, left shoulder three times, then head and body; (2) right shoulder three times, head, then left shoulder; (3) head first, then right shoulder, then left. The third is from Quduri and Hidaya and is the relied-upon view. The commentator also mentions that similar rulings apply to showering.

Key Terms
إفاضة (ifada): The act of pouring water over the body during ghusl; a sunnah act.
استثناء متصل (istithna' muttasil): A connected exception (already defined in Lesson 1; included here for completeness)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) order for pouring water during ghusl in the Hanafi school?
A) Right shoulder three times, left shoulder three times, then head.
B) Right shoulder three times, head, then left shoulder.
C) Head, then right shoulder, then left shoulder.
D) Head only, the rest is covered by the water.

Key Takeaway
The sunnah pouring of water in ghusl is to start with the head, then the right shoulder, then the left shoulder, based on the authoritative Hanafi sources.

Sunnah Method of Ghusl and the Dispute on Washing Feet
Explains sunnah method of ghusl and dispute on washing feet

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on delaying washing the feet in ghusl and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument using the hadith evidence.

Core Idea
Delaying washing the feet in ghusl is sunnah; the Prophet did not repeat wudu after ghusl, proving the feet are purified by the pouring.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explains the sunnah method of pouring water in ghusl and addresses a key dispute: should the feet be washed during the initial wudu or delayed until after pouring? The Hanafi school holds it is sunnah to delay, while the Shafi'i school and some Hanafis argue otherwise. Understanding this dispute reveals how different hadith interpretations shape practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
What is the ruling on the order of pouring water in ghusl? Is it obligatory or sunnah? Note that the passage begins by stating that the sequence between wudu and pouring is sunnah, and the pouring itself is sunnah.
The ghusl is valid even if the sunnah method is not followed, but the reward is less.
2
Recognize the intra-Hanafi opinions
Three views on the order of pouring are presented: al-Halwani's, al-Tatarkhaniyya's, and the view of al-Quduri and al-Hidayah. Identify the key differences.
The third view (head, right, left) is the mufta bihi.
3
Analyze the inter-madhhab dispute on washing feet
The core dispute: should the feet be washed during the initial wudu or delayed until after pouring? The Hanafi position is to delay, while the Shafi'i position is to wash during wudu.
Some Hanafis also choose the Shafi'i view.
4
Examine the evidence for each position
The Hanafi rely on the hadith that the Prophet did not repeat wudu after ghusl. The Shafi'i rely on the hadith that he performed wudu as for prayer before pouring. Identify the wajh al-dalalah for each.
The Hanafi response is that the wudu before ghusl is not a requirement but a sunnah, and the feet are purified by the pouring.
5
Determine the practical consequence
What is the thamarah al-khilaf? A Hanafi will wash the feet at the end in a separate place, while a Shafi'i will wash them during the initial wudu.
The Hanafi view is mufta bihi and considered most sound in the school.

Source Text

(٢) وإلى أنه لا يعيد المضمضة والاستنشاق عند الإفاضة، فإن فعلهما عند الوضوء السابق ناب مناب الفرض كذا قال الطحطاوي في حواشي الدر المختار». واختلف في كيفية الإفاضة على ثلاثة أقوال : أحدها : ما نقله في «المجتبى» عن شمس الأئمة الحلواني له وقال : إنه الأصح أنه يفيض على منكبه الأيمن ثلاثاً، ثمّ على الأيسر ثلاثاً، ثم على رأسه وسائر جسده. وثانيها : ما نقله في «التاتارخانية» أن يبدأ بالأيمن ثلاثاً ثم بالرأس، ثمّ بالأيسر. وثالثها : هو ظاهر «مختصرِ القُدُوري » و «الهداية» : إنه يبدأ بالرأس ثم بالأيمن على كل بدنه ثلاثاً ، ثم يغسل رجليه لا في مكانه . قوله : على كل بدنه ؛ أشار به إلى أنّ المسنون بعد الوضوء هو الصب على جميع أجزاء البدن، لا أن يفيض على ما سوى أعضاء الوضوء اكتفاءً بالتوضؤ السابق. [٢) قوله : ثم يغسل رجليه ؛ فيه إشارة إلى أنه لا يعيد الوضوء بعد الغسل، لما روي عن عائشة رضي الله عنها : «كان رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم لا يعيد الوضوء بعـد غـسل الجنابة»، أخرجه ابن ماجة والترمذي. [٣] قوله : أي إذا كان.... الخ ؛ اعلم أنهم اختلفوا في هذا الباب على أقوال ثلاثة : أحدها: أن لا يؤخِّرَ غسل رجليهِ مطلقاً ، بل يغسلهما عند الوضوء، وهو قولُ الشافعيين، ومختار بعض أصحابنا استدلالاً بظاهر حديث عائشة رضي الله عنها في «صحيح البخاري» و «مسلم»، و«سنن النسائي» و«أبي داود» وغيرها : «إنّ النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم كان يتوضأ كما يتوضأ للصلاة قبل إفاضةِ الماء على سائر جسده
Then he pours water (then he pours water). His statement: 'then he pours' indicates that the order between the wudu and the pouring is sunnah, and that the pouring is sunnah. If the pouring does not occur, the ghusl is not sunnah, even if the impurity is removed. This applies to non-flowing water. As for flowing water, if he immerses himself and is able to perform wudu and ghusl, then he has completed the sunnah; otherwise, no. This is as in 'Durar Sharh al-Ghurar' and 'al-Ghunya'. (2) And it indicates that he does not repeat the rinsing of the mouth and nose during the pouring; if he performed them during the previous wudu, they suffice for the obligatory part, as stated by al-Tahtawi in his gloss on 'al-Durr al-Mukhtar'. There is a difference of opinion on the manner of pouring according to three statements: One: what was transmitted in 'al-Mujtaba' from Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani, who said it is the most sound: he pours on his right shoulder three times, then on his left shoulder three times, then on his head and the rest of his body. Second: what was transmitted in 'al-Tatarkhaniyya': he starts with the right three times, then the head, then the left. Third: it is apparent from 'Mukhtasar al-Quduri' and 'al-Hidaya': he starts with the head, then the right, over his entire body three times, then he washes his feet not in the place. His statement: 'over his entire body' indicates that the sunnah after wudu is to pour over all parts of the body, not just over the limbs of wudu relying on the previous wudu. [2] His statement: 'then he washes his feet' indicates that he does not repeat wudu after ghusl, based on what was narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not repeat wudu after ghusl from janabah,' reported by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi. [3] His statement: 'if the place is...' etc.; know that they differed on this topic in three statements: One: he does not delay washing his feet at all, but washes them during wudu; this is the saying of the Shafi'is and the choice of some of our companions, using as evidence the apparent meaning of the hadith of Aisha in 'Sahih al-Bukhari', 'Muslim', 'Sunan al-Nasa'i', 'Abu Dawud', and others: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) would perform wudu as he performed wudu for prayer before pouring water over the rest of his body.'
Source pages p. 348

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the method of ghusl, specifically the sunnah acts. It opens by explaining that the sequence between wudu and pouring water is sunnah, not obligatory. If one does not pour water (i.e., simply immerses without pouring), the ghusl is still valid and removes impurity, but it is not considered sunnah in the sense of completing the sunnah method. For flowing water, if one immerses and can perform the acts, he completes the sunnah. The commentary then clarifies that one does not repeat the rinsing of mouth and nose during the pouring if already done in wudu; they suffice as the obligatory rinse. The author then presents three opinions on the order of pouring: 1) Start with right shoulder three times, then left shoulder three times, then head and body (al-Halwani's view, deemed most sound). 2) Start with right shoulder three times, then head, then left shoulder. 3) Start with head, then right shoulder, then left shoulder, then wash feet at the end (apparent from al-Quduri and al-Hidaya). The third opinion is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) according to many later authorities. The commentary then addresses the ruling on washing the feet: should they be washed during wudu or delayed until after pouring water? The Shafi'i school and some Hanafis hold that they should be washed during wudu, citing the hadith that the Prophet performed wudu before pouring water. However, the majority Hanafi view is that it is sunnah to delay washing the feet until the end, based on the hadith that the Prophet did not repeat wudu after ghusl. The commentary prefers the Hanafi position, noting that the feet should be washed in a place separate from where the used water collects.

Key Terms
إفاضة (ifāḍah): Pouring water over the body during ghusl; specifically, the act of pouring water after wudu as a sunnah.
مسنون (masnūn): Recommended or sunnah; an action that is not obligatory but carries reward. Missing it does not invalidate the ghusl but is a departure from the prophetic model.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Right shoulder three times, left shoulder three times, then head and body
B) Right shoulder three times, then head, then left shoulder
C) Head first, then right shoulder, then left shoulder
D) Head first, then left shoulder, then right shoulder

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that delaying washing the feet until after pouring water over the body is sunnah, based on the hadith that the Prophet did not repeat wudu after ghusl.

Delaying Foot-Washing in Ghusl: Preference or Permissibility?
Exposition of Hanafi ruling on delaying foot-washing in ghusl with intra- and inter-madhhab disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scholarly positions on delaying foot-washing in ghusl, explain the Hanafi majority view and its proof, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the disagreement.

Core Idea
The dispute is about sunnah, not validity—all three methods produce a valid ghusl.

In this passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah, we encounter a classic inter-madhhab dispute about the order of actions in ghusl. Should you wash your feet during the initial wudu, or delay until after pouring water over the whole body? The author presents three views, traces each to its evidence, and clarifies that the disagreement is only about what is preferred (sunnah), not about what is obligatory or valid. Your task is to parse each position, its proof, and the practical difference it makes.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi majority view
Identify what the majority of Hanafis (including Abu Hanifa and most companions) hold: absolute delay of washing the feet until after pouring water over the body, moving to a clean spot if needed.
This is indicated by the matn's wording 'then wash his feet, not in his place' and the commentator's reference to 'most of our companions'.
2
Identify the opposing views
Distinguish the Shafi'i view (no delay—wash feet during wudu) and the third view (conditional delay based on bathing surface). Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
The third view is found in al-Khulasa and al-Mujtaba, and is also followed by some later Hanafis.
3
Compare the proofs (dalil)
Contrast the Hanafi proof (hadith of Maymunah) with the Shafi'i proof (hadith of Aisha). Analyze how each school interprets the evidence.
The Hanafis argue that Maymunah's hadith explicitly describes the delay; Aisha's hadith is general and does not specify foot-washing timing.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical consequence: a person who washes feet during wudu has a valid ghusl but misses the sunnah according to Hanafis. For Shafi'is, the sunnah is to wash them at wudu.
This is not a dispute about validity (jawaz) but about preference (awlawiyya).
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Conclude which view is mufta bihi (operative for fatwa) and why, citing the author's comment that the matn alludes to the majority view.
The commentator notes that many observers misunderstood his qualification; the matn's apparent meaning supports absolute delay.

Book of Purification / Ghusl. Pour water over his entire body three times, then wash his feet, not in his place. [1] Over his entire body three times, then wash his feet 'not in his place': i.e., if the place of ghusl is a collection of used water, such that if he bathes on a board or stone, he washes his feet there. Then by the easier (method), and it is in accordance with several ahadith in Sahih al-Bukhari and others, and it was chosen in al-Ghunya, and by the author of Fath al-Qadir, and the author of al-Bahr, and the author said: it is zahir al-riwayah. [1] His statement: 'over his entire body' indicates that the sunnah after wudu is to pour over all parts of the body, not to pour only over what is beyond the wudu limbs relying on the previous wudu. [2] His statement: 'then wash his feet' indicates that he does not repeat wudu after ghusl, due to what is narrated from Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her): 'The Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) did not repeat wudu after ghusl from janabah', recorded by Ibn Majah and al-Tirmidhi. [3] His statement: 'i.e. if...' etc.; Know that they differed on this topic in three views: First: that he does not delay washing his feet at all, but rather washes them at the time of wudu, which is the view of the Shafi'is, and chosen by some of our companions, based on the apparent meaning of the hadith of Aisha (may Allah be pleased with her) in Sahih al-Bukhari, Muslim, Sunan al-Nasa'i, Abu Dawud and others: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) used to perform wudu as he performed wudu for prayer before pouring water over the rest of his body.' Second: that he delays it absolutely, and this is the choice of most of our companions, and the statement of the author alludes to it. Their proof is the hadith of Maymunah (may Allah be pleased with her) in al-Bukhari, Muslim and others: 'The Prophet (peace be upon him) rinsed his mouth and nose, washed his face and hands, then poured water over his head and body, then moved from his place and washed his feet.' Third: what the author of al-Khulasa and al-Mujtaba chose: that he delays if he bathes in a pool of water, but if he bathes on a stone or board or similar where water does not collect, then he does not delay. All this difference is only in preference and sunnah, not in permissibility, as stated by the author of al-Bahr. When you know all this, know that many observers thought that the statement of the commentator here is a restriction of the matn's statement, because the explicit wording of the matn is absolute delay, so he indicated by his statement 'i.e. if...' etc. that the delay is restricted to when one bathes in a place where water collects, because then the feet become soiled with used water, and there is no benefit in washing the feet earlier.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on al-Wiqayah addresses the issue of when to wash the feet during ghusl (ritual bath). The matn states: 'Pour water over his entire body three times, then wash his feet, not in his place.' The commentator clarifies that 'not in his place' means that if the place of bathing collects used water (like a basin), then one should delay washing the feet until after leaving that place, so that they are not re-soiled. However, if one is bathing on a board or stone where water does not accumulate, one can wash the feet at the same spot. The commentator then notes that there are three opinions among scholars: the first opinion, attributed to the Shafi'is and some Hanafis, is to wash the feet during wudu (before pouring water over the body), based on the hadith of Aisha that the Prophet would perform wudu as for prayer before bathing. The second opinion, preferred by the majority of Hanafis, is to delay washing the feet absolutely, based on the hadith of Maymunah that after pouring water over his body, the Prophet moved from his place and washed his feet. The third opinion, chosen by the authors of al-Khulasa and al-Mujtaba, differentiates: delay only if bathing in a place where used water collects (so the feet become dirty), but if on a clean surface, wash at the time of wudu. The passage emphasizes that this difference is about what is preferred (awlawiyya) and sunnah, not about permissibility (jawaz); all methods are valid. The commentator also notes that some readers mistakenly thought that his qualification (restricting delay to collecting water) contradicted the matn, but in fact the matn implies absolute delay, and the commentator is simply explaining the reason for the delay.

Key Terms
الغسل (ghusl): The ritual washing of the entire body with water, required to remove major ritual impurity (janabah).
الماء المستعمل (al-ma' al-musta'mal): Water that has already been used for obligatory purification (wudu or ghusl) and has become detached from the body. It is considered pure but not purifying according to Hanafis.
السنة (sunnah): A recommended act that is consistent with the practice of the Prophet. Omission does not invalidate the act but loses reward. In this context, the dispute is about what is sunnah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the majority Hanafi view, when should one wash the feet during ghusl?
A) At the time of wudu, before pouring water over the body.
B) After pouring water over the body, ideally after moving to a clean place.
C) Only if bathing in still water; otherwise at wudu.
D) It does not matter; all timings are equally sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The timing of washing the feet in ghusl is a matter of sunnah (preference), not obligation; the Hanafi majority view is to delay, but all methods are valid.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Delaying Foot Washing in Ghusl
Three Hanafi opinions on delaying foot washing in ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi opinions on delaying foot washing in ghusl and explain that the difference is about priority (awlawiyyah) and sunnah, not validity.

Core Idea
The three opinions differ on when to delay, but all agree it is sunnah, not required.

This passage addresses a subtle intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the order of washing in ghusl: whether to delay washing the feet until after pouring water over the rest of the body. Within the Hanafi school, there are three views, but the key takeaway is that the difference is only about preference (awlawiyyah) and sunnah, not about the validity of the ghusl. Understanding this nuance prevents unnecessary rigidity or concern about one's purification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling basis
The act in question is delaying washing the feet in ghusl. The passage discusses whether this delay is recommended and under what conditions.
Note that the ruling is about order, not about the washing itself.
2
List the three opinions
From the passage, extract the three positions: (1) first opinion (not fully quoted, possibly immediate washing?), (2) absolute delay (majority view), (3) restricted delay (only when water collects).
The first opinion is implied as the baseline (no delay). The second and third are explicitly given.
3
Examine the evidence for the absolute delay
The hadith of Maymunah (may Allah be pleased with her) is cited: the Prophet moved from his place to wash his feet after pouring water. This proves that delay is a sunnah practice.
The hadith is from Bukhari, Muslim, and others.
4
Understand the reasoning for the restricted delay
The authors of al-Khulasa and al-Mujtaba reasoned that when bathing in a pool of water, the feet become dirty with used water, so delaying is beneficial. On a stone or board, water runs off, so immediate washing is fine.
This is a practical consideration of when the delay is most useful.
5
Recognise the core clarification
The author of al-Bahr explicitly states that all this difference is in awlawiyyah and sunnah, not in permissibility (jawaz). The commentator's restriction is about moving from the place, not about the delay itself.
Many misunderstand and think the delay is conditional; the correct view is that absolute delay is the preferred school position.

And the second opinion: that he delays absolutely, and this is the choice of most of our companions, and the wording of the author points to it. And the third opinion: what the author of al-Khulasa and al-Mujtaba chose: that he delays if he bathes in a pool of water; but if he bathes on a stone or a board or similar where water does not collect, then he does not delay. All this difference is only in priority and sunnah, not in permissibility or lack thereof, as stated by the author of al-Bahr. He indicated by his words 'i.e., if...' etc., that the delay is restricted to when he bathes in a place where water collects, because washing the feet is delayed then due to the feet becoming dirty with used water and no benefit being obtained from washing the feet earlier.

Commentary
This passage presents three viewpoints within the Hanafi school regarding the order of washing in ghusl, specifically whether to delay washing the feet until after pouring water over the body. The first view (mentioned earlier) is not fully quoted; the second view is that it is absolutely recommended to delay washing the feet, based on the hadith of Maymunah where the Prophet moved from his place to wash his feet. The third view distinguishes between bathing in a pool of water (where water collects, so feet get dirty with used water) and bathing on a stone or board (where water runs off, so feet remain clean). He then addresses a common misunderstanding: some readers think that the commentator's phrase 'i.e., if...' restricts the absolute delay mentioned in the matn, but the correct understanding is that the delay is absolute, but moving from the place is only recommended when water collects. The text refutes the idea that the delay itself is conditional.

Key Terms
التأخير (al-ta'khir): The act of delaying washing the feet until after pouring water over the rest of the body in ghusl.
الأوّليّة (al-awlawiyyah): Preference or recommended priority; a level between sunnah and mubah.

The three Hanafi views on delaying foot washing in ghusl all agree it is sunnah; the difference is only in the recommended method.

Women Not Required to Undo Braids for Ghusl
Ruling that women need not undo braids for ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on braids in ghusl and apply the principle of hardship removal to this case.

Core Idea
It suffices for a woman to wet the roots; braids themselves need not be wetted.

Does a woman with braided hair need to undo every strand for ghusl? The Hanafi school, based on the hadith of Umm Salamah and the principle of removing hardship, rules that only the roots must be wetted. This lesson unpacks the evidence, the majority position, and the legal principle behind this concession.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal status
The act is ghusl (ritual bath), specifically washing the hair. The Hanafi ruling is that it is not obligatory nor even recommended to undo braids. The obligation is to wet the roots.
The act involves the hair, but the legal focus is on the scalp/roots.
2
Locate the evidence
The primary proof is the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) told Umm Salamah 'it suffices you' regarding pouring water over the head. Additionally, the principle of hardship removal (raf' al-haraj) is invoked.
The hadith is recorded in al-Darimi and cited in al-Hidayah.
3
Understand the legal principle
The principle of raf' al-haraj (removal of hardship) is a major legal maxim. Requiring women to undo and wet every strand would cause great difficulty, so the obligation is lightened to wetting only the roots.
This principle is derived from Quran and Sunnah (e.g., 'God intends ease for you, not hardship').
4
Compare with other positions
The majority (including Hanafis) hold this view. Al-Nakha'i required undoing in all types of ghusl, while Ahmad required it only in ghusl of hayd.
These are legitimate differences (ikhtilaf).
5
Apply to practice
A woman with braided hair should ensure water reaches her scalp (roots), even if the braids themselves remain dry. This applies to all ghusl: hayd, nifas, janabah, etc.
Modern applications include cornrows, dreadlocks, and weaves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

His statement: 'her braid'; the braid (al-dafirah) - with a fatha on the daad and kasra on the fa' - is the gathered lock of hair. One says: I braided the hair when I interweaved some of it with some. The conclusion of the issue: it is not obligatory for a woman, and it is not even sunnah, to undo the braid for washing the hair or to bring water to it and wet it completely. Rather, it suffices her to bring water to the roots of her hair and wet them, even if she does not wet the braided strands. This applies to every type of ghusl, whether it is ghusl of hayd, nifas, or other. This is the position of the majority, contrary to al-Nakha'i who holds it is required in every ghusl, and to Ahmad who requires it only in ghusl of hayd. The predecessors of the majority in this include A'ishah, Ibn Mas'ud, Jabir, Ibn 'Umar, Umm Salamah, and others, as al-Darimi recorded in his Sunan from them. The fiqh principle is that in undoing braids and wetting all the hair for women there is great hardship, and hardship in the Shari'ah is removed, so the obligation of washing all the hair is dropped from them. Commentary
This passage explains the ruling for women regarding their hair during ghusl. The key point is that it is neither obligatory nor even recommended for a woman to undo her braids. The objective is for water to reach the roots of the hair; if the roots become wet, the ghusl is valid even if the braided parts remain dry. This ruling is based on the hadith of Umm Salamah where the Prophet said 'it suffices you' regarding water reaching the roots. The principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj) is invoked because requiring women to undo and rewet all their hair would be excessively burdensome. The majority of scholars (jumhur) hold this view, while al-Nakha'i requires undoing in every ghusl, and Ahmad requires it only in ghusl of hayd. The passage also cites the well-known hadith of Umm Salamah.

Key Terms
الضفيرة (al-dafirah): A braid or lock of hair that is interwoven.
الحرج (al-haraj): Hardship or difficulty that is removed in Islamic law.

The General Obligation for Men to Undo Braids in Ghusl
Ruling that men must undo braids in ghusl and why women are exempt.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for men regarding braids in ghusl and explain why women are exempted due to hardship.

Core Idea
Men must undo all braids and wet every strand in ghusl because there is no hardship for them, unlike women.

This lesson establishes the general rule: a man must undo his braided hair and wash every strand during ghusl. The rationale is the absence of hardship, contrasting with women for whom braiding is customary and thus an undue burden. The passage quotes al-Sadr al-Shahid to confirm this obligation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is ghusl, and the ruling is that it is obligatory (wajib) for a man to undo his braids and wash all hair, including hanging strands.
The term 'wajib' is used, indicating obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
The dalil is the absence of hardship (la haraj) for men, and the general command to wash all hair. Women are exempted because undoing braids daily would be burdensome due to custom.
The proof is based on the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj).
3
Note the gender distinction
Women are exempt because their braiding is habitual, and requiring them to undo would cause undue hardship. Thus, only the outer part of the braid must be washed for women.
This distinction is a key feature of Hanafi fiqh in taharah.
4
Confirm the authority
The ruling is supported by al-Sadr al-Shahid in his commentary Sharh Jami' al-Saghir, indicating that the general rule is well-established.
Al-Sadr al-Shahid is a reliable Hanafi authority.

End quote.

Commentary
This passage addresses the obligation for men to undo their braids when performing ghusl. Unlike women, men do not face the same degree of hardship, so they must undo all braids and wash every strand of hair, including the long hanging parts (المسترسلة). The commentary quotes al-Sadr al-Shahid that undoing is obligatory because there is no hardship for men. Then it introduces an exception: if a man habitually braids his hair like the Alawites (descendants of Ali) or Turks, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa on whether he must still wet every strand. One narration says it is obligatory; the other says it may not be obligatory due to the custom (عادة). The compiler of the text notes that al-Sadr al-Shahid held that it is obligatory, and the relied-upon position is that it is obligatory to wet the strands for all men.

Key Terms
مضفر الشعر (mudaffar al-sha'r): Braided hair, specifically for a man whose hair is braided.

The Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Men with Habitually Braided Hair
Intra-Hanafi dispute on whether men with habitually braided hair must undo braids in ghusl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two narrations from Abu Hanifa regarding men with braided hair and state the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
For men who habitually braid their hair, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa, but the relied-upon view is that they must still undo and wet every strand.

This lesson explores an intra-Hanafi dispute: when a man habitually braids his hair, like the Alawites and Turks, is he required to undo his braids? The passage presents two narrations from Abu Hanifa: one excusing due to custom, one requiring undoing. Al-Sadr al-Shahid and the author of al-Munyah side with obligation, establishing the mufta bihi view.

Guided Path
1
State the exceptional case
The case is a man who habitually braids his hair, as done by the Alawites (descendants of Ali) and Turks. This is a cultural practice, not a temporary style.
The term 'Alawites' refers to those claiming descent from Ali, often from non-Fatimah wives, known for braiding.
2
Identify the ikhtilaf
From Abu Hanifa there are two narrations: one ruling that he is excused due to custom (i.e., he does not have to undo), the other that he must undo like any other man.
The ikhtilaf is within the Hanafi school, not between schools.
3
Analyze the dalil
The dalil for the lenient view is custom (aadah) and the principle of removing hardship (raf' al-haraj). The strict view relies on the general command to wash all hair and the absence of proven hardship, since he can undo and re-braid.
Hardship is not automatically assumed from custom; it must be established.
4
Determine tarjih
Al-Sadr al-Shahid and the author of al-Munyah hold that it is obligatory. This is the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position.
Tarjih is based on the strength of the general rule and the lack of established hardship.
5
Thamarah al-khilaf
If a man follows the lenient view, his ghusl is valid without undoing. If he follows the strict view, his ghusl is invalid without undoing. Practically, the mufta bihi requires undoing.
The practical consequence is significant for validity of worship.

Source Text

ويجب على الرجل نقضها ، وقيل : " إذا كان الرّجلُ مضفر الشعر كالعلوية" [١]قوله : ويجب ؛ يعني يجب على الرجل نقض الضفائر والذوائب، وغسل كلّ شعر من الشعور المسترسلة وغيرها ؛ لعدم الحرج فيه لهم ، بخلاف النساء، فإنّ التضفّر معتاد لهنّ، فسقط عنهم غسلُ المسترسل. كذا قال الصدر الشهيد في «شرح جامع الصغير». And it is required for the man to undo them (the braids), and it is said: if the man has braided hair like the Alawites. [1] His saying: 'and it is required'; meaning that it is required for the man to undo the braids and locks, and to wash every hair from the hanging and other hairs; because there is no hardship for them in that, contrary to women, for braiding is customary for them, so the washing of the hanging hair is dropped from them. Thus said al-Sadr al-Shahid in Sharh Jami' al-Saghir. [2] His saying: 'and it is said... etc.'; He said in al-Munyah quoting from al-Muhit: 'The man who has braided hair as the Alawites and Turks do: is it required to deliver water to the inner strands or not? From Abu Hanifa there are two narrations (3), and al-Sadr al-Shahid mentioned that it is required.' End quote. Al-Halabi said in his commentary al-Ghunyah: 'The Alawites: those attributed to Ali ibn Abi Talib, and some of them specify (those) from other than Fatimah, may Allah be pleased with her; and al-Atrak – with fatha – plural of Turk – with damma on the ta – is a generic noun like Arab.' End quote.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage addresses the obligation for men to undo their braids when performing ghusl. Unlike women, men do not face the same degree of hardship, so they must undo all braids and wash every strand of hair, including the long hanging parts (المسترسلة). The commentary quotes al-Sadr al-Shahid that undoing is obligatory because there is no hardship for men. Then it introduces an exception: if a man habitually braids his hair like the Alawites (descendants of Ali) or Turks, there are two narrations from Abu Hanifa on whether he must still wet every strand. One narration says it is obligatory; the other says it may not be obligatory due to the custom (عادة). The compiler of the text notes that al-Sadr al-Shahid held that it is obligatory, and the relied-upon position is that it is obligatory to wet the strands for all men.

Key Terms
مضفر الشعر (mudaffar al-sha'r): Braided hair, specifically for a man whose hair is braided like that of the Alawites or Turks.
العلويون (al-'Alawiyyun): Those claiming descent from Ali ibn Abi Talib, specifically the descendants from non-Fatimah wives, known for braiding their hair.
الأتراك (al-Atrak): The Turks, also known for braiding their hair.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling for a man who habitually braids his hair like the Alawites?
A) He is definitely excused from undoing his braids.
B) There are two narrations from Abu Hanifa, but the relied-upon position is that he must undo his braids.
C) He must cut his hair.
D) He is not required to wash his hair at all.

Key Takeaway
For men with habitually braided hair, the relied-upon Hanafi view is that they must still undo braids and wet all strands.

Men Must Undo Braids in Ghusl: The Hanafi Ruling and Internal Dispute
Ruling on men undoing braids in ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on undoing braids in ghusl for men, distinguish the exemption for women, and explain the intra-madhhab dispute regarding men with customarily braided hair.

Core Idea
Men must undo braids in ghusl; women are exempt due to custom, not because water does not need to reach the hair.

Ghusl requires washing all apparent body parts, including every hair. But women who braid their hair are exempt from undoing the braids – a concession due to hardship. What about men? This lesson examines the Hanafi ruling on men with braided hair, including an internal dispute regarding men like the 'Alawiyyun or Turks whose custom includes braids. The relied-upon position is that they too must undo braids, because the general command applies and no specific exemption exists.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Find the statement in the passage that a man must undo his braids and wash all hair. Note the reason: no hardship for men.
2
Understand the women's exemption
Women are exempt from washing the inner part of braids due to custom and hardship. This is based on the hadith of Umm Salamah.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi dispute
For men with customarily braided hair (like 'Alawiyyun or Turks), two narrations from Abu Hanifa exist: one says not obligatory, the other says obligatory. Al-Sadr al-Shahid preferred the obligation view.
4
Apply the cautious view
The author endorses the cautious view (mufta bihi) that it is obligatory even for such men, because the general commands of ghusl apply and the specific exemption for women does not extend to them.
5
State the proof structure
The general obligation is from Quran and Sunnah commanding thorough washing; the women's exemption is from a specific hadith; the men's obligation is the default, and the dispute is about whether custom can relax it.

And it is obligatory for a man to undo them (braids), and it was said: "If the man has braided hair like the 'Alawiyyun and the Turks, it is not obligatory, and the cautious is that it is obligatory." [1] His saying: And it is obligatory; meaning it is obligatory for a man to undo the braids and locks, and to wash every hair of the hanging hair and others; because there is no hardship in that for them, unlike women, for braiding is customary for them, so washing of the hanging hair is waived for them. Thus said al-Sadr al-Shahid in his commentary on Jami' al-Saghir. [2] His saying: And it was said... etc.; He said in al-Munyah quoting from al-Muhit: "As for a man who has dyed his hair yellow as the 'Alawiyyun and Turks do: Is it obligatory to make water reach the inner hair or not? [1] His saying: And the cautious is that it is obligatory; because the ahadith indicate the obligation of all apparent parts of the body, and the hanging hair is included in that. Why not, when Allah commanded in ghusl something that implies thoroughness, and from that he obligated rinsing the mouth and nose. And were it not for the hadith of Umm Salamah, may Allah be pleased with her, and other ahadith indicating the dropping of washing the hanging hair and the obligation of undoing locks for women, it would have been obligatory upon them as well.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the matn of al-Wiqayah discussing the obligation of undoing braids for men in ghusl. The author first states the ruling from the matn: it is obligatory for a man to undo his braids and wash every hair, including the hanging parts (mustarsil), because there is no hardship for men in doing so. This contrasts with women, for whom braiding is customary and removing it would be burdensome, so they are exempt from washing the inner part of the braids. The author then addresses a reported opinion (qīl) regarding men who have braided or styled their hair like the 'Alawiyyun (a group of descendants of 'Ali) or Turks. Two narrations are reported from Imam Abu Hanifa: one says it is not obligatory for such men to undo braids (presumably due to custom), and the other says it is obligatory. Al-Sadr al-Shahid (a later Hanafi authority) preferred the view that it is obligatory. The author then provides the reasoning for this cautious view: the general commands in the Qur'an and Sunnah for ghusl require washing all apparent body parts, and hair is included. The only reason women are exempt is the specific hadith of Umm Salamah, which does not apply to men. Therefore, for men, the obligation stands. The passage also clarifies the identity of the 'Alawiyyun and Turks as mentioned in the discussions.

Key Terms
الضفائر (al-dhafā'ir): braids of hair that are woven together
المسترسل (al-mustarsil): the hanging hair that is not reached by water if the hair is braided; this part is waived for women but not men
تضفير الشعر (tadfīr al-sha'r): the act of braiding hair
العلويون (al-'alawiyyūn): Those descended from 'Ali ibn Abi Talib; some specify those from other than Fatima.
الأتراك (al-atrāk): The Turks, a people known for certain customs including braiding hair.
الأحوط (al-ahwaṭ): the cautious or more precautionary view

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the reason women are exempt from washing the inner part of their braids in ghusl?
A) Water does not need to reach that part for anyone.
B) Braiding is customary for women and undoing it would cause hardship.
C) The hadith of Umm Salamah makes it permissible for both men and women.
D) Women are not required to wash their hair at all in ghusl.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, men must undo braids in ghusl to ensure water reaches every part of the hair; women are exempt due to custom, but the relied-upon view extends the obligation even to men with customarily braided hair.

Women's Braids in Ghusl: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on women's braids in ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on women's braided hair in ghusl and explain the evidence for the non-obligation of wetting and squeezing.

Core Idea
Pouring water over the head suffices for braided hair; undoing or squeezing is not required.

This passage addresses a practical concern for Muslim women: how to perform ghusl with braided hair. The Hanafi school, relying on the majority narrations of the hadith of Umm Salamah, holds that it is not obligatory to undo or squeeze the braids; merely pouring water over the head ensuring it reaches the roots is sufficient. However, there is an internal disagreement within the school, which this lesson clarifies.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What exactly is being ruled upon? The act is ghusl for a woman with braided hair. The ruling from the matn is that she does not need to wet or squeeze the braids.
2
Locate the dalil
What proof does the author cite? The hadith of Umm Salamah (may Allah be pleased with her) in which the Prophet (peace be upon him) said something about pouring water. The majority narrations are silent about squeezing.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
How exactly does the dalil prove the ruling? The majority narrations indicate that pouring water on the head is sufficient, as long as water reaches the roots. The absence of the command to squeeze implies it is not required.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Within the Hanafi school, some scholars (al-Baqqali and a narration from Abu Hanifa via Hasan) hold that wetting and squeezing the braids is obligatory, based on a version of the hadith that includes 'squeeze your braids'.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
Which position is relied upon? The correct view (al-ashahh) as stated in al-Hidayah and others is that wetting and squeezing is not obligatory, because the more authentic and numerous narrations are silent on squeezing.
The mufta bihi is non-obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقوله : ولا بلها ، قال بعض مشايخنا له : تبل ذوائبها وتعصرها، لكنَّ الأصح عدم وجوبه ، وهذا إذا كانت مفتولة ، أما إذا كانت منقوضةً يجب إيصال الماء إلى أثناء الشعر كما في اللحية ؛ لعدم الحرج. [٢] قوله : قال بعض المشايخ ) : تبل ؛ أي يجب عليها مع إيصال الماء إلى الأصول، بل الذوائب وعصرها كذا نقله في «المجتبى» عن البقالي، ورواه الحسن عن أبي حنيفة كما في «التاتارخانية». وسنده ما ورد في بعض رواياتِ حديث أم سلمة رضي الله عنها قال رسول الله : واغمزي قرونك عند كلّ حفنة أخرجه أبو داود، لكن أكثر الروايات ساكتة عنه، ومفيدة لكفاية الصب على الرأس ووصول الماء إلى أصول الشعور، فمن ثم صحح في «الهداية» وغيره : عدم وجوبه.
And his saying: And she does not wet it. Some of our scholars said: She should wet her locks and squeeze them, but the correct view is that it is not obligatory. And this is if it is plaited; but if it is undone, then it is obligatory to make water reach the inner hair as in the beard, due to the absence of hardship. [2] His saying: Some scholars said: She should wet; meaning it is obligatory for her, along with making water reach the roots, to wet the locks and squeeze them. Thus it was transmitted in al-Mujtaba from al-Baqqali, and al-Hasan narrated it from Abu Hanifa as in al-Tatarkhaniyya. Its chain is what was reported in some transmissions of the hadith of Umm Salamah, may Allah be pleased with her: the Messenger of Allah said: "And squeeze your braids at every handful" (narrated by Abu Dawud). But most of the transmissions are silent about it, and indicate the sufficiency of pouring water on the head and water reaching the roots of the hair. Therefore, it was considered correct in al-Hidayah and others that it is not obligatory.
Source pages p. 352

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the commentary on the matn, specifically addressing the phrase 'and she does not wet it' (the matn says that a woman does not need to wet her braids). The author explains the correct Hanafi ruling for women regarding braided hair in ghusl. The passage states that the correct view (al-ashahh) is that it is not obligatory for a woman to wet and squeeze her braids (the locks of hair) after pouring water over her head. This applies when the hair is braided (maftūlah); if the hair is undone (manqūḍah), then she must ensure water reaches the inner hair, just as with a man's beard, because there is no hardship. The author then mentions a contrary opinion from some scholars (including al-Baqqali and a narration from Abu Hanifa via Hasan) that she must wet and squeeze her braids. The basis for this opinion is a version of the hadith of Umm Salamah where the Prophet said 'and squeeze your braids at every handful' (narrated by Abu Dawud). However, the majority of the transmissions of this hadith are silent about squeezing, and indicate only that pouring water over the head is sufficient, as long as the water reaches the roots. Therefore, the relied-upon view, as confirmed in al-Hidayah and other works, is that wetting and squeezing is not obligatory. The passage thus resolves the internal Hanafi difference, preferring the majority view that women are not required to do extra actions beyond pouring water.

Key Terms
الذوائب (al-dhawā'ib): braids or locks of hair that hang down; specifically the plaits of a woman's hair
تبل (taballu): to wet or moisten the hair with water
تعصر (ta'sur): to squeeze or wring out water from the hair
منقوضة (manqūḍah): hair that is undone, unbraided, or loose

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) She must undo the braids entirely
B) She must wet and squeeze the braids after pouring water
C) She only needs to pour three handfuls of water over her head, ensuring water reaches the roots
D) She must wash each strand individually

Key Takeaway
In ghusl, braided hair does not require extra wetting or squeezing; pouring water over the head suffices.

Ruling on Undoing Braids During Ghusl
Ruling on braided hair in ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling regarding undoing braids during ghusl and explain the evidence from the Hadith of Umm Salamah.

Core Idea
Women need not undo braids for ghusl; it suffices that water reaches the scalp.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a common question for women: must one undo braided hair when performing ghusl? The Hanafi school teaches that undoing braids is not obligatory, though it is a recommended precaution. The ruling is based on a balance between the general obligation to wash all parts of the body and the specific allowance from the hadith of Umm Salamah.

Guided Path
1
State the act and ruling
Identify what is being ruled (undoing braids during ghusl) and the hukm: it is not obligatory (wajib) but recommended (mustahabb).
The key distinction is between braided (maftula) and undone (manquda) hair.
2
Identify the dalil
Locate the primary evidence: the hadith of Umm Salamah (radiyallahu anha) where the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) instructed her to pour water over her head without ordering her to undo her braids.
This hadith is ahad, thus zanni al-thubut and zanni al-dalalah.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Understand how the hadith proves the ruling: the Prophet's instruction did not include undoing, indicating that reaching the roots suffices without unbraiding.
The absence of a command implies permissibility of non-undoing.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Some Hanafi scholars (e.g., al-Baqqali) held it obligatory to wet and wring the locks. The author mentions the precautionary view (al-ihtiyat) that it is recommended.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab dispute.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position is non-obligation, as declared by Al-Marghinani in Al-Hidayah and others. The precaution is recommended but not required.
Authority weight: zahir al-riwayah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأتراك لا يجب، والأحوط أن يجب. وقوله : ولا بلها ، قال بعض مشايخنا : تبل ذوائبها وتعصرها، لكن الأصح عدم وجوبه، وهذا إذا كانت مفتولة، أما إذا كانت منقوضةً يجب إيصال الماء إلى أثناء الشعر كما في اللحية؛ لعدم الحرج. قوله : والأحوط أن يجب؛ لأن الأحاديث دلت على افتراض سائر أجزاء البدن الظاهرة والشعر المسترسل داخل فيها، كيف لا وقد أمر الله في الغسل بما يفيد المبالغة، ومن ثم افترض الاستنشاق والتمضمض، ولولا حديث أم سلمة رضي الله عنها وغيره من الأحاديث الدالة على سقوط غسل المسترسل، ووجوب نقض الذوائب من النساء لكان ذلك فرضاً عليهن أيضاً.
Braids (or locks) are not obligatory, and the precautionary view is that it is obligatory. And his saying: 'and do not wet it', some of our scholars said: she should wet her locks and wring them, but the correct view is that it is not obligatory. This is if they are braided. But if they are undone, it is obligatory to deliver water into the hair, just like the beard, because there is no hardship. His saying: 'and the precautionary view is that it is obligatory' because the hadiths indicate the obligation (to wash) all apparent parts of the body, and flowing hair is included in them. Indeed, Allah commanded in ghusl what implies thoroughness, and for that reason rinsing the mouth and nose are obligatory. And were it not for the hadith of Umm Salamah, may Allah be pleased with her, and other hadiths indicating the dropping of washing flowing hair and the obligation of undoing locks for women, it would have been obligatory upon them as well.
Source pages p. 353

Commentary
This passage deals with the ruling on washing braided hair during ghusl. The author states that it is not obligatory to undo the braids, although caution (ihtiyat) suggests it is recommended. The commentary explains that if the hair is braided, one need not undo it; rather, it suffices to pour water over the head and let it reach the roots. However, if the hair is unbraided, water must reach every strand, similar to the beard. The evidence comes from the hadith of Umm Salamah where the Prophet (peace be upon him) instructed her to pour water over her head without explicitly requiring her to undo her braids. This indicates that the obligation is to ensure water reaches the scalp, not necessarily to undo plaits. The passage also mentions the principle that any part of the body that is not flowing (mustarshil) is included in the general obligation, but the specific hadith exempts women from undoing their heavy locks due to hardship. Thus, the relied-upon position is non-obligation.

Key Terms
الأتراك (al-atrak): Braids or plaited locks of hair on a woman's head.
المفتولة (al-maftula): Braided or twisted hair, in the context of hair that is plaited.
المنقوضة (al-manquda): Undone or unbraided hair.
الذوائب (al-dhawa'ib): Locks or bundles of hair, specifically the hanging parts.
المسترسل (al-mustarshil): Flowing or hanging hair that is not bound up.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) She does not need to undo the braids; pouring water over the head suffices.
C) She must cut her braids if water cannot reach the roots.
D) She must wash each strand individually.

Key Takeaway
Women need not undo braids for ghusl; it suffices that water reaches the scalp, based on the hadith of Umm Salamah.

Definition of the Cause of Ghusl: Emission of Mani with Gush and Desire
Defining the cause of ghusl and distinguishing fluids

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the cause of ghusl (mujib) according to the Hanafi school, identify the conditions of emission and desire, explain the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf regarding gush, and distinguish maniy from madhi and wadi.

Core Idea
Ghusl is wajib only upon emission of maniy with desire; gush is not required per the mufta bihi, and no ghusl for madhi or wadi.

This passage defines the cause (mujib) of ghusl as the emission of maniy with gush and desire. It then distinguishes three types of fluids and presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement about whether gush is a condition. Understanding these details is crucial for correctly determining when ghusl is obligatory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The text states that the cause (mujib) of ghusl is the emission of maniy with gush and desire. Note that ghusl becomes wajib upon this occurrence. Identify the hukm level: wajib.
2
Dissect the key terms
Analyze the terms 'inzal' (emission/exit to outside), 'maniy' (semen including female), 'dafq' (gush), and 'shahwa' (desire). Note the characteristics of maniy: exit with desire, lethargy, smell like pollen, and gush. For women: yellow and thin.
3
Distinguish maniy from madhi and wadi
Madhi is thin, exits with weak desire without gush, requires only wudu. Wadi is white, turbid, exits after urination, also only wudu. Ghusl is only for maniy with desire.
Memorize the three fluids and their rulings.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on gush
Abu Yusuf requires dafq (gush) as a condition for ghusl. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani only require exit with desire. The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is that gush is not necessary.
Later authorities like al-Shurnbulali, Mulla Khusru, and the authors of al-Tanwir and al-Durr al-Mukhtar prefer the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf
If maniy exits with desire but without gush, Abu Yusuf says no ghusl; the majority says ghusl is wajib. The practical fatwa follows the majority view.
Always check the mufta bihi for fatwa.

وموجبه: إنزال مني ذي دفق وشهوة. قوله: وموجبه; بكسر الجيم; أي ما يوجب الغسل، وكان المناسب بالقياس إلى ما ذكره في بحث الوضوء وناقضه، وإن كان ما لهما واحداً، فإن كل حدث ناقض لما سبق، وموجب لما يأتي. قوله: إنزال; إفعال من النزالة بالضم، وهي: ماء الرجل، يقال: أنزل الرجل صار ذا نزالة، والمراد هاهنا الخروج بقرينة إضافته إلى المني، قاله البرجندي: ويشترط فيه الخروج إلى خارج البدن أو ما في حكمه كالفرج الخارج، والقلفة على قول، فما دام في قصبة الذكر أو الفرج الداخل لا يجب الغسل، قال في الغنية. قوله: مني; بفتح الميم وكسر النون وتشديد الياء آخر الحروف – وهو عام يشمل ماء الرجل وماء المرأة، وله خواص يعرف بها: أحدهما: الخروج بشهوة مع الفتور عقبه. الثانية: الرائحة كرائحة الطلع. الثالثة: الخروج بدفق ودفعات، هذا كله في مني الرجل. وأما مني المرأة فهو أصفر رقيق، كذا قال النووي. وأما المذي: وهو الماء الرقيق الذي يخرج عند الشهوة الضعيفة بالملاعبة ونحوها من غير دفق، فموجب للوضوء لا للغسل. والودي: وهو ماء أبيض كدر لا رائحة له يخرج بعد البول، فموجب للوضوء لا للغسل. قوله: ذي دفق; بالفتح - أي سيلان بسرعة، وصب بشدة، وهو وصف.
صورة إنزال المني على دفق وشهوة عند الانفصال موجب للغسل عند الإمام وصاحبيه، وشرط الدفق إنما هو عند أبي يوسف الله لا عندهما، فلم يشترطا إلا الانفصال عن شهوة; لذا لم يقيد الإنزال بالدفق الشرنبلالي الله في «المراقي» (ص 131)، وملا خسرو في «الغرر» (1: 19)، وصاحب التنوير (1: 109)، وتبعه صاحب (الدر المختار) (1: 109)، فقال الشرنبلالي: إذا انفصل عن مقره بشهوة.
And its cause: the emission of maniy with gush and desire. His saying: 'and its cause' — with kasra on the jim — meaning that which obligates ghusl. And it would be appropriate by analogy to what he mentioned in the chapter of wudu and its nullifiers, even though both are one in that every event nullifies what preceded and obligates what comes. His saying: 'emission' — a verbal noun from 'al-nuzala' (with damm), which is the water of a man. It is said: 'the man emitted' meaning he became possessing nuzala. What is intended here is 'exit', indicated by its addition to maniy. Al-Birjandi said: it is a condition that it exits to the outside of the body or what is in its ruling like the external genitals, and the prepuce according to one view. As long as it is in the urethra or internal genitals, ghusl is not obligatory, as stated in al-Ghunya. His saying: 'maniy' — with fatha on the mim and kasra on the nun and shadda on the ya — and it is general, including the water of man and woman. It has characteristics by which it is known: one: exiting with desire followed by lethargy. Second: smell like the pollen of palm. Third: exiting with gush and spurts. All of this is for the man's maniy. As for the woman's maniy, it is yellow and thin, so said al-Nawawi. As for madhi: it is thin water that comes out with weak desire during foreplay and the like, without gush, and it requires wudu, not ghusl. And wadi: it is white, turbid water with no smell, exiting after urination, and it requires wudu, not ghusl. His saying: 'with gush' — with fatha — meaning rapid flow and forceful pouring, and it is a description.
The form of emitting maniy with gush and desire upon separation (from the body) is a cause for ghusl according to Imam Abu Hanifa and his two companions. However, the condition of gush is only according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, not the other two, so they only stipulate separation with desire. Hence, al-Shurnbulali in al-Maraqi, Mulla Khusru in al-Ghurar, and the author of al-Tanwir, followed by the author of al-Durr al-Mukhtar, did not restrict emission to gush, saying: if it separates from its place with desire (ghusl is obligatory).
Source pages p. 354

Commentary
This passage defines the cause (mujib) of ghusl: the emission of maniy accompanied by gush (dafq) and desire (shahwa). The commentary dissects each term. 'Inzal' means the exit of semen to the outside; if it remains inside, no ghusl is required. 'Maniy' includes both male and female fluids, but the characteristics listed are for men. The text then distinguishes between maniy, madhi (pre-ejaculatory fluid), and wadi (post-urine fluid). Only maniy requires ghusl; madhi and wadi require only wudu. The footnote clarifies an intra-Hanafi disagreement: Abu Yusuf required gush (dafq) as a condition, while Abu Hanifa and Muhammad only required exit with desire. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that gush is not a necessary condition; separation with desire is sufficient. This is the view of later Hanafi scholars like al-Shurnbulali, Mulla Khusru, and the authors of al-Tanwir and al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

Key Terms
إنزال (inzal): Emission of maniy (semen) from the body, specifically the act of its exit.
مني (maniy): Seminal fluid, characterized by exit with desire, smell like pollen, and gush/spurts for men; for women it is yellow and thin.
دفق (dafq): Gush, rapid flow, forceful pouring of fluid.
شهوة (shahwa): Sexual desire or excitement, which accompanies the emission of maniy.
مذي (madhi): Pre-ejaculatory fluid, thin, exiting with weak desire without gush.
ودي (wadi): Post-urine fluid, white, turbid, no odor, exiting after urination.
نزالة (nuzala): Another term for maniy, meaning the water of a man.

Ghusl is wajib when maniy exits with desire; gush is not required, and madhi/wadi only require wudu.

Cause of Ghusl: The Condition of Lust (Shahwa) in the Hanafi School
Defining the cause of ghusl and contrasting Hanafi and Shafi'i views

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school conditions ghusl on lust, identify the intra-Hanafi difference regarding gushing flow, and contrast the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Without lust, emission of semen does not obligate ghusl — the cause is the sexual desire, not the fluid itself.

This passage from the sharh of al-Wiqayah clarifies the cause of ghusl. It defines the key terms and contrasts the Hanafi view with that of Imam al-Shafi'i. It also resolves an internal Hanafi difference about whether gushing flow is required. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly determining when ghusl becomes fard.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling and its cause
The matn states: 'And its cause: emission of semen having gushing and lust.' Identify that the act is ghusl (obligatory bath) and its cause is emission of semen (maniy) with two attributes.
The cause is not mere emission but emission qualified by lust and flow.
2
Distinguish the two conditions: lust (shahwa) and gushing (dafaq)
Note that the text mentions both. However, only lust is agreed upon by all Hanafi imams. Gushing is required by Abu Yusuf but not by Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Understand that the matn includes it to cover the full view.
The intra-Hanafi difference: Abu Yusuf requires gushing at exit; the majority require only lust at separation from its place.
3
Contrast with the Shafi'i position
The commentary explicitly states 'contrary to al-Shafi'i' – the Shafi'i school obligates ghusl from any emission of semen, whether with or without lust. This is a clear inter-madhhab dispute.
The thamarah al-khilaf: in cases of emission without desire (e.g., due to illness), Hanafis say no ghusl, Shafi'is say ghusl.
4
Apply the distinction between maniy, madhy, and wady
The passage defines three fluids: maniy (requires ghusl with lust), madhy (pre-ejaculate, requires wudu), wady (post-urine, requires wudu). This classification is crucial for practical taharah.
Only maniy with lust triggers janabah; madhy and wady are minor impurities.
5
Determine the mufta bihi position
The commentary indicates that the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (lust sufficient, flow not required) is relied upon. The matn's inclusion of both conditions is for completeness.
No difficulty in accepting the view without flow, implying it is the operative fatwa.

[4] قوله : ذي دفق - بالفتح - أي سيلان بسرعة، وصب بشدة، وهو وصف عند الانفصال حتى لو أنزل بلا شهوة لا يجب الغسلُ عندنا، خلافاً للشافعي" يوجد في مني الرجل والمرأة كليهما كما اختاره في «غاية البيان»، و«جامع الرموز، و «معالم التنزيل»، وغيرها. ومنهم من أنكر الدفق في مَنِي المرأة، وليس بصحيح كما بسطته في «السعاية». فإن قلت : ذكر الدفق والشهوة كليهما إنّما يستقيم على قول أبي يوسف له لاشتراطه الدفق والشهوة عند الخروج لا على قولهما، فإنهما لم يشترطا الدفق عند الخروج حتى قالا يجبُ الغُسلِ إذا زالَ المَنِي عن مكانه بشهوة، ثم سكنت الشهوة وخرج من غير دفق. قلت: هو مستقيم على قول الكلّ، فإنه إذا خرج المني بشهوة ودفق يجبُ الغُسِلُ عند الكل، غاية الأمر بعض الموجبات على رأيهما، وهو خروج المني بشهوة عند الانفصال من دون دفق ولا مضائقة فيه.
