Ghusl for a Female Convert: The Ruling and the Janabah Analogy
Ruling and intra-Hanafi analogy on ghusl for female convert

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for a female convert whose menstruation ended before conversion, and explain the distinction from janabah.

Core Idea
A cause that is not continuous does not persist after conversion; janabah persists, cessation does not.

This lesson examines a specific case in taharah: a woman converts to Islam after her menstruation has ended. Does she need to perform ghusl? The Hanafi school says no, based on a key distinction between continuous and non-continuous causes. We will trace the reasoning and see how it differs from the case of janabah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is converting to Islam after menstruation ends. The ruling is that ghusl is not obligatory.
2
Understand the reasoning
The cause (cessation of blood) occurred during disbelief; she was not commanded then, and the cause is not continuous, so it does not apply after conversion.
The passage states: 'إذ وقت الانقطاع كانت كافرة، وهي غير مأمورة بالشرائع عندنا'.
3
Contrast with janabah
Janabah is a continuous state that persists after conversion, so ghusl is required in that case.
The text says: 'بخلاف ما إذا أجنبت الكافرة، ثُمَّ أسلمت، حيث يجب عليها غُسل الجنابة'.
4
Locate the Quranic evidence
Quran 2:222 is cited showing that purification (ghusl) is a condition for intercourse, but the cessation occurred before Islam.
The verse is used to establish the obligation of ghusl after menstruation, but not for the convert.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أسلمت لا يلزمها الاغتسال ؛ إذ وقت الانقطاع كانت كافرة، وهي غير مأمورة بالشرائع عندنا ، ومتى أسلمت لم يوجد السبب ، وهو الانقطاع ، بخلاف ما إذا أجنبت الكافرة، ثُمَّ أسلمت، حيث يجب عليها غُسل الجنابة ؛ لأنَّ الجنابة أمر مستمر، فتكون جنبا بعد الإسلام
فَاعْتَزِلُوا النِّسَاءَ فِي الْمَحِيضِ ) : أي عن الوطء، وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ ) بالوطء حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ ) () ، قُرى بتشديد الطاء والهاء فمعناه : حتى يغتسلن، وقرئ بسكون الطاء وضم الهاء مخففاً(۲) فمعناه : حتى ينقطع دم حيضهن، كذا ذكره المفسرون، فهذه الآية دلت على حرمة الوطء قبل الاغتسال.
ومن المعلوم أن الوطء تصرف واقع في ملكه ، فلو كان الاغتسال جائزاً ومستحباً لم يمنع الزوج من الوطء، فعلم أنه واجب ، وبهذا ظهر وجه اختيار قراءة التشديد ، فإنّه على قراءة التخفيف يكون مفسّراً بانقطاع الدم، فلا يدل على وجوب الغسل. فإن قلت : فأين ذكر النفاس. قلت : فهم من هذه الآية ضمناً بقوله : هُوَ أذى ، فإن دم الحيض والنفاس مشتركاً في كونه أذى. [1] قوله : عندنا ؛ ظاهره أنه متفق عليه بين أصحابنا، وليس كذلك، قال ابن الهمام في «تحرير الأصول»: «عدم كون الكفار مكلفين بالفروع مذهب مشايخ سمرقند ، ومن عداهم متَّفقون على التكليف بها ، وإنّما اختلفوا في أنه في حق الاعتقاد فقط ، أو الاعتقاد والعمل كليهما ، فقال البخاريون بالأوّل، فعندهم يعاقب الكفار على ترك الاعتقاد بها ، وعلى ترك الاعتقادِ بالإيمان وتركه ، وقال العراقيون بالثاني كالشافعية».
والانقطاع غير مستمر فافترقا.
[1] قوله : فافترقا ؛ محصل الفرق الذي ذكره تبعاً لما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي في «شرح السير الكبير» وصاحب «الذخيرة» و«قاضي خان» وغيرهم: إنّ موجب غسل الجنابة هو الجنابة، وهو مستدامة إلى زمان الغسل، فإذا أجنبت الكافرة، ثمَّ أسلمت فجنابتها باقية، بقاؤها في الإسلام كإنشائها، فيجب عليها الغُسل. وأما غسل الحيض ونحوه فسببه انقطاع الدم، وهو أمر غير مستمر، فإذا وجد حال الكفر لم يبق في الإسلام، فلا يجب الغسل به لا في الإسلام؛ لعدم وجوده، ولا قبله ؛ لعدم كون الكفار مخاطبين بالفروع عملاً.
وفيه نظر من وجوه :
الأول : إن كون الكفّار غير مخاطبين ليس أمراً إجماعياً، بل هو أمر خلافي بين أصحابنا أيضاً كما مرّ، فمن قال من أصحابنا بكونهم مخاطبين لا يظهر الفرق على مذهبه بين الجنابة والانقطاع، بل يجب الغُسل في كل منهما حال الكفر، ويبقى بعد الإسلام.
الثاني : إنّ الغُسل سبب وجوبه في الحقيقة إنّما هو إرادة الصلاة وما يتبعها لا الانقطاع ولا الجنابة، فيجبُ الغُسلُ في كلتا الصورتين بعد الإسلام.
الثالث : إنّ الجنابة حدوثها أيضاً بإنزال المني أو غيبوبة الحشفة آني غير مستمر ، وإنما المستمر حكمه وأثره ، وكذا الانقطاع ، وإن كان نفسه غير مستمر، لكن أثره باق ما لم يغتسل، فما وجه اعتبار البقاء في أحدهما والابتداء في الآخر فافهم.
Then she becomes Muslim: ghusl is not obligatory for her, because at the time of cessation she was a disbeliever, and she is not commanded with the religious laws according to us. And when she becomes Muslim, the cause (cessation) is not found. Unlike if the disbelieving woman becomes junub (impure) then becomes Muslim, ghusl from janabah is obligatory upon her, because janabah is a continuous matter, so she remains junub after Islam. ... And cessation is not continuous, so they differ. [1] His statement: 'so they differ'; the gist of the difference that he mentioned following what Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir and the author of al-Dhakhira and Qadi Khan and others: The cause of ghusl from janabah is janabah itself, and it is continuous until the time of ghusl. So if a disbelieving woman becomes junub, then becomes Muslim, her janabah remains; its continuation in Islam is like its inception, so ghusl is obligatory upon her. As for ghusl from menstruation and the like, its cause is the cessation of blood, which is not continuous. So if it occurs during disbelief, it does not remain in Islam, so ghusl is not obligatory due to it in Islam—due to its non-existence—nor before it, due to the fact that disbelievers are not addressed with the detailed rulings in practice. And there is critique from several angles: First, that disbelievers are not addressed is not a matter of consensus; it is a point of disagreement among our companions as well, as previously. So whoever among our companions holds that they are addressed does not see a difference between janabah and cessation; rather, ghusl would be obligatory in both cases during disbelief and after Islam. Second, the cause of obligation of ghusl in reality is the intention to pray and what follows it, not cessation nor janabah; so ghusl would be obligatory in both cases after Islam. Third, the occurrence of janabah itself is also by ejaculation or insertion of the glans, which is momentary and not continuous; rather, what is continuous is its ruling and effect. Similarly, cessation, though itself not continuous, its effect remains until one performs ghusl. So what is the basis for considering continuity in one and inception in the other? Understand.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether a woman who converts to Islam after her menstruation has ended must perform ghusl. The main text states that ghusl is not required because the cause (cessation of blood) occurred while she was a disbeliever, and according to the Samarkand school, disbelievers are not addressed by the detailed rulings (furu'). The commentator, however, notes that this view is not unanimous among Hanafis. He cites Ibn al-Humam from Tahrir al-Usul, stating that the Samarkand school holds that disbelievers are not addressed by the detailed rulings, while the Bukhari and Iraqi schools (comparable to the Shafi'i position) hold that they are addressed, either in terms of belief only (Bukhari) or both belief and action (Iraqi). The commentator then elaborates the difference between janabah and menstruation: janabah is a continuous state, so it persists after conversion, necessitating ghusl; cessation is momentary, so it does not persist. However, he raises three objections: (1) the disagreement on taklif al-kuffar means the distinction collapses for those who hold they are addressed; (2) the true cause of ghusl is the intention to pray, not cessation or janabah, so ghusl would be required in both cases; (3) both janabah and cessation have momentary occurrences but continuous effects, so the distinction seems arbitrary. The passage ends with 'فافهم' (understand), implying the matter is deep and requires contemplation. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position appears to be that of the main text, as the commentary critiques the criticism.

Key Terms
الجنابة (al-janabah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, caused by sexual ejaculation or intercourse
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstrual blood that prevents prayer, fasting, and intercourse
الشرائع (al-shara'i'): Detailed religious laws (furu') such as salah, fasting, etc.
تكليف الكفار بالفروع (taklif al-kuffar bil-furu'): Whether disbelievers are legally obligated to perform the detailed rulings of Islam

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school (Samarkand view) not require ghusl for a female convert whose menstruation ended before conversion?
A) Because disbelievers are never required to perform ghusl.
B) Because the cause (cessation) is not continuous and occurred during disbelief.
C) Because the woman can perform tayammum instead.
D) Because menstruation does not require ghusl in the Hanafi school.

Key Takeaway
Ghusl after menstruation is wajib, but for a convert it is not required because the cause occurred during disbelief and is not continuous.

Taklif al-Kuffar bil-Furu': The Intra-Hanafi Dispute and Its Impact on Ghusl
Intra-Hanafi usul dispute on taklif al-kuffar and its fiqh application

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the three positions within the Hanafi school regarding whether disbelievers are addressed by detailed rulings, and evaluate the commentator's three critiques of the main ruling.

Core Idea
The Samarkand school says no; the Bukhara school says yes (in belief); the Iraqi school says yes (in action) – this disagreement affects many fiqh rulings.

The previous lesson established the ruling that a female convert does not need ghusl after menstrual cessation. However, this ruling depends on a deeper usul principle: whether disbelievers are obligated by the detailed rulings (furu') of Islam. This lesson explores the three Hanafi positions on this issue and the commentator's objections to the main ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul principle
Taklif al-kuffar bil-furu': are disbelievers addressed by the detailed laws?
This principle underlies the ruling.
2
Map the three Hanafi positions
Samarkand: no; Bukhara: yes for belief only; Iraq: yes for belief and action.
Reference: Ibn al-Humam in Tahrir al-Usul as cited in the commentary.
3
Apply to the ghusl case
For Samarkand, the cause during kufr is not binding; for others, it is binding.
This determines the thamarah al-khilaf.
4
Analyze the commentator's three critiques
Each critique challenges the consistency or basis of the main ruling: (1) lack of consensus on taklif, (2) true cause is intention to pray, (3) both janabah and cessation have momentary occurrence but continuous effect.
The critiques are from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya.
5
Evaluate the force of the critiques
The main text upholds the Samarkand view; the critiques are noted but not adopted as mufta bihi.
The final 'فافهم' suggests deep contemplation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم أسلمت لا يلزمها الاغتسال ؛ إذ وقت الانقطاع كانت كافرة، وهي غير مأمورة بالشرائع عندنا ، ومتى أسلمت لم يوجد السبب ، وهو الانقطاع ، بخلاف ما إذا أجنبت الكافرة، ثُمَّ أسلمت، حيث يجب عليها غُسل الجنابة ؛ لأنَّ الجنابة أمر مستمر، فتكون جنبا بعد الإسلام
فَاعْتَزِلُوا النِّسَاءَ فِي الْمَحِيضِ ) : أي عن الوطء، وَلَا تَقْرَبُوهُنَّ ) بالوطء حَتَّى يَطْهُرْنَ ) () ، قُرى بتشديد الطاء والهاء فمعناه : حتى يغتسلن، وقرئ بسكون الطاء وضم الهاء مخففاً(۲) فمعناه : حتى ينقطع دم حيضهن، كذا ذكره المفسرون، فهذه الآية دلت على حرمة الوطء قبل الاغتسال.
ومن المعلوم أن الوطء تصرف واقع في ملكه ، فلو كان الاغتسال جائزاً ومستحباً لم يمنع الزوج من الوطء، فعلم أنه واجب ، وبهذا ظهر وجه اختيار قراءة التشديد ، فإنّه على قراءة التخفيف يكون مفسّراً بانقطاع الدم، فلا يدل على وجوب الغسل. فإن قلت : فأين ذكر النفاس. قلت : فهم من هذه الآية ضمناً بقوله : هُوَ أذى ، فإن دم الحيض والنفاس مشتركاً في كونه أذى. [1] قوله : عندنا ؛ ظاهره أنه متفق عليه بين أصحابنا، وليس كذلك، قال ابن الهمام في «تحرير الأصول»: «عدم كون الكفار مكلفين بالفروع مذهب مشايخ سمرقند ، ومن عداهم متَّفقون على التكليف بها ، وإنّما اختلفوا في أنه في حق الاعتقاد فقط ، أو الاعتقاد والعمل كليهما ، فقال البخاريون بالأوّل، فعندهم يعاقب الكفار على ترك الاعتقاد بها ، وعلى ترك الاعتقادِ بالإيمان وتركه ، وقال العراقيون بالثاني كالشافعية».
والانقطاع غير مستمر فافترقا.
[1] قوله : فافترقا ؛ محصل الفرق الذي ذكره تبعاً لما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي في «شرح السير الكبير» وصاحب «الذخيرة» و«قاضي خان» وغيرهم: إنّ موجب غسل الجنابة هو الجنابة، وهو مستدامة إلى زمان الغسل، فإذا أجنبت الكافرة، ثمَّ أسلمت فجنابتها باقية، بقاؤها في الإسلام كإنشائها، فيجب عليها الغُسل. وأما غسل الحيض ونحوه فسببه انقطاع الدم، وهو أمر غير مستمر، فإذا وجد حال الكفر لم يبق في الإسلام، فلا يجب الغسل به لا في الإسلام؛ لعدم وجوده، ولا قبله ؛ لعدم كون الكفار مخاطبين بالفروع عملاً.
وفيه نظر من وجوه :
الأول : إن كون الكفّار غير مخاطبين ليس أمراً إجماعياً، بل هو أمر خلافي بين أصحابنا أيضاً كما مرّ، فمن قال من أصحابنا بكونهم مخاطبين لا يظهر الفرق على مذهبه بين الجنابة والانقطاع، بل يجب الغُسل في كل منهما حال الكفر، ويبقى بعد الإسلام.
الثاني : إنّ الغُسل سبب وجوبه في الحقيقة إنّما هو إرادة الصلاة وما يتبعها لا الانقطاع ولا الجنابة، فيجبُ الغُسلُ في كلتا الصورتين بعد الإسلام.
الثالث : إنّ الجنابة حدوثها أيضاً بإنزال المني أو غيبوبة الحشفة آني غير مستمر ، وإنما المستمر حكمه وأثره ، وكذا الانقطاع ، وإن كان نفسه غير مستمر، لكن أثره باق ما لم يغتسل، فما وجه اعتبار البقاء في أحدهما والابتداء في الآخر فافهم.
Then she becomes Muslim: ghusl is not obligatory for her, because at the time of cessation she was a disbeliever, and she is not commanded with the religious laws according to us. And when she becomes Muslim, the cause (cessation) is not found. Unlike if the disbelieving woman becomes junub (impure) then becomes Muslim, ghusl from janabah is obligatory upon her, because janabah is a continuous matter, so she remains junub after Islam. ... And cessation is not continuous, so they differ. [1] His statement: 'so they differ'; the gist of the difference that he mentioned following what Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir and the author of al-Dhakhira and Qadi Khan and others: The cause of ghusl from janabah is janabah itself, and it is continuous until the time of ghusl. So if a disbelieving woman becomes junub, then becomes Muslim, her janabah remains; its continuation in Islam is like its inception, so ghusl is obligatory upon her. As for ghusl from menstruation and the like, its cause is the cessation of blood, which is not continuous. So if it occurs during disbelief, it does not remain in Islam, so ghusl is not obligatory due to it in Islam—due to its non-existence—nor before it, due to the fact that disbelievers are not addressed with the detailed rulings in practice. And there is critique from several angles: First, that disbelievers are not addressed is not a matter of consensus; it is a point of disagreement among our companions as well, as previously. So whoever among our companions holds that they are addressed does not see a difference between janabah and cessation; rather, ghusl would be obligatory in both cases during disbelief and after Islam. Second, the cause of obligation of ghusl in reality is the intention to pray and what follows it, not cessation nor janabah; so ghusl would be obligatory in both cases after Islam. Third, the occurrence of janabah itself is also by ejaculation or insertion of the glans, which is momentary and not continuous; rather, what is continuous is its ruling and effect. Similarly, cessation, though itself not continuous, its effect remains until one performs ghusl. So what is the basis for considering continuity in one and inception in the other? Understand.
Source pages p. 360

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning whether a woman who converts to Islam after her menstruation has ended must perform ghusl. The main text states that ghusl is not required because the cause (cessation of blood) occurred while she was a disbeliever, and according to the Samarkand school, disbelievers are not addressed by the detailed rulings (furu'). The commentator, however, notes that this view is not unanimous among Hanafis. He cites Ibn al-Humam from Tahrir al-Usul, stating that the Samarkand school holds that disbelievers are not addressed by the detailed rulings, while the Bukhari and Iraqi schools (comparable to the Shafi'i position) hold that they are addressed, either in terms of belief only (Bukhari) or both belief and action (Iraqi). The commentator then elaborates the difference between janabah and menstruation: janabah is a continuous state, so it persists after conversion, necessitating ghusl; cessation is momentary, so it does not persist. However, he raises three objections: (1) the disagreement on taklif al-kuffar means the distinction collapses for those who hold they are addressed; (2) the true cause of ghusl is the intention to pray, not cessation or janabah, so ghusl would be required in both cases; (3) both janabah and cessation have momentary occurrences but continuous effects, so the distinction seems arbitrary. The passage ends with 'فافهم' (understand), implying the matter is deep and requires contemplation. The relied-upon (mufta bihi) position appears to be that of the main text, as the commentary critiques the criticism.

Key Terms
تكليف الكفار بالفروع (taklif al-kuffar bil-furu'): Whether disbelievers are legally obligated to perform the detailed rulings of Islam
الجنابة (al-janabah): State of major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, caused by sexual ejaculation or intercourse
الحيض (al-hayd): Menstrual blood that prevents prayer, fasting, and intercourse
الشرائع (al-shara'i'): Detailed religious laws (furu') such as salah, fasting, etc.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Hanafi school holds that disbelievers are addressed in both belief and action?
A) Samarkand school
B) Bukhara school
C) Iraqi school
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has internal differences on taklif al-kuffar bil-furu', affecting rulings for converts; the Samarkand view is the relied-upon.

Ghusl for Bestiality Without Ejaculation
Ruling on ghusl for bestiality with causal reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on ghusl for intercourse with an animal without ejaculation and explain the causal reasoning (sababiyya) that distinguishes it from human intercourse.

Core Idea
Ejaculation is the original cause of ghusl; insertion is a substitute only where it typically leads to ejaculation.

This lesson clarifies a nuanced ruling in Hanafi fiqh regarding the obligation of ghusl (ritual bath) after bestiality. While the act itself is sinful, the question of whether ghusl is required depends on the presence of ejaculation. The passage explains the underlying principle: ejaculation is the primary cause, and insertion only stands in for it in contexts where it customarily leads to ejaculation—which does not apply to animals.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Ruling
Read the passage to extract the explicit ruling: intercourse with an animal without ejaculation does not require ghusl. Note that if ejaculation occurs, ghusl becomes obligatory.
The ruling is unconditional: no ghusl for insertion alone.
2
Understand the Original Cause (Asl)
The text states that the original cause (asl) of ghusl is ejaculation (inzal). This is the fundamental basis. Insertion (ilaj) is not an independent cause; it is a substitute (qaim maqam) only because it typically leads to ejaculation in certain contexts.
The substitution is based on customary occurrence (ghalaba).
3
Trace the Analogy (Qiyas)
The ruling is derived via qiyas from human intercourse. In humans, insertion without ejaculation still obligates ghusl because insertion generally leads to ejaculation. The 'illa (effective cause) is the typical outcome. However, this 'illa is absent in animals because their private parts do not provide the same stimulation.
Thus the analogy fails: the far' (animal) differs from the asl (human) in the 'illa.
4
Apply the Principle of Sababiyya
The concept of sababiyya (causality) in Hanafi usul is based on what is typical (ghalaba). Here, the causality of insertion is established only where complete sexual desire is fulfilled. The passage explicitly says this causality is not realized in animals.
Therefore, the ruling for animals reverts to the original cause: ejaculation alone triggers ghusl.
5
Note the Practical Outcome
The practical consequence (thamarah) is that a person who commits bestiality without ejaculation does not need to perform ghusl before prayer, though they must repent from the sin. If ejaculation occurs, ghusl is obligatory.
This ruling is specific to ghusl; the sinfulness of the act is a separate matter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[۲] قوله : لا وطء بهيمة ؛ أي لا يوجب الغسل وطء بهيمة إذا لم ينزل ، فإن أنزلَ يجب الغسل بالإنزال ؛ وذلك لأنّ أصل موجبِ الغُسلِ هو الإنزال، والإيلاج أقيمَ مقامه لكونه سبباً له، مفضياً إليه غالباً، وهذه السبية إنّما تتحقق فيما تتكامل فيه الشهوة، وفرج البهائم ليس كذلك.
[2] His statement: 'No intercourse with an animal'; meaning that intercourse with an animal does not necessitate ghusl if he does not ejaculate. If he ejaculates, then ghusl is obligatory due to the ejaculation. This is because the original cause of ghusl is ejaculation. Insertion is made to stand in its place because it is a cause leading to it, generally resulting in it. This causality only obtains where sexual desire is complete, and the vagina of animals is not like that.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This is a ruling on when ghusl becomes obligatory (fard) in the case of bestiality. The passage states that the primary cause (sabab) of ghusl is ejaculation (inzal). Insertion (ilaj) is considered a substitute cause only because it typically leads to ejaculation in cases where sexual desire is fully stimulated. However, with animals, this typical outcome does not hold because the animal's private part does not provide the same stimulation. Therefore, mere insertion without ejaculation does not necessitate ghusl. If ejaculation occurs, then ghusl is obligatory due to the ejaculation itself. This ruling is consistent with the Hanafi principle that the basis for ghusl is the seminal discharge, and insertion is only a proxy in human intercourse where it is the norm. The proof is rational (istihsan) based on the nature of the act.

Key Terms
إنزال (inzal): Ejaculation of semen
إيلاج (ilaj): Insertion of the male organ into a vagina or anus
سببية (sababiyyah): Causality: a cause that leads to a legal effect

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on ghusl for a person who has intercourse with an animal without ejaculation?
A) Ghusl is obligatory because insertion alone is sufficient.
B) Ghusl is not obligatory because ejaculation is the original cause, and insertion only stands in for it where it typically leads to ejaculation, which is not the case with animals.
C) Ghusl is recommended but not obligatory.
D) Ghusl is obligatory only if the animal is a domestic animal.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, ghusl is only required after bestiality if ejaculation occurs, because ejaculation is the original cause; insertion alone does not suffice due to the absence of customary causality.

Convert's Ghusl: Janabah vs Hayd Obligation
Intra-Hanafi dispute on convert's ghusl obligation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between continuous (janabah) and non-continuous (hayd) causes of impurity and apply the Hanafi ruling on a convert's obligation to perform ghusl for states incurred before Islam.

Core Idea
A convert must perform ghusl for janabah (continuous) but not for hayd (non-continuous), though this distinction is debated within the Hanafi school.

This passage from *ʿUmdat al-Riʿāya* critically examines a key Hanafi ruling regarding a convert's obligation to perform ghusl. The standard position distinguishes between janabah, a continuous impurity, and hayd, whose cause is the cessation of blood—a non-continuous event. However, the commentator raises three nuanced objections that reveal internal scholarly disagreement. By unpacking this analysis, you will gain a deeper understanding of how Hanafi jurists reason about the persistence of rulings before and after conversion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the standard Hanafi distinction
The standard view holds that janabah is a continuous state until purified, so it persists after conversion. Hayd's cause (inqitaʿ) is non-continuous, so it does not persist, thus no ghusl required.
2
Examine the first objection (khitab of non-Muslims)
Some Hanafis argue that non-Muslims are addressed by subsidiary rulings. If they are, then both states incur ghusl obligation, collapsing the distinction.
3
Analyze the second objection (true cause of ghusl)
The objector claims the true cause of ghusl is the intention to pray, not the impurity state itself. Thus after conversion, ghusl is needed for both janabah and hayd.
4
Evaluate the third objection (inconsistency in persistence)
Even janabah's occurrence is instantaneous; what persists is its effect. Hayd's effect also persists. So why treat them differently? This challenges the original distinction.
This objection strikes at the core of the continuity argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والانقطاع غير مستمر فافترقا.
[1] قوله : فافترقا ؛ محصل الفرق الذي ذكره تبعاً لما ذكره شمس الأئمة السرخسي في «شرح السير الكبير» وصاحب «الذخيرة» و«قاضي خان» وغيرهم: إنّ موجب غسل الجنابة هو الجنابة، وهو مستدامة إلى زمان الغسل، فإذا أجنبت الكافرة، ثمَّ أسلمت فجنابتها باقية، بقاؤها في الإسلام كإنشائها، فيجب عليها الغُسل. وأما غسل الحيض ونحوه فسببه انقطاع الدم، وهو أمر غير مستمر، فإذا وجد حال الكفر لم يبق في الإسلام، فلا يجب الغسل به لا في الإسلام؛ لعدم وجوده، ولا قبله ؛ لعدم كون الكفار مخاطبين بالفروع عملاً (۱).
وفيه نظر من وجوه :
الأول : إن كون الكفّار غير مخاطبين ليس أمراً إجماعياً، بل هو أمر خلافي بين أصحابنا أيضاً كما مرّ، فمن قال من أصحابنا بكونهم مخاطبين لا يظهر الفرق على مذهبه بين الجنابة والانقطاع، بل يجب الغُسل في كل منهما حال الكفر، ويبقى بعد الإسلام.
الثاني : إنّ الغُسل سبب وجوبه في الحقيقة إنّما هو إرادة الصلاة وما يتبعها لا الانقطاع ولا الجنابة، فيجبُ الغُسلُ في كلتا الصورتين بعد الإسلام.
الثالث : إنّ الجنابة حدوثها أيضاً بإنزال المني أو غيبوبة الحشفة آني غير مستمر ، وإنما المستمر حكمه وأثره ، وكذا الانقطاع ، وإن كان نفسه غير مستمر، لكن أثره باق ما لم يغتسل، فما وجه اعتبار البقاء في أحدهما والابتداء في الآخر فافهم.
(۱) وفي «تحفة الملوك» (ص (۳۱) نص على أن غسل الجنابة والحيض والنفاس لا يسقط بالإسلام.
And the interruption is not continuous, so they differ. [1] He said: 'So they differ'; the summary of the difference that he mentioned following what Shams al-A'imma al-Sarakhsi mentioned in Sharh al-Siyar al-Kabir and the author of al-Dhakhira and Qadi Khan and others: The cause of the bath of janabah is janabah, and it is continuous until the time of bathing. So if a non-Muslim woman becomes in a state of janabah, then embraces Islam, her janabah remains; its persistence in Islam is like its origination, so bathing is obligatory for her. As for the bath of menstruation and the like, its cause is the cessation of blood, and that is not continuous. So if it occurred during disbelief, it does not remain in Islam, so bathing is not obligatory due to it—neither in Islam due to its non-existence, nor before, because non-Muslims are not addressed with the branches according to practice (1). There is a consideration in this from several aspects: First: That non-Muslims not being addressed is not a matter of consensus; rather, it is a disputed matter among our companions as well, as has passed. So whoever among our companions says that they are addressed, the difference between janabah and cessation does not appear in his school; rather, bathing is obligatory in both cases during disbelief and remains after Islam. Second: The true cause of the obligation of bathing is the intention of prayer and what follows it, not cessation or janabah. So bathing is obligatory in both cases after Islam. Third: The occurrence of janabah is also through the emission of semen or the disappearance of the glans—an instantaneous, non-continuous [act]; rather, what is continuous is its ruling and effect. Similarly, cessation, even if it itself is not continuous, its effect remains as long as one has not bathed. So what is the consideration of persistence in one and initiation in the other? So understand. (1) And in Tuhfat al-Muluk (p. 31), it states that the bath of janabah, menstruation, and post-partum bleeding is not waived by embracing Islam.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement exploring the legal difference between janabah (major ritual impurity) and hayd (menstruation) for a person who embraces Islam after experiencing these states. The commentator begins by summarizing the standard Hanafi position from Sarakhsi, al-Dhakhira, and Qadi Khan: janabah is a continuous state that endures until purified, so if a non-Muslim woman becomes junub and then converts, her janabah persists and necessitates ghusl. In contrast, the cause of ghusl for hayd is the cessation of blood, which is a non-continuous event; having occurred during kufr, it does not remain after conversion, so no ghusl is required. The commentator then raises three objections. First, the premise that non-Muslims are not addressed with furu' (subsidiary rulings) is disputed among Hanafis; if they are addressed, then both janabah and hayd would require ghusl after conversion. Second, the true cause of ghusl is the intention to pray, not the state itself, so both cases would necessitate ghusl. Third, janabah's occurrence is also instantaneous, but its effect persists; similarly, hayd's effect (impurity) persists, so the distinction is inconsistent. The footnote cites Tuhfat al-Muluk, which asserts that ghusl for janabah, hayd, and nifas is not waived by conversion.

Key Terms
جنابة (janabah): Major ritual impurity requiring ghusl, caused by sexual intercourse or emission of semen.
حيض (hayd): Menstrual blood that occurs regularly and ceases after a period, requiring ghusl after cessation.
انقطاع (inqita'): Cessation of menstrual blood; a non-continuous cause for ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because janabah is a continuous state until purified, while hayd's cause (cessation) is non-continuous.
B) Because hayd does not exist in non-Muslims.
C) Because janabah is considered a sin while hayd is natural.
D) Because ghusl for hayd requires waiting for a certain period.

Key Takeaway
The obligation of ghusl for a convert depends on whether the impurity's cause is continuous (janabah) or non-continuous (hayd), a distinction subject to intra-Hanafi debate.

No Ghusl for Bestiality Without Emission
Explaining the ruling that bestiality without emission does not necessitate ghusl

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Hanafi fiqh distinguishes between human and animal intercourse regarding the obligation of ghusl, using the principle of qiyas jaliy.

Core Idea
Ghusl is primarily caused by emission; insertion substitutes only where it typically leads to emission.

This lesson tackles a specific ruling on bestiality: if no emission occurs, ghusl is not required. The reasoning reveals a fundamental principle in Hanafi fiqh about the causes of ritual purity. We will examine the qiyas (analogy) that justifies this ruling and understand why human intercourse is treated differently.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Clearly state the act being ruled upon (intercourse with a beast without emission) and the hukm (ghusl is not wajib).
The ruling is negative: it does not make ghusl obligatory.
2
Locate the asl (original case)
The original case is sexual intercourse with a human without emission. In that case, ghusl is obligatory due to insertion alone.
This is based on the hadith: 'When he sits between her four parts and exerts, ghusl is obligatory even if he does not ejaculate.'
3
Identify the illa (effective cause)
The illa is that insertion typically leads to emission where desire is complete. This is the reason why insertion substitutes for emission in obligating ghusl.
The illa is not just any insertion, but insertion in a context of complete sexual desire.
4
Apply to the far (new case)
The far is intercourse with a beast. The illa (complete desire) is absent because the vagina of a beast does not provide the same gratification. Thus, insertion alone does not substitute for emission.
If emission occurs, ghusl is wajib due to the original cause (inzal), not due to insertion.
5
Note the thamarah (practical result)
The practical consequence: bestiality without emission requires no ghusl; with emission, ghusl is required. Human intercourse always requires ghusl, even without emission.
This distinction is unique to the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا وطء بهيمة بلا إنزال
[۲] قوله : لا وطء بهيمة ؛ أي لا يوجب الغسل وطء بهيمة إذا لم ينزل ، فإن أنزلَ يجب الغسل بالإنزال ؛ وذلك لأنّ أصل موجبِ الغُسلِ هو الإنزال، والإيلاج أقيمَ مقامه لكونه سبباً له، مفضياً إليه غالباً، وهذه السبية إنّما تتحقق فيما تتكامل فيه الشهوة، وفرج البهائم ليس كذلك.
No intercourse with a beast without emission. [2] He said: 'No intercourse with a beast'; meaning that intercourse with a beast does not necessitate ghusl if he does not ejaculate; if he does ejaculate, ghusl is obligatory due to the emission. That is because the original cause of ghusl is emission, and insertion is placed in its position because it is a cause for it, usually leading to it. And this causality is only actualized where desire is complete, and the vagina of beasts is not like that.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the ruling that bestiality without emission does not require ghusl. The commentator clarifies the matn: if a person has intercourse with an animal and does not ejaculate, no ghusl is obligatory. If he does ejaculate, then ghusl is required due to the emission. The reasoning is based on qiyas: the original cause (asl) for ghusl is emission (inzāl). Insertion (īlāj) is substituted for emission because it typically leads to it (ghāliban). However, this substitution is valid only where desire is fully realized, meaning that insertion in a human vagina usually leads to emission. In animals, the vagina does not provide the same degree of sexual gratification, so insertion alone does not warrant ghusl. This is a clear application of qiyas jaliy where the effective cause ('illa) is the strong likelihood of emission.

Key Terms
إنزال (inzal): Emission of semen, the primary cause for ghusl.
إيلاج (ilaj): Insertion of the glans into a vagina; a cause for ghusl in humans due to the likelihood of emission.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, why does intercourse with a beast without emission not require ghusl?
A) Because intercourse with a beast is not considered a sexual act.
B) Because insertion is only a substitute for emission when desire is complete, and that is not the case with beasts.
C) Because beasts are not considered lawful partners.
D) Because the emission is impure.

Key Takeaway
Bestiality without emission does not necessitate ghusl because the effective cause ('illa) of substitution only applies where desire is complete, which is absent with beasts.

Recommended Ghusl for Friday, Eids, Ihram, and Arafah
Stating the recommended baths for Friday, the two Eids, Ihram, and Arafah, with proofs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the recommended baths for Friday, the two Eids, Ihram, and Arafah, and explain the intra-madhhab difference regarding the basis of Friday ghusl.

Core Idea
The Friday ghusl is a confirmed sunnah for the prayer, not for the day itself; women and children are not recommended to bathe.

After covering obligatory baths, the text now turns to recommended (sunnah) baths. The commentator clarifies that these baths are sunnah mu'akkadah, but there is a significant intra-Hanafi difference regarding the reason for Friday ghusl—whether it is for the day or for the prayer. This directly affects who should perform it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the acts and their ruling
The passage states that ghusl is sunnah for Friday, the two Eids, Ihram, and the day of Arafah. The commentator specifies this is the confirmed sunnah (sunnah mu'akkadah).
2
Locate the dalil (proofs)
Two hadith are cited: the hadith of al-Fakih showing the Prophet’s consistent practice of bathing on these occasions, and the hadith commanding bathing for Friday ('If one of you comes on Friday, let him bathe').
The second hadith is the main proof for Friday ghusl.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah (how the proof indicates the ruling)
The imperative 'let him bathe' is understood as sunnah, not wajib, due to other evidence (the Prophet’s practice and the absence of blame for omitting it). The consistency of the Prophet’s action confirms sunnah mu'akkadah.
In Hanafi usul, the imperative yields wujub unless there is a qarinah (context) indicating recommendation. Here, the qarinah is the lack of rebuke for omission.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on Friday ghusl
Al-Hasan ibn Ziyad holds that Friday ghusl is sunnah for the day itself, making it recommended for all Muslims, including women and children. The correct view (sahih) is that it is for the Friday prayer, so it is recommended only for those who attend the prayer.
The commentator explicitly labels the latter view as 'al-sahih' (the correct one).
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
According to the correct view, women and children (who are not obligated to pray Friday) are not recommended to bathe. According to al-Hasan, they are. This difference directly impacts personal practice and fatwa.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the recommended baths (ghusl) for specific occasions. The commentator explains that after discussing obligatory baths, the text now covers sunnah (recommended) baths. The default understanding is that these are sunnah mu'akkadah (confirmed sunnah). The primary proof is the hadith of al-Fakih that the Prophet used to bathe on Friday, the two Eids, the day of Nahr (Eid al-Adha), and the day of Arafah. Additionally, there is a hadith that he bathed for Ihram. The commentator then discusses a point of difference: the correct view (sahih) is that Friday ghusl is sunnah for the day of Friday, not for the prayer itself. This is based on the hadith that commands bathing for 'coming on Friday.' There is a contrary view from al-Hasan ibn Ziyad that it is for the day, leading to a practical difference: those not obligated to pray Friday (women, children) are recommended to bathe according to al-Hasan, but not according to the correct view. The footnotes mention that al-Halabi and Ibn al-Humam considered these baths mustahabb (recommended) rather than sunnah, an opinion that reduces their status. Furthermore, 'Abd al-Ghani al-Nabulusi argued that these baths are for cleanliness (nazafa) rather than ritual purity (tahara), so they would not be invalidated by a minor hadath (breaking wudu) during the day. Ibn 'Abidin supported this view. The commentator ends by directing to his work al-Si'aya for further discussion.

Key Terms
سنة مؤكدة (sunnah mu'akkadah): A confirmed recommended act that the Prophet consistently performed and sometimes emphasized.
مستحبة (mustahabbah): Recommended acts that are less emphasized than sunnah mu'akkadah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Only those who attend the Friday prayer.
C) Only men who have reached puberty.
D) Only those who do not have wudu.

Key Takeaway
The Friday ghusl is a confirmed sunnah for those attending the Friday prayer, not for the day itself; women and children are not recommended to bathe.

Water Changed by Long Storage or Pure Substances Remains Purifying
Ruling on permissible water sources and effect of change

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water that has changed due to long storage or pure substances, and distinguish it from change caused by najis.

Core Idea
Change by time or pure substances does not remove water's purifying property; only najis change does.

Not every change in water renders it impure. The Hanafi school carefully distinguishes the cause of change. In this passage, the commentator explains that water from natural sources (rain, springs, etc.) remains permissible for wudu even if its color, taste, or smell change due to prolonged storage or contact with a pure substance like dough or sidr leaves. Only change caused by an impure substance (najis) strips water of its tahur status. This principle is supported by the Prophet's own actions and instructions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of the ruling
The passage begins by listing permissible water sources: rain, springs, rivers, seas, wells, and melted snow. Note that snow is only allowed if it is liquid enough to drip.
Frozen snow cannot be used because it does not wet the limb, a requirement for purification.
2
Distinguish the two types of change
The commentator divides water change into two categories: change caused by mixing with an impure substance (najis) and change without najis. The latter includes change from long storage (mere time) and change from mixing with a pure substance.
Only the first category nullifies tahuriyya. The second category does not.
3
Analyze the evidence
The passage cites two hadith: the Prophet bathed from a bowl with traces of dough (a pure substance), and ordered washing a deceased person with water mixed with sidr (pure leaves). These actions demonstrate that water mixed with pure substances remains valid for purification.
These ahadith are used as proof that change from a pure source does not affect water's purifying property.
4
Apply the ruling to a scenario
Consider water that has turned slightly green due to standing in a tank for a week. Since the change is from time alone, the water is still tahur and can be used for wudu. If the green color came from urine (najis), the water would become impure and unusable.
This distinction is crucial for daily practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(٢) قوله : والعين ؛ سواء كان عيناً سائلاً أو راكداً ، ومثله ماء البحر والنهر والبئر. [٣]قوله : فإن كان ذائباً ؛ أي سائلاً رقيقاً ، يقال : ذابَ الشيءُ يذوب ذوباً وذوباناً إذا سال، والذائب خلاف المتصلب الجامد. [1] (And it is permissible to perform wudu with water from the sky and earth, like rain and springs), and as for snow water, if it is melted such that it drips, it is permissible, otherwise not. (And if it has changed due to long storage). [1] His statement: 'wudu'; he confined his mention to it even though the ruling for ghusl and washing clothes and body from najasah is also the same, because wudu occurs more frequently and is more important. [2] His statement: 'and springs' whether it is flowing or stagnant water; similarly sea, river, and well water. [3] His statement: 'if it is melted' i.e. liquid and thin. [4] His statement: 'otherwise not' i.e. if it is not melted but frozen, it is not permissible to perform wudu with it due to the lack of dripping required for removing legal and physical impurities. [0] His statement: 'due to long storage'; know that change in water is either due to mixing with something impure or not. If the former, purification with it is not permissible because the property of tahurah is lost. If the latter, the change is either due to mere length of time and duration in one place, for water when left for a time in a place undergoes some change, or due to mixing with a pure substance. In either case, purification with it is permissible because such change does not remove it from the property of purifying. Commentary
This passage opens the section on categories of water (aqsam al-miyah). The author states a general ruling: it is permissible to use water from natural sources like rain and springs for wudu. The commentary then defines terms and exceptions. Snow water is allowed only if it is melted enough to drip; frozen snow cannot be used because it does not wet the skin. The main topic is water that has changed (mutaghayyir). The change can be from an impure substance, which renders the water non-purifying, or from a pure substance or simply from long storage. The latter two do not affect the water's purifying property (tahuriyya). The commentator provides hadith evidence: the Prophet bathed from a vessel that had traces of dough (a pure substance), and he ordered washing the deceased with water mixed with sidr (a pure leaf). These show that water mixed with pure things remains valid. Thus, water that changes color, taste, or smell from prolonged storage or from pure substances is still pure and can be used for purification.

Key Terms
التغير (al-taghayyur): Change in water's color, taste, or smell.
بطول المكث (bi-tul al-makth): Due to prolonged stay in one place; a natural change from time.
ماء طهور (ma' tahur): Water that is pure and purifying; can be used for wudu and ghusl.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Water that has become greenish due to being stored for a long time.
B) Water that has been mixed with pure sidr leaves.
C) Water that has changed due to a drop of urine falling into it.
D) Water that has a slight taste of salt from its source.

Key Takeaway
Water remains purifying unless its change is caused by an impure substance; time and pure substances do not strip its tahur status.

Types of Permissible Water for Wudu
Ruling on permissible waters for wudu (rain, spring, snow)

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of water permissible for wudu according to the Hanafi school and state the condition for using snow water.

Core Idea
All natural waters (rain, spring, sea, river, well) are permissible; snow water is valid only if melted and dripping.

The Hanafi school begins the discussion of taharah by establishing a broad principle: any water that is naturally occurring from the sky or earth is intrinsically pure (tahur) and can be used for purification. This lesson covers the basic categories and the specific condition for snow water, which requires it to be in a liquid state that can flow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two primary water categories
Recognise that water is classified as 'ma' al-sama' (sky water, e.g., rain) and 'ma' al-ard' (earth water, e.g., springs, wells, rivers, seas). All are inherently pure and purifying.
2
Understand the consistency ruling for snow
Snow water is permissible only when it has melted into a state that can drip (yatasattir), i.e., it must be liquid enough to flow. Frozen snow not yet melted is not valid.
This is due to the requirement of water flow for washing.
3
Contrast with change by storage
The base text mentions that even if water changes due to prolonged storage, it remains permissible. This point is expanded in later lessons.
4
Apply the ruling to common scenarios
Recognise that tap water sourced from natural reserves falls under this ruling, as does bottled spring water or collected rainwater.

Source Text

Wudu is permissible with water from the sky and the earth, like rain and spring water, even if it has changed due to prolonged storage. [1] (Wudu is permissible with water from the sky and the earth, like rain and spring water). As for snow water, if it is melted such that it drips, it is permissible; otherwise, no. (And if it has changed due to prolonged storage). [1] His statement: 'wudu' – he confined himself to mentioning it even though the ruling of ghusl and washing of clothes and body from impurity is also likewise, because it is more frequent and more emphasized. (2) His statement: 'and spring' – whether it is flowing or stagnant, and likewise sea, river, and well water. [3] His statement: 'if it is melted' i.e., liquid and fluid. It is said: the thing melts, melting and melting, when it flows. Melted is the opposite of solid. [4] His statement: 'otherwise, no' i.e., if it is not melted but frozen, wudu is not permissible with it due to lack of dripping, which is conditioned in removing both legal and actual impurities. [0] His statement: 'by prolonged storage' – know that change in water is either by mixing with something impure or without. If the first, purification is not permissible with it due to loss of the property of purifying. If the second, then it is either change due to mere prolongation of time and extension of its stay in a place, for water when it remains for a time in a place undergoes some change; or it is by mixing with something pure. In either case, purification with it is permissible because such change does not remove its purifying property. Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof (ruling_with_proof) from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the matn of al-Wiqayah. It addresses the permissibility of wudu with various types of water and the effect of changes caused by pure substances. The author begins by stating the basic rule: wudu is valid with rainwater and spring water, even if the water has changed due to prolonged storage. This is because such change does not remove the water's purifying property (tahuriyyah). The commentary clarifies that this ruling extends to ghusl and washing clothes, but wudu is mentioned due to its frequent occurrence. The commentary then discusses snow water: it is valid only if it has melted and can drip. Next, it distinguishes changes in water: if due to an impurity, water becomes impermissible for purification; if due to prolonged storage or mixing with a pure substance (like dough, sidr, soil, saltwort, soap, or saffron), it remains permissible. Two hadiths are cited as proof: the Prophet performed ghusl from a bowl with dough traces, and he instructed washing a deceased in ihram with water and sidr. The passage then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding mixing with pure substances. Abu Hanifa holds that any pure substance (even saffron) does not invalidate wudu. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the added substance is intended for purification (like saltwort or soap), it is permissible unless it overcomes the water's thinness and fluidity; for other substances there are two narrations. Muhammad al-Shaybani considers predominance by parts. The correct view is that as long as the water has not lost its name (i.e., not become thick), wudu is valid. The commentary notes that the examples given (soil, saltwort, soap, saffron) are to show that the ruling does not vary even if the pure substance is from different categories (earth-like, purifying agents, or others like saffron).

Key Terms
ماء السماء (ma' al-sama'): Rainwater, water falling from the sky.
ماء الأرض (ma' al-ard): Spring water, water emerging from the earth.
الثلج (al-thalj): Snow; frozen precipitation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is NOT a condition for using snow water in wudu?
A) It must be melted such that it drips.
B) It must be collected directly from snowfall.
C) It must be in a liquid state.
D) It must be able to flow over the limbs.

Key Takeaway
Wudu is valid with all natural waters (rain, spring, melted snow) as long as they are in a liquid state fit for flowing over the limbs.

Taharah — Purification
beginner
6 min
Effect of Prolonged Storage on Water Purity
Ruling on change in water by prolonged storage

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling that a change in water's taste, colour, or odour due to prolonged storage does not affect its purifying property and distinguish it from change caused by impurity.

Core Idea
A change in water from mere storage does not invalidate wudu; only change from impurity removes its purifying quality.

When water is stored for a long time, it may naturally develop a different taste, colour, or smell. The Hanafi school addresses this common situation by clarifying that such change does not compromise the water's ability to purify. This lesson examines the distinction between two types of change: from impurity and from time or pure mixing.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate between change types
The commentary divides water change into two categories: (1) change by an impure substance, and (2) change by other causes (prolonged storage or mixing with pure substances).
2
Understand the ruling for impurity
If water changes due to contact with an impurity, it loses its purifying property (tahuriyyah) and becomes najis. Wudu with such water is invalid.
3
Understand the ruling for storage change
If water changes merely due to being kept in a place for a long time (e.g., developing a stale smell), it remains tahur. Wudu is valid because such change does not remove its purifying nature.
4
Recognise the same ruling for pure mixing
Change due to mixing with a pure substance (e.g., soil, leaves) also does not invalidate, as long as the water has not become so thick that it loses the name 'water'. This is a separate sub-case but shares the same principle.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof (ruling_with_proof) from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the matn of al-Wiqayah. It addresses the permissibility of wudu with various types of water and the effect of changes caused by pure substances. The author begins by stating the basic rule: wudu is valid with rainwater and spring water, even if the water has changed due to prolonged storage. This is because such change does not remove the water's purifying property (tahuriyyah). The commentary clarifies that this ruling extends to ghusl and washing clothes, but wudu is mentioned due to its frequent occurrence. The commentary then discusses snow water: it is valid only if it has melted and can drip. Next, it distinguishes changes in water: if due to an impurity, water becomes impermissible for purification; if due to prolonged storage or mixing with a pure substance (like dough, sidr, soil, saltwort, soap, or saffron), it remains permissible. Two hadiths are cited as proof: the Prophet performed ghusl from a bowl with dough traces, and he instructed washing a deceased in ihram with water and sidr. The passage then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding mixing with pure substances. Abu Hanifa holds that any pure substance (even saffron) does not invalidate wudu. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the added substance is intended for purification (like saltwort or soap), it is permissible unless it overcomes the water's thinness and fluidity; for other substances there are two narrations. Muhammad al-Shaybani considers predominance by parts. The correct view is that as long as the water has not lost its name (i.e., not become thick), wudu is valid. The commentary notes that the examples given (soil, saltwort, soap, saffron) are to show that the ruling does not vary even if the pure substance is from different categories (earth-like, purifying agents, or others like saffron).

Key Terms
تغير بطول المكث (taghayyur bi tawl al-makth): Change in water's taste, color, or odor due to prolonged storage without mixing with any external substance.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If water changes taste due to being stored in a copper container for a month, and no impurity is present, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) Wudu is invalid because the taste changed.
B) Wudu is valid because the change is from storage, not impurity.
C) Wudu is invalid because copper is an impure metal.
D) Wudu is valid only if the change is in colour, not taste.

Key Takeaway
Water changed by time remains pure; only change by impurity invalidates wudu.

Taharah — Purification
intermediate
8 min
Mixing with Pure Substances: General Ruling and Proofs
Ruling on wudu with water mixed with pure substances, supported by hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi ruling that wudu with water mixed with any pure substance is valid as long as it retains the name 'water', and cite the hadith proofs that support this.

Core Idea
The Prophet's use of water with dough and his command to wash the deceased with sidr prove that pure additives do not destroy water's purifying power.

This lesson focuses on a key principle: mixing water with a pure substance—whether soil, saltwort, soap, or even saffron—does not invalidate wudu unless the mixture becomes so thick that it no longer resembles water. The hadith of Umm Hani' and the instruction to wash the deceased with sidr provide strong evidence, showing that the Prophet himself used water mixed with dough and prescribed washing with sidr for a deceased person in ihram.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadith of Umm Hani'
The Prophet performed ghusl from a bowl in which there was a trace of dough (a pure substance). This demonstrates that mixing with a pure substance does not invalidate the water.
The hadith is from Sunan al-Nasa'i and others.
2
Read the hadith of Ibn 'Abbas on washing the deceased
The Prophet commanded that a deceased in ihram be washed with water and sidr (a plant). This shows intentional mixing with a pure substance is permissible for purification.
Narrated in Bukhari and Muslim.
3
Identify the general ruling
Commentary states that any pure substance (soil, saltwort, soap, saffron) does not remove the water's purifying property. The crucial condition is that the water has not become thick (thakhina) and lost the name 'water'.
4
Apply to examples
Soil (earth), saltwort/soap (cleaning agents), saffron (non-cleaning) all follow the same rule. The ruling does not vary based on the type of pure substance.

Wudu is permissible with water from the sky and the earth, like rain and spring water, even if it has changed due to prolonged storage. [1] (Wudu is permissible with water from the sky and the earth, like rain and spring water). As for snow water, if it is melted such that it drips, it is permissible; otherwise, no. (And if it has changed due to prolonged storage). [1] His statement: 'wudu' – he confined himself to mentioning it even though the ruling of ghusl and washing of clothes and body from impurity is also likewise, because it is more frequent and more emphasized. (2) His statement: 'and spring' – whether it is flowing or stagnant, and likewise sea, river, and well water. [3] His statement: 'if it is melted' i.e., liquid and fluid. It is said: the thing melts, melting and melting, when it flows. Melted is the opposite of solid. [4] His statement: 'otherwise, no' i.e., if it is not melted but frozen, wudu is not permissible with it due to lack of dripping, which is conditioned in removing both legal and actual impurities. [0] His statement: 'by prolonged storage' – know that change in water is either by mixing with something impure or without. If the first, purification is not permissible with it due to loss of the property of purifying. If the second, then it is either change due to mere prolongation of time and extension of its stay in a place, for water when it remains for a time in a place undergoes some change; or it is by mixing with something pure. In either case, purification with it is permissible because such change does not remove its purifying property. (1) The Prophet (SAW) performed ghusl from a bowl in which there was a trace of dough, as narrated by al-Nasa'i. And the Prophet (SAW) commanded that the deceased be washed with water mixed with sidr, as narrated by the two Shaykhs. (1) From Umm Hani' (RA) who said: The Messenger of Allah (SAW) was at the top of Mecca on the day of conquest, and I came to him. Abu Dharr brought a bowl with water. I said: 'I see in it a trace of dough.' She said: Abu Dharr screened him and he performed ghusl. Then the Prophet (SAW) screened Abu Dharr and he performed ghusl. (2) From Ibn 'Abbas (RA): A man fell from his mount and died while in ihram. The Messenger of Allah (SAW) said: 'Wash him with water and sidr, and shroud him in his two garments, and do not cover his head or face, for he will be raised on the Day of Resurrection reciting the talbiyah.' Or unless one of its three attributes (taste, color, smell) is changed by a pure substance, like soil, saltwort, soap, or saffron. [1] His statement: 'one of its attributes' – this restriction is agreed upon. For if it changes in other than the three attributes also: saltwort, soap, prolonged storage, or leaves, and it has not lost the name of water (i.e., has not become thick), then wudu with it is permissible according to the correct view, as in al-Nihayah, al-Binayah, and Jami' al-Mudmarat etc. (2) His statement: 'he only mentioned these things' – to rebut an implied objection: it would have sufficed to mention one of these things as an example, so what is the point of lengthening by mentioning them? [3] His statement: 'And according to Abu Yusuf (RA) – the summary of his madhhab is that if something is mixed with it that is intended for the same purpose as water, i.e., purification, then its mixing is not harmful unless it removes the name of water. And if it is other than that, then there are two narrations from him: in one, if that thing dominates, wudu is not permissible with it; otherwise it is permissible. In another, wudu is absolutely not permissible. And Muhammad (RA) considered in this class of issues that the permissibility of wudu is prevented by the predominance of the mixture over the water. Thus in al-Dhakhirah. [4] His statement: 'until' etc. – it indicates that what is considered is predominance in terms of parts, not predominance of

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof (ruling_with_proof) from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the matn of al-Wiqayah. It addresses the permissibility of wudu with various types of water and the effect of changes caused by pure substances. The author begins by stating the basic rule: wudu is valid with rainwater and spring water, even if the water has changed due to prolonged storage. This is because such change does not remove the water's purifying property (tahuriyyah). The commentary clarifies that this ruling extends to ghusl and washing clothes, but wudu is mentioned due to its frequent occurrence. The commentary then discusses snow water: it is valid only if it has melted and can drip. Next, it distinguishes changes in water: if due to an impurity, water becomes impermissible for purification; if due to prolonged storage or mixing with a pure substance (like dough, sidr, soil, saltwort, soap, or saffron), it remains permissible. Two hadiths are cited as proof: the Prophet performed ghusl from a bowl with dough traces, and he instructed washing a deceased in ihram with water and sidr. The passage then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding mixing with pure substances. Abu Hanifa holds that any pure substance (even saffron) does not invalidate wudu. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the added substance is intended for purification (like saltwort or soap), it is permissible unless it overcomes the water's thinness and fluidity; for other substances there are two narrations. Muhammad al-Shaybani considers predominance by parts. The correct view is that as long as the water has not lost its name (i.e., not become thick), wudu is valid. The commentary notes that the examples given (soil, saltwort, soap, saffron) are to show that the ruling does not vary even if the pure substance is from different categories (earth-like, purifying agents, or others like saffron).

Key Terms
الأشنان (al-ushnan): Saltwort or any plant used for cleaning, like soap.
الزعفران (al-za'faran): Saffron, a pure substance used for coloring and fragrance.
السدر (al-sidr): Lote tree leaves used as a cleaning agent.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a pure substance mentioned in the lesson as an example of an additive that does not invalidate wudu?
A) Soil (turab)
B) Saltwort (ushnan)
C) Vinegar (khal)
D) Saffron (za'faran)

Key Takeaway
Wudu with water mixed with any pure substance is valid as long as the water remains thin and retains its name; the hadith of Umm Hani' and Ibn 'Abbas provide strong proof.

Taharah — Purification
advanced
8 min
Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Pure Mixtures (Saffron Case)
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mixing with pure substances not intended for purification

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on wudu with water mixed with a pure substance not intended for purification, and identify the mufta bihi.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa permits any pure mixture; Abu Yusuf distinguishes by purpose and domination; Muhammad by predominance of parts; the mufta bihi is permissibility as long as water remains thin.

While the general rule permits mixing with pure substances, the Hanafi school itself has an internal disagreement on the details, especially regarding substances not intended for purification (like saffron). This lesson explores the nuanced positions of the three major Hanafi imams: Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani. Understanding their differences is crucial for a mufti when issuing fatwas in diverse circumstances.

Guided Path
1
State Abu Hanifa's position
Abu Hanifa holds that any pure substance, including saffron, does not invalidate wudu unless the water loses its name (becomes thick). The purpose of the additive is irrelevant.
2
State Abu Yusuf's position
Abu Yusuf divides pure substances into two categories: those intended for purification (like saltwort, soap) and others (like saffron). For purification-intended substances, mixing is permissible unless it dominates and removes the water's thinness. For others, there are two narrations: one permits unless it dominates, another absolutely prohibits.
3
State Muhammad's position
Muhammad al-Shaybani considers that the permissibility of wudu is prevented by the predominance of the mixture over the water in terms of parts (ghalaba bi al-ajza'). Even if the water retains its name, if the mixed substance is quantitatively dominant, wudu is invalid.
4
Identify the mufta bihi
The text states that 'on the correct view' (على الصحيح) and in later works like al-Nihayah, al-Binayah, wudu is valid as long as the water has not become thick and lost the name 'water'. This is the relied-upon position, which aligns closest with Abu Hanifa's view.

Source Text

The Messenger of Allah (SAW) said: 'Wash him with water and sidr, and shroud him in his two garments, and do not cover his head or face, for he will be raised on the Day of Resurrection reciting the talbiyah.' Or unless one of its three attributes (taste, color, smell) is changed by a pure substance, like soil, saltwort, soap, or saffron. [1] His statement: 'one of its attributes' – this restriction is agreed upon. For if it changes in other than the three attributes also: saltwort, soap, prolonged storage, or leaves, and it has not lost the name of water (i.e., has not become thick), then wudu with it is permissible according to the correct view, as in al-Nihayah, al-Binayah, and Jami' al-Mudmarat etc. (2) His statement: 'he only mentioned these things' – to rebut an implied objection: it would have sufficed to mention one of these things as an example, so what is the point of lengthening by mentioning them? [3] His statement: 'And according to Abu Yusuf (RA) – the summary of his madhhab is that if something is mixed with it that is intended for the same purpose as water, i.e., purification, then its mixing is not harmful unless it removes the name of water. And if it is other than that, then there are two narrations from him: in one, if that thing dominates, wudu is not permissible with it; otherwise it is permissible. In another, wudu is absolutely not permissible. And Muhammad (RA) considered in this class of issues that the permissibility of wudu is prevented by the predominance of the mixture over the water. Thus in al-Dhakhirah. [4] His statement: 'until' etc. – it indicates that what is considered is predominance in terms of parts, not predominance of
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof (ruling_with_proof) from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah on the matn of al-Wiqayah. It addresses the permissibility of wudu with various types of water and the effect of changes caused by pure substances. The author begins by stating the basic rule: wudu is valid with rainwater and spring water, even if the water has changed due to prolonged storage. This is because such change does not remove the water's purifying property (tahuriyyah). The commentary clarifies that this ruling extends to ghusl and washing clothes, but wudu is mentioned due to its frequent occurrence. The commentary then discusses snow water: it is valid only if it has melted and can drip. Next, it distinguishes changes in water: if due to an impurity, water becomes impermissible for purification; if due to prolonged storage or mixing with a pure substance (like dough, sidr, soil, saltwort, soap, or saffron), it remains permissible. Two hadiths are cited as proof: the Prophet performed ghusl from a bowl with dough traces, and he instructed washing a deceased in ihram with water and sidr. The passage then delves into an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding mixing with pure substances. Abu Hanifa holds that any pure substance (even saffron) does not invalidate wudu. Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the added substance is intended for purification (like saltwort or soap), it is permissible unless it overcomes the water's thinness and fluidity; for other substances there are two narrations. Muhammad al-Shaybani considers predominance by parts. The correct view is that as long as the water has not lost its name (i.e., not become thick), wudu is valid. The commentary notes that the examples given (soil, saltwort, soap, saffron) are to show that the ruling does not vary even if the pure substance is from different categories (earth-like, purifying agents, or others like saffron).

Key Terms
الغلبة بالأجزاء (al-ghalaba bi al-ajza'): Predominance in terms of quantity of parts, i.e., the mixed substance constitutes the majority of the mixture.
الرقة والسيلان (al-riqqah wa al-sayalan): Thinness and fluidity; the natural state of water.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Hanafi position is considered the mufta bihi (relied-upon for fatwa) regarding wudu with water mixed with a pure substance like saffron?
A) Muhammad al-Shaybani's view: invalid if the mixture predominates by parts.
B) Abu Yusuf's first narration: valid unless the substance dominates.
C) Abu Hanifa's view: valid as long as the water retains its name (thinness).
D) Abu Yusuf's second narration: absolutely invalid regardless of quantity.

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute on pure mixtures is settled by the relied-upon view: wudu is valid unless the water becomes thick and loses the name 'water'.

Water Mixed with Pure Substances: Rulings for Wudu
Intra-Hanafi dispute with response to Shafi'i on water mixed with pure substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) on water mixed with pure substances and apply the criterion of predominance (ghalaba) by parts to determine validity of wudu.

Core Idea
Water mixed with a pure substance remains valid for wudu unless the substance overcomes the water by parts and removes its fluidity.

This passage addresses a common question: can you perform wudu with water that has been mixed with a pure substance like saffron, soap, or earth? The Hanafi school has detailed internal discussions, and the ruling hinges on the concept of 'ghalaba' (predominance) and the intended use of the additive. We will unpack the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad, and then see how the Hanafi school differs from Imam al-Shafi'i on this issue.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three Hanafi positions
List the opinions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad as presented in the text. Note what each considers the key factor for permissibility.
Abu Hanifa permits any pure mixture unless it removes fluidity; Abu Yusuf distinguishes by intent; Muhammad focuses on predominance by parts.
2
Understand the criterion of ghalaba (predominance)
The text states that the correct view considers predominance 'in terms of parts' (bil-ajzā'), not by color or taste. Grasp why the Hanafis prioritize parts over sensory changes.
This is from al-Hidayah: color change alone does not invalidate.
3
Apply to a scenario: saffron water
Given a bowl of water with a few strands of saffron (color changed but still fluid), determine validity under each Hanafi imam.
Abu Hanifa: valid. Abu Yusuf: if not intended for purification, two narrations; Muhammad: valid if saffron is not predominant by parts.
4
Compare with the Shafi'i position
State why Imam al-Shafi'i considers such water muqayyad (restricted) and thus invalid for wudu. Identify the Hanafi rebuttal based on the analogy to well water.
The key difference is whether the water can still be called 'absolute water' (mutlaq) despite the additive.
5
Summarize the relied-upon view
Conclude that the mufta bihi position is that predominance by parts is considered, and wudu is valid unless the substance overcomes water's fluidity. This aligns with Abu Hanifa's view as adopted in al-Hidayah.
This is the fatwa for daily practice.

Or [if] one of its characteristics [is changed] by a pure substance like earth, ashes, soap, saffron... According to Abu Yusuf: if the mixed substance is intended for purification, wudu is permissible with it, unless it overcomes the water until it removes its nature, which is thinness and fluidity. And if it is other than that, then from him there are two narrations... And Muhammad considered in these types of cases, to prevent the permissibility of wudu, the predominance of the mixture over the water... His statement: 'until...' – therein is an indication that what is considered is predominance in terms of parts, not predominance in terms of color, and that is the correct view as in al-Hidayah. And if it is something not intended for purification: in one narration, predominance over water is required for impermissibility; in another, it is not required. Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the permissibility of using water mixed with pure substances for wudu, with an inter-madhhab disagreement with al-Shafi'i. The base text states the general rule: if water's three characteristics (taste, color, smell) are changed by a pure substance, the ruling varies. The commentator explains that Abu Hanifa permits wudu regardless of the substance, as long as the water remains fluid (i.e., retains its name as water). Abu Yusuf distinguishes: if the mixed substance is intended for purification (like soap or ashes), wudu is valid unless the substance overcomes the water's fluidity; if not intended for purification, there are two narrations – one requiring predominance, one not. Muhammad considers predominance in terms of parts. The correct view is that predominance by parts is what matters, not color (per al-Hidayah). The passage then addresses substances not from the earth's genus (like saffron), where al-Shafi'i considers the water restricted (muqayyad) and thus invalid for wudu. The Hanafi response, quoting Ibn al-Humam, argues that as long as the substance is overcome, the water retains its absolute (mutlaq) name, analogous to well or spring water. The relied-upon position is Abu Hanifa's view (per the majority).

Key Terms
غلبہ (ghalaba): Predominance of the mixed substance over water, which can be by parts or by characteristics.
طاهر (tāhir): A substance that is pure in itself and permissible to use, like earth, soap, or saffron.
مقيد (muqayyad): Water that is restricted by an attribute, e.g., saffron water; considered not absolute by Shafi'is.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Whether the substance is from the genus of earth.
B) Whether the substance overcomes the water by parts.
C) Whether the water's color changes.
D) Whether the person intended the water to be used for purification.

Key Takeaway
Water mixed with a pure substance remains valid for wudu in the Hanafi school as long as the substance does not overcome the water by parts and negate its fluidity; the relied-upon view considers predominance by parts, not by sensory changes.
