Purification with Flowing Water: Definition and Condition of Flow
Clarifying the ruling on wudu with flowing water and the disagreement on whether it requires a continuous source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of flowing water and identify the condition for its validity in wudu.

Core Idea
Flowing water is defined by motion, not a continuous source; even rainwater suffices.

This lesson clarifies the precise Hanafi definition of 'flowing water' (mā' jārin). You will learn that the key requirement is motion, not a permanent source like a spring. We will also address a common misconception and the relied-upon position of the school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Recognize that the passage rules on wudu with flowing water. The ruling is permissible (jā'iz) if the water is in motion and impurity is not visible.
2
Define the key term
Understand what 'flowing' (mā' jārin) means: water that is moving, whether naturally or by drawing. The definition is broad and includes rain, snowmelt, or any current.
Contrast with standing water.
3
Identify the disagreement
Note that Ibn al-Humam required a continuous source (imdad) for flow. The passage then presents the correct view that no source is needed.
The tarjih comes from later authorities like al-Bahr al-Rā'iq.
4
Apply the condition
The only condition mentioned is that impurity must not be visible (color or trace). If the water flows over impurity but no change appears, it remains pure.
5
Relate to practice
Understand how this ruling applies in daily life: a tap, hose, or rainwater flowing in a street is valid for wudu as long as no impurity is seen.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبماء جار
[٢] قوله : وبماء ; عطف على قوله : «بماء السماء». جار من الجريان : صفة للماء، وهو أعم من أن يكون الجريان بمدد أو بغير مدد ، فماء المطر والثلج إذا جرى في الطريق وفيه نجاسة متفرقة بحيث لا يرى لونها ولا أثرها يجوز التوضؤ به ، كما في «مختارات النوازل»(4). وفروع هذا التعميم مفصلة في «السعاية»، واختار ابن الهمام) اشتراط المدد للجريان، كما في العين والنهر ، وقال صاحب «البحر» نقلاً عن «السراج الوهاج»: «إنَّه لا يشترط فيه المدد، وهو الصحيح.
And [purification] with flowing water. [2] His statement: 'And with flowing water' – it is conjoined to his statement 'with rain water'. 'Flowing' from flow: a description of water, and it is more general that the flow be with a source or without a source. So rain and snow water, if they flow in the path and there is scattered impurity such that its color or trace is not seen, it is permissible to perform wudu with it, as in 'Mukhtarāt al-Nawāzil'. The branches of this generalization are detailed in 'al-Si'ayah'. Ibn al-Humam chose the condition of a source for flow, as in a spring and river. The author of 'al-Bahr' said, quoting from 'al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj': 'Indeed, a source is not required for it, and that is the correct view.'
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement about purification with flowing water (mā' jārin). The base text permits wudu with flowing water. The commentary explains that 'flowing' describes water that moves, whether or not it has a continuous source (imdad). This includes rain or melting snow flowing in a street, even if it passes over impurity that is not visible; such water is valid for wudu. The commentator notes that branches of this general ruling are detailed in other works. There is a disagreement: Ibn al-Humam (al-Sa'ati) required a continuous source for the water to be considered flowing (like a spring or river), but the majority view, as stated by the author of al-Bahr al-Rā'iq quoting al-Sirāj al-Wahhāj, is that a source is not required, and this is the correct (sahīh) position. The relied-upon view is that flowing water without a continuous source is still valid for purification.

Key Terms
ماء جار (mā' jārin): Water that is flowing, whether by natural current or by being drawn, and does not necessarily require a continuous source.
مدد (imdad): A continuous source that supplies the flow, like a spring feeding a river.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) It must come from a continuous source like a spring.
B) It must be in motion, regardless of a source.
C) It must be more than a specific quantity (qullatān).
D) It must not have any impurity in it.

Key Takeaway
Flowing water is any water in motion; it is valid for wudu as long as no impurity is visible, and this does not require a continuous source.

Water Mixed with Pure Substances: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Explains ruling on water mixed with pure substances and Shafi'i dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on water mixed with pure substances and explain why it differs from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Water mixed with non-dominant pure substances remains mutlaq for wudu; attribution does not change its essence.

This passage addresses a classic dispute: when water is mixed with a pure substance like saffron, can it still be used for wudu? The Hanafi school distinguishes between dominant and non-dominant mixtures, while Imam al-Shafi'i considers any change in name makes the water muqayyad. We will examine the ruling, its proof, and the practical consequence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal status
What is the act under consideration? (wudu with water mixed with a pure non-earth substance like saffron). Determine the Hanafi ruling: it is permissible if the mixture is not dominant.
The passage includes two riwayat on the condition of dominance.
2
Locate the dispute
Who disagrees with the Hanafi position? Imam al-Shafi'i holds that such water is muqayyad and invalid for wudu regardless of dominance.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The Hanafi proof uses qiyas (analogy) to water attributed to a well or spring – such water is still called water and is mutlaq. Similarly, 'saffron water' remains water as long as the saffron is not dominant.
The logical connection: attribution does not change the essence.
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: Hanafis permit wudu with such water if the mixture is not dominant; Shafi'is do not and would require tayammum if no other water is available.
This shows how a minor difference in classification leads to a different ruling in practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن كان شيئاً لا يقصد به التطهير : ففي رواية يشترط لعدم جواز التوضؤ به غلبته على الماء. وفي رواية : لا يشترط . وما ليس من جنس الأرض فيه خلاف الشافعي قوله : ففيه خلاف الشافعي له ; قال ابن الهمام في فتح القدير: اتفقوا على أن الماء المقيد لا يزيل الأحداث، والحكم عند فقد المطلق منصرف إلى التيمم، والخلاف في الماء الذي خالطه الزعفران وغيره مبني على أنه مقيد عند الشافعي، ونحن لا ننكر أنه يقال له : ماء الزعفران، ولكنا نقول : لا يمتنع مع ذلك ما دام المخالط مغلوباً أن يقال : إنه ماء من غير زيادة، والإضافة إلى الزعفران لا يمتنع الإطلاق : كالإضافة إلى البئر، والعين، وغيرهما.
And if it is something not intended for purification: in one transmission it is conditioned for the impermissibility of performing wudu with it that its dominance over water. In another transmission: it is not conditioned. And what is not from the genus of earth, there is a difference of opinion with al-Shafi'i. His statement: 'In it is a difference with al-Shafi'i.' Ibn al-Humam said in Fath al-Qadir: 'They agreed that restricted water does not remove ritual impurity, and the ruling when unrestricted water is absent is to turn to tayammum. The difference regarding water mixed with saffron and other things is based on it being restricted according to al-Shafi'i. We do not deny that it is called saffron water, but we say: as long as the admixture is overwhelmed, it does not prevent it from being called water without addition, and the attribution to saffron does not prevent it being called unrestricted, like attribution to a well, a spring, and others.'
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This is an inter-madhhab dispute passage. The base ruling is that water mixed with a pure substance not intended for purification (like saffron) is still valid for wudu if the mixture is not dominant. Two transmissions within the Hanafi school differ on whether dominance is a condition. The dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i is then stated: he considers such water muqayyad (restricted) and thus invalid for wudu. Ibn al-Humam's comment explains the Hanafi position: as long as the mixture is overwhelmed (maghlub), the water remains mutlaq. The analogy is to water attributed to a well or spring—it is still called water despite the attribution. The Hanafi position is that the name does not change the essence.

Key Terms
ماء مقيد (ma' muqayyad): Water mixed with a substance that changes its name, like saffron or rose water, considered restricted for purification.
ماء مطلق (ma' mutlaq): Pure, unrestricted water that can be used for purification.
الغلبة (al-ghalabah): Dominance of the mixture over the water; if dominant, water becomes muqayyad.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a small amount of saffron is added to water such that it is still called water, can one perform wudu with it?
A) Yes, it is permissible as long as the saffron is not dominant.
B) No, it is impermissible because the water is now muqayyad.
C) Only if the saffron is from earth.
D) Only if the water is flowing.

Key Takeaway
Water mixed with a pure non-dominant substance remains mutlaq for wudu; only dominance makes it muqayyad and invalid.

Flowing Water: Definition and Ruling on Trace Impurity
Defines flowing water and rules on impurity without trace

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of flowing water (ma' jar) and apply the ruling that flowing water remains purifying when no trace of impurity is visible.

Core Idea
Flowing water is defined by custom, not by source continuity; impurity without trace does not nullify its purifying power.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies a critical point in taharah: what constitutes flowing water (ma' jar) and how impurity affects its use. The Hanafi school provides flexibility by adopting a custom-based definition ('urf), which eases practical application. Understanding this ruling is essential for correctly judging water purity in daily life.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its scope
Locate the term 'ma' jar' in the text. Note that 'jar' means flowing, and the definition includes water with or without a continuous source (madad).
The term is broader than you might think.
2
Trace the internal Hanafi dispute
Ibn al-Humam required madad (continuous supply) for flow. The author of al-Bahr rejects this, and later works prefer the custom-based definition.
This dispute shows how later scholars simplified the rule.
3
Extract the ruling on impurity
When an impurity falls into flowing water, if its trace (taste, color, or smell) is not visible, wudu with that water is permissible.
The condition is 'trace unseen', not 'impurity absent'.
4
Understand the three definitions and the tarjih
Three definitions are given: (1) removes impurity before second scoop, (2) hand across does not stop flow, (3) custom considers it flowing. The third is declared correct by later authoritative works.
Custom ('urf) is the operative standard.
5
Apply to practical cases
A rainwater stream or a garden hose is 'jari' by custom. If a small impurity falls and no trace remains, the water is pure and usable.
Know when the water is no longer valid: if trace appears.

قوله : وبماء ; عطف على قوله : «بماء السماء». جار من الجريان : صفة للماء، وهو أعم من أن يكون الجريان بمدد أو بغير مدد ، فماء المطر والثلج إذا جرى في الطريق وفيه نجاسة متفرقة بحيث لا يرى لونها ولا أثرها يجوز التوضؤ به ، كما في مختارات النوازل. وفروع هذا التعميم مفصلة في السعاية، واختار ابن الهمام اشتراط المدد للجريان، كما في العين والنهر ، وقال صاحب البحر نقلاً عن السراج الوهاج: إنه لا يشترط فيه المدد، وهو الصحيح. فيه نَجَسٌ لم يُرَ أثره : أي طعمه، أو لونه، أو ريحه .. اختلفوا في حد الجاري قوله : فيه نجس ; بفتح الجيم ; أي نجاسة، وهو صفة ثانية للماء، وظاهره أن الجاري إذا وقعت فيه نجاسة لم ير أثرها يجوز الوضوء به ، سواء كان النجس جيفةً أو غيره، فإذا بالَ إنسان فيه فتوضأ آخر من أسفله جاز ما لم يظهر في الجرية أثره. كذا في البحر. قوله : لم ير أثره ; صفةٌ لنجس : أي لم يعلم في ذلك الماء أثر ذلك النجاسة، فالمراد بالرؤية : العلم وبه يندفع ما يقال : إنّ الرؤية إنما تتعلق باللون لا بالطعم والريح. قوله : اختلفوا ; أي اختلف الفقهاء في تعريف الماء الجاري ، وما يُقدَّر به جريه فقيل : هو الذي يذهب بالنجاسة قبل اغتراف الغرفة الثانية. وقيل : هو ما يكون بحيث لو وضع إنسان يده عليه عرضاً لم ينقطع. وقيل : ما يعده العرفُ جارياً، كذا ذكره في التاتارخانية، وصحح في غاية البيان والبناية والبدائع والبحر وغيرها القول الآخر.
His statement: 'And with water' is connected to his statement: 'with rainwater'. 'Jarin' from 'jaryan' is an adjective for water, and it is more general as to whether the flow is with a continuous supply (madad) or without. So rainwater and snow when they flow in the road and there is scattered impurity in them such that its color and trace are not seen, it is permissible to perform wudu with them, as in Mukhtarat al-Nawazil. The branches of this generalization are detailed in al-Si'aya. Ibn al-Humam chose the condition of madad for flow, as in a spring and river. And the author of al-Bahr said, quoting from al-Siraj al-Wahhaj: 'Madad is not required for it, and that is correct.' 'In it is impure matter whose trace is not seen': i.e., its taste, or color, or smell. They differed on the definition of 'jari' (flowing). His statement: 'In it is impure matter'—with a fatha on the jim (najas)—i.e., impurity; it is a second adjective for water, and its apparent meaning is that when impurity falls into flowing water and its trace is not seen, it is permissible to perform wudu with it, whether the impurity is a carcass or otherwise. So if a person urinates in it and another performs wudu from downstream, it is permissible as long as its trace does not appear in the flow. This is in al-Bahr. His statement: 'Its trace is not seen' is an adjective for 'najas': i.e., the effect of that impurity is not known in that water. The meaning of 'ru'yah' is knowledge, and by this it is rebutted what is said that sight only pertains to color, not taste and smell. His statement: 'They differed' means the jurists differed on the definition of flowing water and what determines its flow. Some said: it is that which removes the impurity before the second scoop. Some said: it is such that if a person places his hand on it crosswise, it does not cease to flow. Some said: it is what custom considers flowing. This is mentioned in al-Tatarkhaniyya, and it is declared correct in Ghayat al-Bayan, al-Binaya, al-Bada'i', al-Bahr, and others: the latter opinion.
Source pages p. 365

Commentary
This passage focuses on the definition and ruling of flowing water (ma' jar). The sharh first clarifies that the word 'jar' is an adjective describing water that flows, and it includes both water with a continuous flow (like a river) and without (like rain running on a road). The ruling is that if such water has an impurity but no trace (taste, color, smell) is visible, it remains purifying. This is supported by the quote from 'Mukhtarat al-Nawazil'. Then an intra-Hanafi dispute is presented: Ibn al-Humam required a continuous flow (madad) for something to be considered flowing, while the author of al-Bahr, quoting al-Siraj al-Wahhaj, rejected this requirement and declared the latter view correct. Next, the passage explains that 'trace' includes taste, color, and smell, and 'seeing' means knowledge, not just sight. Finally, three definitions of flowing water are given: (1) water that removes impurity before a second scoop, (2) water that does not stop if one places a hand across it, and (3) water that custom ('urf) considers flowing. The preferred view among later Hanafi scholars (like in Ghayat al-Bayan, al-Binaya, al-Bada'i', al-Bahr) is the custom-based definition.

Key Terms
ماء جار (ma' jar): Water that flows, including rain and snow run-off, as well as rivers and springs.
المدد (al-madad): Continuous supply of water (e.g., from a spring or river) that maintains flow.
أثر النجاسة (athar al-najasah): Visible trace of impurity: taste, color, or smell.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred view in the text, what is the definition of flowing water (ma' jar)?
A) Water that removes impurity before a second scoop.
B) Water that does not stop if a hand is placed across it.
C) Water that custom considers flowing.
D) Water that has a continuous source like a spring.

Key Takeaway
Flowing water is defined by custom, and impurity without visible trace does not invalidate it for purification.

Definition of Flowing Water and Ruling on Impurity
Commentary on a ruling with a definitional dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of flowing water (al-jari) and apply the ruling that wudu is permissible with it when no trace of impurity appears.

Core Idea
Flowing water is pure if no trace of impurity; defined as water that carries away a straw or leaf.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary clarifies the ruling on wudu with flowing water into which an impurity has fallen. The key is whether the impurity leaves a trace—taste, colour, or smell. The commentator also explains the internal Hanafi debate on what exactly constitutes 'flowing' water, settling on a practical definition that avoids hardship.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
Focus on the scenario: impurity falls into flowing water, but no trace appears. What is the ruling for using that water for wudu?
The passage says it is permissible (ja'iz).
2
Define 'flowing water'
The jurists differed on the definition. List the three opinions: (1) water that removes impurity before second scoop; (2) water that does not stop when hand placed across; (3) water considered flowing by custom. Then note the preferred definition: water that goes with a straw or leaf.
Custom-based and straw/leaf definitions are closely related; the latter is more precise.
3
Understand the grammatical defense
The commentator defends the preferred definition by analyzing the word 'ma' (ما) – it is not a relative pronoun but an indefinite noun described by 'yadhabu'. This rebuts objections like a camel or ship carrying straw.
This shows the importance of linguistic precision in Hanafi usul.
4
Apply the ruling
Given the preferred definition, consider a dammed river where water flows weakly but can still carry a straw. According to the mufta bihi position, such water is considered flowing and wudu with it is permissible if no impurity trace appears.
This is the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).

[٢] قوله : ما يذهب من الإذهاب، فالباء الداخلة على «التبنة» زائدة، ويمكن أن يقرأ معروفاً من الذهاب، فالباء للتعدية. بتبنة : بكسر التاء المثناة الفوقية وسكون الباء الموحدة، وفتح النون، يقال له بالفارسية : كياه ؛ أي الحشيش. أو ورق : بفتحتين، يطلق على ورق الشجر ، ويقال له بالفارسية: برك، وورق وأورد عليه بأنه غير نافع لصدقه على السفينة والجمل ؛ فإنهما يذهبان بتبن كثير من موضع إلى موضع. وأجاب عنه صاحب «البحر» بأنّ ما ليست موصولة، بل نكرة موصوفة ، والمعنى: الجاري : ماء يذهب بتبنة. وبمثله يجاب عمّا أورد على عبارة «الكافية»: «الكلام ما تضمن كلمتين بالإسناد» (۱).
In it there is impurity whose trace is not seen: meaning its taste, or its colour, or its smell. They differed regarding the definition of flowing (water). [1] His saying: 'in it there is impurity' with a fatha on the jim, meaning impurity, and it is a second attribute of water, and its apparent meaning is that if impurity falls into flowing water and its trace is not seen, it is permissible to perform wudu with it, whether the impurity is a carcass or otherwise. So if a person urinates in it and another performs wudu from its lower part, it is permissible as long as its trace does not appear in the flow. This is as in 'al-Bahr'. [2] His saying: 'its trace is not seen' is an attribute of impurity: meaning that the trace of that impurity is not known in that water. By 'seeing' is meant knowledge, and by this is repelled what is said: that seeing is only connected to colour, not taste and smell. [3] His saying: 'they differed' means the jurists differed regarding the definition of flowing water and what constitutes its flowing. It is said: it is that which removes the impurity before the second scoop. And it is said: it is such that if a person places his hand across it, it does not stop. And it is said: it is what custom considers flowing. This is mentioned in 'al-Tatarkhaniyya', and the last opinion is verified in 'Ghayat al-Bayan', 'al-Binaya', 'al-Bada'i', 'al-Bahr', and others. So the definition in whose comprehension there is no difficulty is 'what goes with a straw or leaf.' So if a river is dammed from above and the rest of the water flows weakly, wudu is permissible with it, for it is flowing water. [1] His saying: 'difficulty' – an objection is raised that all definitions are equal in that there is no difficulty in comprehending them, for there is no difficulty in comprehending any of the concepts. The answer is that what is meant is that there is no difficulty in comprehending the flowing water itself. It is replied that the absence of difficulty in comprehending the definition entails the absence of difficulty in comprehending the defined. What occurred to me during writing 'al-Si'aya' is that his intent is that this definition has no difficulty in determining its referent and the truth of this concept upon its particular instances, unlike the remaining definitions. [2] His saying: 'what goes' – from 'al-ithhab' (taking away), the ba' entering upon 'al-tibna' is extra, and it can be read as known from 'al-dhahab' (going), so the ba' is for transitivity. 'Batinatin' (with a straw): with a kasra on the ta' and sukun on the ba' and fatha on the nun, it is called in Persian 'kiyah' meaning grass. 'Or waraq' (leaf): with two fatha, it is applied to tree leaf, and in Persian 'barg' and 'waraq'. An objection is raised that it is not beneficial because it applies to a ship and a camel, for they carry away much straw from place to place. The author of 'al-Bahr' answered that 'ma' is not a relative pronoun but an indefinite described noun, so the meaning is: flowing (water) is water that goes with a straw. Similarly, an answer is given to what is objected upon the expression of 'al-Kafiya': 'Speech is what comprises two words with attribution.' (1)
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the ruling regarding flowing water (al-ma' al-jari) when impurity falls into it. The base text states that if impurity falls into flowing water and its trace (taste, colour, or smell) is not visible, it is permissible to perform wudu with that water. The commentator clarifies that 'trace not seen' means not known, whether by sight or other senses. The key issue here is the definition of 'flowing water' (al-jari). The commentator presents three opinions from the jurists: (1) water that removes the impurity before the second scoop; (2) water that does not stop when a hand is placed across it; (3) water that custom ('urf) considers flowing. The third opinion is certified in major Hanafi works like al-Bada'i' and al-Bahr. However, the commentator then states the preferred definition: 'what goes with a straw or leaf' (ma yadhabu bi-tibnatin aw waraq), meaning that if you drop a straw or leaf into the water from one end, the flow carries it away. This definition avoids the difficulty of determining the exact flow. The commentator addresses objections: first, that all definitions are equally easy to understand; the answer is that this definition is easy to apply to particular cases. Second, that the word 'ma' could be misinterpreted as a relative pronoun leading to objections like a ship or camel carrying straw; but the correct grammatical analysis is that 'ma' is an indefinite noun described by 'yadhabu', so the meaning is 'a water that goes with a straw', not 'that which goes'. This is a typical Hanafi commentary style using grammatical precision to defend the definition. The ruling remains: flowing water, defined by this criterion, is pure and permissible for wudu as long as no trace of impurity appears.

Key Terms
الجاري (al-jari): Flowing water; defined in this text as water that carries away a straw or leaf, or water that custom considers flowing.
نجس (najis): Impurity; here it refers to any impure substance that falls into water, including carcasses or urine.
أثر (athar): Trace of impurity; includes taste, colour, or smell that would indicate the impurity is present.
تبنة (tibnah): A straw; used as a measure in the definition of flowing water — if the water can carry a straw, it is considered flowing.
ورق (waraq): Leaf; similar to straw, used as an alternative measure in the definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the preferred definition in this passage, what is the criterion for water to be considered flowing (jari)?
A) It removes impurity before the second scoop.
B) It does not stop when a hand is placed across it.
C) It carries away a straw or leaf.
D) It has a visible current.

Key Takeaway
Flowing water is defined in the Hanafi school as water that carries away a straw or leaf, and wudu with it is valid even if impurity falls in, as long as no trace (taste, colour, smell) appears.

Defining Flowing Water: The Straw Test
Defining flowing water with the straw test and defending it against objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi definition of flowing water and explain why the counterexample of a ship carrying straw does not invalidate it.

Core Idea
Flowing water is defined by its ability to carry a light object like a straw — a definition that withstands scrutiny when correctly parsed.

The early Hanafi masters defined flowing water with a simple practical test: water that can carry away a straw or leaf. This definition is not arbitrary; it embodies the necessary attribute of current. However, critics have raised objections, such as that ships and camels also carry straw. In this lesson, we examine the definition and its defense, uncovering a subtle grammatical point that safeguards its precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition
State the Hanafi definition of flowing water as given in the text: water that carries away a straw or leaf.
The objects are chosen for lightness, indicating a minimal current.
2
Understand the practical purpose
Recognize that this definition distinguishes flowing water from standing water, affecting rulings on purification.
Standing water may become impure; flowing water is less likely to be affected.
3
Examine the objection
Consider the criticism: a ship or camel can also carry away straw, so the definition seems too broad.
This counterexample is based on a literal reading of 'ma' as 'that which'.
4
Analyze the grammatical answer
Understand that 'ma' in the definition is an indefinite noun (naktira mawsufa), not a relative pronoun. Thus the phrase means 'water that carries away a straw', not 'anything that carries away a straw'.
This parsing limits the definition to water, excluding ships and camels.
5
Apply the lesson
Test the definition with other potential counterexamples (e.g., wind carrying a leaf over water) to ensure it correctly identifies flowing water.
Only water's own movement counts, not external forces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[٢] قوله : ما يذهب من الإذهاب، فالباء الداخلة على «التبنة» زائدة، ويمكن أن يقرأ معروفاً من الذهاب، فالباء للتعدية. بتبنة : بكسر التاء المثناة الفوقية وسكون الباء الموحدة، وفتح النون، يقال له بالفارسية : كياه ؛ أي الحشيش. الكتاب. أو ورق : بفتحتين، يطلق على ورق الشجر ، ويقال له بالفارسية: برك، وورق وأورد عليه بأنه غير نافع لصدقه على السفينة والجمل ؛ فإنهما يذهبان بتبن كثير من موضع إلى موضع. وأجاب عنه صاحب «البحر» بأنّ ما ليست موصولة، بل نكرة موصوفة ، والمعنى: الجاري : ماء يذهب بتبنة. وبمثله يجاب عمّا أورد على عبارة «الكافية»: «الكلام ما تضمن كلمتين بالإسناد» (۱). (۱) انتهى من «البحر الرائق (۱) : (۸۸) ، وعبارته : قد توهم بعض المشتغلين أن هذا الحد فاسد ؛ لأنه يرد عليه الجمل والسفينة، فإنهما يذهبان بتبن كثير ومنشأ التوهم أن ما موصولة في كلامه وقد وقع مثلها في عبارة ابن الحاجب، فإنه قال : الكلام ما يتضمن كلمتين بالإسناد، فقيل يرد عليه الورقة والحجر المكتوب عليه كلمتان فأكثر ؛ لأن ما موصولة بمعنى الذي لكن الجواب عنهما أن ما ليست موصولة، وإنما هي نكرة موصوفة، فالمعنى الجاري ماء بالمد يذهب بتبنة والكلام لفظ يتضمن كلمتين.
The definition which has no difficulty in comprehending it is 'what carries away a straw or leaf.' So if a river is blocked from above, and the remaining water flows weakly, it is permissible to perform ablution with it, because it is flowing water. And similarly it is answered to what was objected to the expression of 'al-Kafiyah': 'Speech is what includes two words with attribution.' (1) End from 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq' (1:88), and his expression: Some students mistakenly thought that this definition is flawed because a camel and a ship are counterexamples, for they carry away much straw. And the source of the mistake is that 'ma' is a relative pronoun in his speech, and a similar case occurred in Ibn al-Hajib's expression, for he said: 'Speech is what includes two words with attribution.' So it was said that a leaf and a stone with two words written on them are counterexamples; because 'ma' is a relative pronoun meaning 'that which.' But the answer for both is that 'ma' is not a relative pronoun, but rather an indefinite noun described. So the meaning is: the flowing is water that carries away a straw, and speech is an utterance that includes two words.
Source pages p. 367

Commentary
This passage defines flowing water (al-ma' al-jari) in the Hanafi school. The author states the definition given by the early scholars: 'water that carries away a straw or leaf.' This is a practical definition meant to distinguish flowing water from standing water, which affects rulings such as purification. The commentary then defends this definition against objections. The first objection is that all definitions are equally easy to grasp; the answer clarifies that the intended meaning is that the specific definition of flowing water is not burdensome. A second objection points out that ships and camels also carry away straw; the rebuttal, from 'al-Bahr al-Ra'iq', explains that the word 'ma' in the definition is not a relative pronoun but an indefinite noun, so the definition means 'water that carries away a straw'—a feature unique to flowing water, not ships or camels. The comparison to the definition of speech in Arabic grammar (al-kalam) further illustrates this grammatical point. The overall aim is to preserve the definition's precision against apparent counterexamples.

Key Terms
تبنة (tabnah): A straw or piece of grass; used in the definition of flowing water as something light that water carries away.
ورق (waraq): A leaf; also used in the definition as another light object.
جاري (jari): Flowing water; water that has current, defined by its ability to carry a light object.
ما (نكرة موصوفة) (ma (naktira mawsufa)): The word 'ma' used as an indefinite noun (not a relative pronoun) meaning 'something that' or 'water that' in this context.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is the definition of flowing water as 'water that carries away a straw' not invalidated by the example of a ship carrying straw?
A) Because ships are not water
B) Because 'ma' in the definition is a relative pronoun, excluding ships
C) Because 'ma' is an indefinite noun, so it means 'water that carries away a straw', which only applies to water
D) Because ships are not mentioned in the text

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi definition of flowing water is precise and defended by grammatical analysis against superficial counterexamples.
